MAETERLINCK
THE RED PAGE
IT is questionable whether the world has
lost or gained by the transformation of
the Maeterlinck of the symbolic drama
to the Maeterlinck of modern philosophy.
Would we willingly give up The Life of the
Bee for Pelleas and Melisande ? Probably if
Maeterlinck could have continued his
dramatic work, perfecting and refining
its strange beauties, we would have been
well content without Wisdom and Destiny ;
but the frail, naive beauty of his earlier
drama was but spoiled and coarsened when
he added to it strength. Monna Vanna is a
fine play, richly embroidered with romance,
reverberant with the clash of arms, suffused
with human emotion, deliberately dramatic;
but its appeal is cruder, its grace lost be-
neath the riot of passion. Maeterlinck
brought his characters out of the twilight
into the day ; it was no longer they who
were the passive instruments of fate ; he
flung destiny into the background and made
his people articulate,. self-sufficient. The
result was a piece of the theatre—and he
had shown us a visionary beauty. Maeter-
linck had grown up. He deliberately closed
the door of fantasy and adventured into
medieval romance. He shocked us into
applause, but the ghostly thrill had gone.
Then he woke and saw the world with the
vision of a man. And his eyes, once accus-
tomed to the glare, saw everywhere curious
and stimulating things. Everywhere life, in
its least articulate, least pretentious manifes-
tations charmed and intrigued him; he
became the philosopher of modernity. And
in his essays the old charm survived ; but
now, instead of being half in love with
easeful death, he became enamored with life.
He wrote of the bee, of Shakespeare, of the
fist, of the perfume of flowers, of the motor
car. He had passed from the shadows of
his forest of fantasy into the crisp, crude,
palpitating world of science. And in this
later phase of his there is still the naive
outlook of the wondering child.
jt
His latest volume of essays, Life and
Flowers (Gr. Allen, per Sydney Book Club),
has the stimulating and suggestive charm of
his earlier work. His criticism of life is
always incisive, his analogies and dreams
always logically based. And he is not with-
out the saving-grace of humor.
Let us not sav to ourselves that the best
truth always lies in moderation, in the decent
average. The average, the decent moderation of
to-day will be the least human of things to-
moirow. At the time of the Spanish Inquisition,
the opinion of good sense and of the just medium
was certainly that people ought not to burn too
large a number of heretics; extreme and unreason-
able opinion obviously demanded that they should
burn none at all. It is the same to-day with the
question of marriage, of love, of religion, of crimi-
nal justice. At the present moment, the most
reasonable opinion on the subject of the social
question invites us to do all that we can gradually
to diminish inevitable inequalities, and distribute
happiness more equitably. Extreme opinion de-
mands instantly integral division, the suppression
of property; obligatory labor, and the rest. We
do not yet know how these demands will be
realised ; but it is already quite certain that very
simple circumstances will one day make them
appear as natural as the suppression of the right
nobd?ty 0f?emtUre ° r ° f the privile S es of the
His standpoint toward nature is that of
the modern scientist. The belief in the
infallibility of nature has gone into the limbo
• * s ,VP® r i st . itions alon g with a host of other
infallibilities. Maeterlinck sees Nature’s
mistakes, and suggests their cause. He
notes that Nature, in her most constant and
familiar manifestations, very rarely acts
according to human good sense.
W bat could be more senseless than her waste
of existences? What more unreasonable than
those billions of germs blindly squandered to
achieve the chance birth of a single being ? What
more illogical than the useless complication of
her means (as, for instance, in the life of certain
parasites and the impregnation of flowers bv
insects) to attain the simplest ends ? All this
goes beyond our good sense, and shows it that it is
not in agreement with general life, and that it is
almost isolated in the universe.
j*
Yet he finds that, after all, Nature is no
more stupid than Man. * '
Hie genius of the earth, which is probably that
of the whole world, acts, in the vital struggle
exactly as a man would act. It employs the same
methods, the same logic. It attains its aim by
the same that we would use ; it gropes it
hesitates, it corrects itself time after time * it
adds, it suppresses, it recognises and repairs its
errors, as we should do in its place. It makes
great efforts, it invents with difficulty and little
by little, after the manner of the workmen and
engineers in our workshops. It fights like our-
selves against the heavy, huge and obscure mass
of its being. It knows no more than we do
whither it is going; it seeks and finds itself
gradually. It has an ideal that is often confused,
but one in which, nevertheless, we distinguish a
host of great lines that rise towards a more
ardent, complex, nervous and spiritual form of
existence. ... Does this not mean that the
methods of the human mind are the only possible
methods, that man has not erred, that he is
neither an exception nor a monster, but the being
hrough whom pass, in whom are most intensely
manifested the great volitions, the great desires
of the universe?... It would not, I imagine,
be very bold to maintain that there are
not any more or less intelligent beings,
but a scattered general intelligence, a sort
of universal fluid that penetrates diversely the
organisms which it encounters according as they
are good or bad condubtors of the understanding.
Man would then represent, until now, upon this
earth the mode of life that offered the least resist-
ance to this fluid, which the religious called
divine. Our nerves would be the threads along
which this more subtle electricity would spread.
Ihe circumvolutions of our brain would, in a
manner, form the induction-coil in which the
force of the current wotild be multiplied : but this
current would be of no other would pro-
ceed from no other source than that which passes
through the stone, the star, the flower or the
animal.
In his detailed treatment of this theme, in
that interesting essay “The Intelligence of
the Flowers, Maeterlinck calls our attention
to some remarkable instances of this uni-
versal intelligence working through the
flowers. He shows us that the vegetable
world, which to us seems so placid, so
resigned, is one in which impatience and the
revolt against destiny are the most vehement
and stubborn. The plant lies under the
heavy ban of immobility from birth to death.
Against this law it strives with a remarkable
persistence.
It exerts itself wholly with one sole aim : to
escape above from the fatality below, to set itself
free, to invent or invoke wings, to escape as far as
as it can, to conquer the space in which destiny
encloses it, to approach another kingdom, to
Penetrate into a moving and active world. . . .
We shall see. that the flower sets man a prodigious
example of insubmission, courage, perseverance,
and ingenuity.
So, with wonderful instances of the in-
genuity of the flowers, Maeterlinck fills in
his suggestion of the kinship between man
and the dumb intelligences of the lower
kingdoms. Ideas seem almost to come to
the flowers in the same way that they come
to us. They grope in the same darkness,
encounter the same obstacles ; they have the
same disillusions, the same triumphs; they
seem to possess our patience, our perseve-
rance, our self-love ; thdy struggle, like our-
selves, against a great indifferent force that
ends by assisting them. Their inventive
imagination not only follows the same pru-
dent and minute methods, the same tiriuw,
narrow, and winding paths ; it also has un-
expected leaps and bounds that suddenly
fix definitely an uncertain discovery. Natu-
rally enough, Maeterlinck fixes upon the
orchids as the most remarkable inventors
of the vegetable race ; and his whole elabo-
rate essay is a plea that the flower possesses
the power, or the intelligence, to adapt
itself to changing circumstances, which we
call evolution. Incidentally he mentions the
rational behavior of the thrifty black bee
when transported to Australia.
After one or two years, finding that summer is
perpetual and flowers for ever abundant, she will
live from day to day, content to gather the honey
and pollen indispensable for the day’s consump-
tion ; and her recent and thoughtful observation
triumphing over hereditary experience, she will
cease to make provision for her winter
J*
Maeterlinck points out that it may be
necessary to experiment with flowers for
many years before we can prove their adapta-
tion to changed circumstances—such as sub-
stituting one class of inseet for another and
seeing what changes the flowers would make
in the structure of tfteir fertilising machinery
visited by the new-comers. Yet his view
that flowers do show the intelligence neces-
sary to meet unforseen circumstances is
obviously more than probable. For instance,
the flowers came upon this earth before the
arrival of insects. The appearance of the
insects was seized upon by the flowers in a
way that undoubtedly suggests a keen prac-
ticality and an invention and courage suffi-
ciently remarkable. But, on the larger
question, as the evolution of man and of
atoms is now established, it is certain
that the orders m between follow the same
course. We may figure Nature as a rather
stupid but very persistent chemist, always
almost haphazardly experimenting, and—like
the Federal Labor Party—intent only on the
immediate concerns of her creatures, and
with a hazy idea of whither her efforts will
lead them. Atom, or algae, or crystal, plant,
or bee, or man, Nature is for ever restlessly
at work through all, reaching out into a
higher complexity of life, a greater chance
of greatly living.
Arthur. Streeton leaves for England to-dav
wel provisioned for his next attack upon the art-
world of London. Australia has been appreciative
of the work of her returned son ; he must have
I works aggregating three thousand
pounds in value. Ihere is a moral here for our
expatriate Australian artists. Streeton’s last
show in Melbourne put £350 into his pocket—for
value received.
THE SYMBOLIST.
I sometimes think the rose is red,
I sometimes think it's green ;
I sometimes think the cauliflower
Is the finest thing I’ve seen.
I sometimes think the world is round,
I sometimes think it’s flat ;
I sometimes think the symbolist
Is talking through his hat !
W. A. OSBORNE
in “ The Laboratory and
Other Poems.”
COLLOLDS AND GATALYSTS
f INHERE is certainly something to be
said for W. A. Osborne, author
of The Laboratory and Other
Poems, charmingly printed by T.
0. Lothian, Melb. He has the gift of
sonorous phrase, and sometimes he has a
thought behind the phrase, and he is com-
mendably shy of roses and dawns and gloam-
ings. In his striving to be modern he occa-
sionally becomes but a parody of Bernard
O Dowd ; but as Bernard O’Dowd sometimes
parodies himself Osborne may be forgiven.
Evidently O’Dowd is founding a school, and,
so far, Osborne is his prize pupil. Still,
even O’Dowdism is better than. Tennysonism,
for O’Dowd acridly stings, while Tennyson
merely cloys. The opening verses of The
Laboratory admirably shows Osborne’s
method :
We live amid the buzzing flames
That from our bunsens fly,
The measured tube, the seamless bulb,
And rippling mercury.
The prisoned rainbow waits on us,
The trapped aurora glows,
And from the Ruhmkorff terminals
The vibrant lightning flows.
This is our furnished factory,
There are the gleaming tools
With which we fashion or unlock
The dancing molecules;
By them we rivet complexes
By Nature never seen ;
By them we cut entanglements
Of cumbrous protein.
*Tis here we scrawl the formulae
That ope the stores of earth,
Or cryptic hexagons that bring
A commerce into birth.
And here within the hissing tubes
Our tiny search-light’s source,
Which yet may outline and reveal
The mystery of force.
And so amid no haughty pomp,
No gaudy flag unfurled,
Our fingers wander lovingly
On switchboards of the world.
But, in his endeavor to be crisp, Osborne
frequently becomes forced. His similes
become far-fetched, and they are often
commonplace when fetched. As examples:
The solar breeze blows fresh astern ;
Our strenuous youths and maids
Go gladly up and square the yards
To catch the ether trades. . . .
To discipline bacteria,
To guide the comet’s flight,
To hammer out the molecule,
Or blast a satellite. . . .
Each works but for the common weal,
Each other each assists.
When on the colloids drifting by
They loose the catalysts.
It may be all right for the catalysts, but it
seems hard luck for the colloids. And,
despite Osborne’s aptitude for the sonorous
phrase he hardly knows yet how to manage
his words. Thus, in a sonnet upon “An
Old Map of Assyria,” he spoils his sestette
by a “ rocky ” ending :
Ah they are dead, .their glory passed away ;
Yet, oft I hear in unforgetful sleep,
The din of multitudinous chariots drawn
Through surging streets of lordly Nineveh,
And mingled myriads, voiceful in the deep
Roar of irrevocable Babylon.
Osborne is promising. . . ,

“N.L.”: In the article on “English Prosody’
(“Red Page,” 19/9/’O7), mention of the metrica
irregularities in Christina Rossetti’s verse recalls
the well-meaning Ruskin’s effort, fortunately un
successful, to induce her to use “ standard ” verse
methods. In 1861 Dante Rossetti left his sister’s
manuscript poems with Ruskin, whose comment
forms portion of a letter published in Buskin-
Bossctti-Preraphnelitism (George Allen, 1899) :
I sate up till late last night reading poems.
1 hey are full of beauty and power. But no
publisher—l am deeply grieved to know this—
would take them, so full are they of quaintnesses
and offences. Irregular measure (introduced to
my great regret, in its chief wilfulness, by Cole-
ridge) is the calamity of modern poetry. The
Iliad, the Divina Commedia, the TEneid, the
whole of Spenser, Milton, Keats aie written
without taking a single license or violating the
common ear for metre ; your sister should exercise
herself in the severest commonplace of metre
until she can write as the public like. Then if
she puts in her observation and passion all will
become precious. But she must have the Form
first.” The advice was not taken, and in the
following year many of the poems, issued in Miss
Rossetti’s first publicly-circulated volume of verse,
proved to be what a large percentage of the public
dtd like, Possibly the judgment of present-day
restnctionists on the point of popularity will
prove to be as defective as Ruskin’s.
The recogni tion that—with the modern methods
ot photography which afford the photographer
ample opportunity to express his individuality—
photography is an art has called forth a number of
books upon the work of the camera. Of these,
one or the most stimulating is Art and the Camera,
by Anthony G-uest (George Bell). The work is
written for the amateur—the professional is
usually hopelessly beyond the pale of art: but
there is no technical mention of processes; the
critic is solely concerned with the artistic effect
and the universal principles underlying its attain-
ment. Guest has no. hesitation in pronouncing
photography an art. Artistic photography he
dehnes as “the correction of the mistakes of the
camera, and the vivifying of its productions by
human emotion.... When photography is solely
the work of the sun and the camera, finished off
by the operation of certain chemical laws, it car
by no possibility be an art. When it is the pro-
duction of the same agents modified by unin-
spired manipulation, it is still outside the pale.
But when the individual is in control of the
operation throughout, insisting on the attainment
of his ideal, and preventing any appearances that
he does not want, then, be the medium what it
may, it holds the possibility of producing a work
of art. ... The ‘ pure photography ’ standpoint
is open to objection, for the camera’s work needs
milch correction before it can claim to be artistic,
and whether that correction be applied through
the technical resources peculiar to photography
seems to matter very little. On the whole, pro-
bably, the hand, as the most immediate instru-
ment of the br.ain, offers the surest means of
carrying out its behests. A photograph
should be a photograph first of v all, and its
modification or beautification by other means
should be regarded as subsidiary. The true
theory is that the process to which a material is
subjected should beautify it, not obliterate its
character.” Art and the Camera is written from
this sane standpoint, aiid the numerous illustra-
tion of artistic work, though necessarily losing
much from reproduction upon smooth surfaces,
are highly artistic in quality. It is a pity, how-
ever, that Guest has not included in his survey
some examples from those European workers whose
work is so outstanding among the artists of the
camera.
0. 0. Oabot sends this stanza of Henry Parkes,
deceased, from the inexpressible Douglas Brooke
Wheelton Sladen’s unspeakable Australian “An-
thology,” as possessing a present fitness :
Oh, for some awakening cause
Where we face eternal laws,
Where we dare not turn aside,
Where the souls of men are tried—
Something of a nobler strife,
Which consumes the dross of life,
To unite to truer aim,
To exalt to loftier fame.
Leave behind the bats and balls,
Leave the racers in the stalls,
Leave the cards for ever shuffled,
Leave the yachts on seas unruffled,
Leave the haunts of pampered ease,
Leave your dull festivities ;
Better far the savage glen—
Fitter school for earnest men.
Contributors to the T. D. Mutch-John P. con-
troversy will note that we’ve had T.D.P. Mutch
of it.
OUR SAUSAGE SERIAL
This serial must stop. It is wrecking the
Federal Constitution. From all over the
Commonwealth reports are coming in of girls
named Alice running amok. The life of a red-
whiskered constable or a Customs officer or a pine-
apple is not for one moment safe, earthquakes and
explosions are endemic, and Carruthers (not the
dog) has resigned. The only exciting thing
that so far hasn’t happened is a chasm, and
when even the missing will has turned up a
chasm may be expected at any moment. So, in
the interests of lawnorder, this serial must con-
clude. The final chapter, already written, will
appear next week. The author of this week’s
chapter is N. Edmond, Mount Royal, Strathfield.
The Trend That Didn’t.
(By the “ Red Page," Hunt Coleman, J. M.
Leape, H. Paton, “ Anon,’’ R. C. Batchelor ,
H. S. Mitchell, and N. Edmond.)
Chapter vm.—An Assortment.
Alice and Charles, who were now growing old,
were seated with their hands round each other’s
neck, discussing the non-appearance of the missing
will. ,Tust then a violent explosion shook the
place, and something crashed down the chimney.
It was thfe missing will.
J ust as Charles was about to peruse it, Alice
uttered a blood-curdling shriek, and pointed a
shaking finger at the window.
Horrors ! It was the face at the window.
“Goodness!” cried Alice, “it must be the
missing child.”
It was the missing child.
It entered, staggering under the weight of three
huge volumes of Chaucer.
“Ha!” yelled Alice, “there are the forged
marriage lines.”
Charles turned pale, but soon turned back
, again.
“What’s the signature?” he demanded in a
shaky voice.
“They are marked with the sign of the faded
flannel-flower.”
“ The game’s up !” cried he, throwing off his
mask.
There stood John Blaclcadder, with nine pistols
in each hand, and two in his teeth. Just then a
roll of wire-netting, covered with gore, hurtled
through the window.
“ Too late ! ” cried a Customs officer, as he took
John in charge.
Alice was saved.
(To be concluded.)

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Laughter.
To-day young Genius drank his wine
In a shady glen with a host divine.
And, chucking the chin of his favorite Muse,
Mirthfully told them thus the news :
“Ho ! ho! I laugh with a joyous laugh
At those who have written my epitaph,
“ And, under the pall of a century’s lays,
Have buried me deep in the olden days.
“ There’s Dead-in-Earnest, the critic man—
The eminent ‘ I ’ of the graveyard clan—
“ Last night he mourned through a vigil long,
So I sought him out in a myall song—
-44 A simple thing from the heart of Time
A laughing riot in lilting rhyme ;
“ ‘Um ! ah ! ’ he said, as he faced me there.
' Ahem ! er ! yes ! Now, that is fair.
“ * One almost feels that the verses sing;
And yet—it is only a lightsome thing.
“ ‘And yet—and yet’ With a bound I sought
His inner self as a happy thought.
“ And he saw me throned in a thousand lays
He had damned before in his graveyard craze.
“ And he wrote— 4 ’Tis true in the world’s wide
range
That nothing dies, but that all things change ;
“ 4 And swords of famine, and gouts of blood,
And emotions running at fever flood
44 4 Shall change and change till the bloody past
Evolve in the Laughter-Life at last. ’
“ But lo 1 I laugh with a joyous Lugh
At those who have written my epitaph,
44 And, under the pall of a century’s lays,
Have buried me deep in the olden days;
44 For Dead-in-Earnest, this morn, arose
And wrote afresh in his graveyard prose.”
And the Lost laughed : 44 Ha! in the world’s wide
range
All things, all things but the critics change.”
VIC.
E. S. EMERSON.
Godly Folks!
What ails the godly folks around ? They sneer as
passers-by,
And oft methinks I see them glint the corner of
an eye;
Maybe’tis just because a year agone—my heart
was light—
I loved a goodly lover—yes ! I loved too well one
night!
I felt so lonely from thenceforth, for conscience
bade him flee;
Love’s captive, chained to stand the gibes, I stayed
while he was free.
Then came the travail and the tears—and joy
when baby cried ;
Then sorrow knocked and broke my heart, the
dawn my darling died !
What ails the godly folks around ? They tilt their
noses high,
And pass this bit of bitter flesh with heavy groan
and sigh ;
They quite forget how Mother Eve in ages past
would taste,
And put the ban upon the crowd through her un-
seemly haste !
I would not blame the godly crew if they would
but afford
To hurl a stone, or brush aside the teachings of
their Lord ;
Since God is Love, and Love’s not sin, yet loving
brings us pain,
Methinks the godly, when we fall, might help us
up again !
VIC.
RITA SUNYASEE.
On the Cliffs.
I knelt upon the cliffs last night— ten thousand
drifting stars
Kept vigil and the vestal light knelt down and
kiss’d the bars;
The night wind came and knelt with me; in
yawning depths beneath
The dark tongue of a mighty sea showed grim
between its teeth.
I knelt upon the cliffs last night, and yearned for
endless rest;
I longed to toss on breakers white, to sleep on
Neptune’s breast.
Alone, I thought, with watching trees, while
planets blazed above,
I longed within my soul to claim the sea’s eternal
love.
But as I knelt forlorn, afraid, a figure glided by
That tarried not, but leapt out wide with harsh,
exultant cry. ...
The night wind whimpered in the crags, and
whined among the trees ;
Old Ocean’s organs pealed their deep and un-
changed harmonies.
I left the lonely cliffs last night with coward
heart, afraid,
For far below on beaches white a mangled corpse
was laid. . . .
The sea gave neither love nor rest; with garments
trailed and torn
That corpse bore on its pulseless breast the spume
of Ocean’s scorn.
N.S.W.
GEORGE F. BRIGGS.

THE LATEST FOREIGN-TRADE FROST.

in favor of the importing policy. His observations were received without warmth.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice. •
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the. contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, ivhether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, SOOO words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is fonvarded without stamps
for return it ■will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications-for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —ivill be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
a/pplying to MS.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 17, 1907.
THE SWEATED JOBS OF ENGLAND.
A few months ago the London Daily Mail got
up an exhibition of Britain’s sweated industries —
the chronically-sweated ones which live, as a
regular thing, on the flesh and bones of the
people—and sent the horror travelling around the
country. The authenticity of the monster was
sworn to by the Mail and by many other
authorities, and it was never disputed in any
detail. A duplicate exhibition was sent out to
Maoriland, and served as a side issue of the recent
big show at Christchurch. It consisted simply of
a collection of articles, made or partly made, with
an accusing statement alongside each, setting
forth the wages which the worker receives, and
the number of hours per day or per week that it is
necessary for that worker to labor to earn a plank
and a crust. The catalogue is a huge one—it seems
as if the sweated industry was the rule rather
than the exception in Freetrade Britain.
* * * *
It is amazing and incredible to consider what
an alleged human being can live upon in
Bull’s country. Not that England is a cheap
country ; to live there as an ordinary Australian
workman and his family live would cost more in’
many cases than that Australian workman could
earn. The price of fresh meat alone would be
liable to knock him out in one round. But the
art of crowding many people into one room and
making it serve for all purposes; of dis-
pensing with meat, fish, butter, cheese, jam,
fresh air, amusement, exercise and many
other things; and of keeping a scanty
wardrobe together more by accident and the
intervention of Providence than by any recog-
nised means —these things have become an
art in the land of Cobden. Life in many
trades is a matter of four main propositions:
bread, weak tea, 16 hours work, and eight hours
sleep.' So the mystery of that crude kind of cheap-
ness which comes of low-priced labor is explained.
In the making of match-boxes, for instance, the
English working day is mentioned as 16 hours, and
the earnings of an expert worker are 9s. per
week, out of which he has to find paste and string.
At carding hooks and eyes the working day
is 14 hours, and the average earnings are 3s. 3d.
a week. The worker finds cotton and needles,
which take 4d. out of the wretched pittance,
so that the wild affluence at the end of the
week really boils down to 2s. lid. Carding
buttons runs to 3s. 6d. a week (15 hours per day),
chough pearl buttons bring in higher remunera-
tion ; sometimes the giddy altitude of 7s. a
week is attained. The worker finds needles and
thread. At making helmet chains for the head-
gear of soldiers, firemen, and policemen 10s. a
week can be made by working eighteen hours a
day, from 5 a.m. to 11 p.m. (It seems that each
branch of sweating has its customs, built up during
a long course of hereditary misery, so that there
are recognised 14- and 15- and 16-hour trades, and
soon.) Paper-bag making, which is a machine
industry here, and pays young women from 13s. 6d.
to £1 a week for perhaps 45 hours, is largely a hand
industry in Freetrade England, just as the making
of the dilly-bag is among our aborigines, and 12
hours daily work returns 4s. 6d. a week, less cost
of paste and alum. Artificial flower making
yields 7s. to 10s. a week, less about 2s. 3d. for
sundries, for 10J hours daily work, but it takes
five years to thoroughly learn the trade. Chain-
making pays the woman-toiler about 7s. per week
for 12 hours daily toil. From 2s. Bd. to 6s. per
week can be made “capping” and carding
safety-pins. Curiously enough, while industries
that yield as little as 3s. 6d. a week live
and flourish in Freetrade England, nailmaking
is dying out for want of workers; the cata-
logue says briefly that “ parents refuse to
bring up their children to so wretched a prospect.”
Considering the horror of the other prospects this
prospect must be something incredible.
* * * *
Still the catalogue goes on its interminable way.
Sacksvieldfis. to 6s. 3d. perweek, less cost of needles,
etc., and the woman has either to carry the sacks
(in 561 b. bundles) from the factory and back, or
pay 2d. porterage. The making of pom-poms,
whatever they may be, returns ss. 6d. a week.
The folding of prayer-books and of the holy and
blessed Scripture returns as much as 9s. fid. a
week, for 16 hours daily labor, and the callous,
dead, withered, dried-up church doesn’t care.
Colored tassels yield 7d. per gross ; and furniture
gimp lid. per dozen yards. The umbrella business
runs as high as 10s. per week, for 10 hours per
day ; and ladies’ shoes go as far as 6s. per week.
There are 188 sweated trades on the list, and so far
this enumeration has only got as far as No. 20. It is
only possible to quote a few samples at random out
of the other 168. Buttons return 6s. 9d. per week;
toothbrushes, Bs. or 9s. per week (12 hours per
day); boys’ knickerbockers, l|d. each (equal to
about 9s. per week, the worker paying for her
own thread and sewing machine out of this);
sailor suits, 13s. 6d. per week for 15 hours work
per day, worker finding machine and thread ; also
children’s boots yield 7s. fid. to Bs. 6d. net per
week for something like 16 hours daily toil. The
price has been reduced one-half within the last
few years. (In Maoriland this work is paid at
about Is. per hour.) Then in Freetrade England
waistcoats are made for Id. each, the worker
finding the thread. Men’s trousers yield as
much as 7s. 6d. per week for 16 hours
daily struggle (the Maoriland rate for women
in this industry is 255. a week working
hours per day.) Shirts return about 9s. 6d. per
week for from 12 to 17 hours work per diem.
Coats are a gorgeous item, and pay as much a.s
14s. per week to an expert. Blouses pay a divi-
dend of about -Bs. a week net to a clever worker
with her own machine. Nightdresses pan out for
9s. 6d. per week ; bed-spreads, Bs. 3d. ; children’s
frocks, Bs.; ladies’ belts, ss. per week, assuming
the hours to be 16 per day ; skirts, about 10s. per
week (15 hours per day); underclothing, about 7s.
per week. But the catalogue grows too monoto-
nous, and further details are dropped. It is a
very long catalogue, and according to Australian
ideas it should have been much longer. For
instance, the skilled carpenter, though his wages
in some parts get down below sd. an hour, doesn’t
rank in the United Kingdom as the representative
of a sweated industry. He is simply an ordinary
case.
* * * #
If any further argument were needed in favor
of High Protection, the fixing of a decent standard
of wages in all trades by Wages Boards or Arbi-
tration Courts, the breaking up of great estates,
and the exclusion of cheap undesirables, the Ex-
hibition of Britain’s sweated industries is that
argument. It isn’t as if the evil were a small one;
in one way and another sweated industries keep a
good twelve millions of the British people on the
very brink of starvation. Britain is reckoned to
have more wealth per head than any other country
on earth, but so long as Freetrade lasts it is hard
to see how that wealth is going to do any good to
the sweated industries. Rather it tends to
aggravate their case. In a rich country living
is usually expensive, and it is distinctly
expensive in England. To take the single item
of rent, in one of the miserable rabbit warrens of
London —a tenement which holds in itself the
population of a village—a single room about the
dimensions of a boot-trunk costs something like
£lO a year. Yet under Freetrade the occupant
has to make things just as cheaply as any foreigner
who can get equally good accommodation for £3 ;
and there are cheaper foreigners even than that.
And if the wages were compulsorily raised, the only
result, while Freetrade lasted, would be that all
the work which could be sent abroad would go
abroad. The mild Hindu, living in a village
shack that costs perhaps £1 a year, in a climate that
makes for great economy of firing, with cheap food
and few taxes, could tackle the slop pants, and the
slop boots, and the reach-me-down underclothing
just as well as anybody else, and the cheap ship,
manned by the cheap nigger, would bring his
produce to England for next to nothing, and the
cheap lumper would unload it for almost nothing,
and there would be no duty to pay, and the end
of the sweated London toiler would be worse than
the beginning. Yet though the sweated person
is cheap the sweated article isn’t. In a country
where the average wealth is over £3OO per head
prices are always fairly high. They are based to
a great extent on the purchasing power of the
- affluent half of the population and of the
bloated few, while as to the great starving
section, it isn’t catered for to any great
extent except in a few lines, not being
seriously expected to purchase anything beyond
bare necessaries. And among people who often
dispense with meat, butter, fish, cheese, jam,
pickles, furniture and bedding (rags and news-
papers can form a couch and the unfinished sewing
work often serves as a covering), and whose very
scanty clothing is bought second-hand or possibly
cadged off “the parish,” necessaries are very
bare indeed.
* * * *
There are still some Freetrade Laborites in
Australia who believe in imports because they
fancy them to be cheap. The cheapness is to a
great extent a delusion ; the worker is economical
enough, but the middleman and the landlord see
to it that the goods are not. But, even granting
the cheapness, it is a question whether the indi-
vidual, who is willing to profit by such economy,
shouldn’t be afraid to die, and if he hasn’t
every right to be greatly alarmed as to
what may happen to him after death. To be a
Freetrade Laborite, gladly taking advantage of a
real or imaginary twopence saved by such horrors
as those of the sweated industries of England, is
to be a sweater of the worst kind. The ultimate
employer of the wretched woman who makes
shirts from 12 to 17 hours a day at 9s. fid. per
week, is the buyer of the article —the man like
Page, M.H.R., the virulent Freetrader from
Queensland. And the fact that Page, who is
part of the cause, and the miserable nine-and-
sixpenny woman, who is part of the effect, are
very far apart doesn’t diminish the responsibility
of Page, the sweater, by one iota. He and his
kind may only sweat by proxy, but all the same
it is really they who are held up to obloquy ..
such books as “ Alton Locke,” and such poems as
“The Song of the Shirt.” And the hypocrisy
of clamoring for good wages and fair hours in the
handful of industries which it is possible to have
here under Freetrade conditions, while being a
voluntary and sordid accomplice of the horrors of
a multitude of sweated industries among people
of our own race simply because these people are
far off, is rather an aggravation of the offence
than otherwise. If Page and his kind are re-
solved to be voluntary sweaters of the worst type
it would look better to go the whole hog.
* * * *
It is a cant phrase among Freetraders that this
fair young country doesn’t want to raise squalid
manufacturing towns in its midst —-that it wants
its people to live in the open air, and to health-
fully pursue the wheat and the sheep and the
bullock. The argument is a mere shirking of
our responsibilities. If we have our manufac-
turing towns here we can see that they are neither
squalid nor unhealthy. A factory can be made
ornamental, just as a church can be. By rigid
laws regarding cleanliness, ventilation, water
supply, over-crowding, wages and hours of labor,
the life of the factory operative can be made as
healthy as that of any one else. As a case in
point, Maoriland has a larger proportion of its
people employed in manufactures than any other
State of Australasia, and a larger proportion than
is to be found in most of the countries of the
world, and yet its death rate is the lowest that
the globe can show. But if, to save ourselves the
trouble of making our manufacturing towns
healthy, we have no manufacturing towns of our
own, then we must hire the use of some one
else’s manufacturing towns, and give them our
patronage and support, and wink at their
practices. Consequently their slums are, in
a measure, our slums, and their sweating
is our sweating ; and when Page, the Labor
Freetrader from Queensland, voluntarily employs
the wretched London woman at 9s. 6d. a week
and 12 to 17 hours a day to make his shirts, it is
the same disgrace to him as if he employed the
woman at his own door under similar conditions.
The Bulletin cheerfully admits that some of its
own wearables may be made under exactly the
same conditions, but it sets forth the difference
—that it calls out, in and out of season, for
Australian manufactures, with good pay and
fair hours; that it demands a tariff that will
wholly shut out the products of sweated indus-
tries and that when that tariff arrives and
creates a demand for labor that exceeds the local
supply, it will gladly insert free a two-column
advt. next to reading matter to tell the sweated
employee of English, Irish, Welsh or Scottish
birth that there is a job waiting here at decent
wages, and reasonable hours, and under healthy
conditions. Furthermore, as that employee lacks
the necessary passage money, it is fully prepared
to pay its share of the cost of assisting him or her
to these shores. And if, in the meantime,
it is compelled, by reason of a miserably
inefficient tariff, to be one of the em-
ployers of the skeletons who sleep on
rags in unfurnished rooms, and live on dry bread
and a thin fluid made out of tea refuse, it does it
with regret, and under pressure of necessity. It
isn’t a voluntary sweater, like Page, the Free-
trader from Queensland.
• » •* *
•Furthermore, this paper has no faith in the
Gospel of Cheapness. It firmly believes that if
Australia puts on a prohibitive tariff, and starts
its own manufactures in a wholesale way, and
helps and encourages British immigrants to come
out here to supply the labor required in these
manufactures, and makes it a chilled-steel,
riveted, armored, boiler-plated law that a good
living wage must be paid in every industry, and
discourages trusts, combines, and monopolies, and
utterly destroys the great landlord—it firmly
believes that, if all these things are done, this
will be a land of cheap production. The middle-
man may get less, and the landlord may get
less, but there is no reason why, when the country
has got over the preliminary disturbance, and
adapted itself to the new conditions, goods should
cost any more. There isn’t even any reason why
the profits of the genuine manufacturer should be
seriously diminished. It is difficult for any
reasonable being to see why the “biled shirt”
that is made for one penny and a halfpenny and
sold at ss. 6d. shouldn’t struggle through at ss. 6d.
if the maker got 3d. or 4d. or even the towering
sum of fid. The 4|d. extra for making might
come off the ground landlord, who, as a rule,
can stand the loss. A claimant has just gone from
Sydney to London to put in his demand for a bit
of land in the British capital which is said to yield
£2,000,000 a year in rent, and if the shirt was
made in Sydney for fid. instead of in London for
lijd., the corresponding bit of land in that city
would possibly only yield £500,000 a year in rent.
But even £500,000 a year is something, and quite
a lot of people pull through on that. And if the
shirt did cost ss. Bd. instead of ss. fid., it isn’t
worth anybody’s while to lose his soul for 2d., or
to be a sweater for l|d.
THE WADE GOVERNMENT.
The Wade Government in New South Wales is
the old Carruthers gang, minus the rat-like
cuteness of Joseph Carruthers and the pomp-
ously respectable appearance of James Ashton.
It offers nothing new in the way of policy, nothing
new in the way of manners. As regards its
programme it will continue to do as little as
possible, relying for a grip on office on the hope
that it can persuade people that it is “better
than the Labor party.” (To hold office on the
plea that one is the less of two evils, and. the
smaller and less inky devil of the pair, isn’t
very dignified.) As regards its administration,
the Wade Government will continue the
Carruthers’ method of being a small and irrita-
ting insect, buzzing in the ear of Australia. That
not one-tenth of the voters approve of the small-
minded cantankerousness towards Australia which
Wade promises to continue is as certain as any-
thing human can be. There is nothing in the
climate, the natural features, or the usual food of
the New South Wales man to so differentiate
him from other Australians that he must behave
like an unpleasant infant with the colic when-
ever the Commonwealth comes into question.
But the small-minded and stupid Mr. Wade, fol-
lowing blindly in the steps of the small-minded and
cunning Mr. Carruthers, fancies it is “ good
business ” to be anti-Australian, and is resolved
to continue in that attitude. The resolve was
publicly declared at a very early stage.
When the Wade Ministry was announced,
Lyne, on behalf of the Australian Government,
promptly came forward with a courteous invita-
tion to let bygones be bygones, and allow the
dead and evil past to be decently buried and
forgotten. The invitation at that stage was mag-
nanimous. The results of the elections had shown
that anti-Australianism, regarded as a party cry,
was a fail ure. It had wrecked the fortunes of Carru-
thers, and obviously, if there had been on the
other side an Opposition making it its business to
be aggressively Australian, it would have
absolutely ruined the so-called “Reform ” party.
Lyne might, with ample justification, have left
Wade in his unpleasant and ultimately untenable
position, and waited for him to announce his own
failure. But, very handsomely, the Australian
Minister suggested that there was now no reason
for anything but friendliness. Wade promptly
snarled back an assurance that he, as officially
representing N. S. Wales, still intended to be the
sulky child in the corner. A paragraph in the
Sydney Telegraph (8/10/’O7) puts the facts clearly:
In the course of a few complimentary remarks on the
new State Ministry, as published in our issue yesterday,
Mr. Lyne expressed the opinion that there would
not now be the same friction between the State and the
Commonwealth as there had been. It was certainly not
the desire of the Federal Government that there should
be friction. He looked forward to a period of very much
better relations between the Governments. Mr. Lyne
also stated that there was a desire on the part of the
Wade Ministry not to create trouble, and that did away
with the urgency of dealing with the wire-netting duties
until they came along in the ordinary course of the tariff
debate. Mr. Wade no iced these remarks, and made
some observations on them yesterday. “As to the
Federal attitude,” the Premier said, “ I hold very strong
views of the rights of this State as a component part of
the Federation, and that those rights should be observed
loyally and sacredly. I have every hope that Mr. Lyne
realises that the present Government has at heart the in-
terests of the State in this respect, and I trust it will
occur to him that it is very desirable that the forbear-
ance of the State and its people should not be further
strained. I don’t know what ground Mr. Lyne had for
suggesting that we were willing that the discussion on
the wire-netting' duties should be postponed. On the
contrary, we are very anxious that this question should
be disposed of as early as possible, so that the settlers
may know what their position is going to be.”
\ * * ■*
So things are to be as before. The great and
powerful State of N. S. Wales, which should,
from its position, its population, and its wide
range of products, dominate the. Federation, is to
continue the whining complaints of the brat with
an injury to its temper, or the lorn widow with
an incurable habit of tears. To this extent,
N. S. Wales loses by the substitution of Mr.
Wade for Mr. Oarruthers. There is very
little doubt that Oarruthers, who was
cute enough as a rule, would, after the lessons
of the last elections, have dropped his anti-
Australianism. But Wade, who is a wooden
and stupid man, does not know how to
take the opportunity to retire from an untenable
position. He does not see that what destroyed
Oarruthers will surely also destroy him. He
cannot recognise the strength of the Australian
sentiment in N. S. Wales, which at last State
elections was able to return an anti-OARRUTHERS
majority as a protest against Joseph’s anti-Aus-
tralianism, though it had no daily paper support
and no organised party to rally round. If the
N. S. Wales Labor party as a whole had
chosen to even pretend to be Australian, there
would have been an end of the “ Reform ” party.
If Sydney had had but one daily paper
on the right side, an independent party might
have been organised to represent the Australian
sentiment. But judgment was, in a sense,
allowed to go by default. There was no official
appearance on behalf of Australia, no definite
challenge to the miserable pettiness of the State
Frights policy ; and, even so, without guidance,
and without the stimulus of any leadership, a
majority of the voters instinctively voted against
Oarruthers. Many of the Labor candidates
distinctly disavowed being any more Australian
than Oarruthers. Such of them as didn’t were
triumphantly returned ; others got suffrages
on the suspicion that they might be better
than their professions. Every reasonable oppor-
tunity was taken by the voters to be unpleasant
to the Insect Premier. The opportunities were
very limited ; but the net result was to force him
into retirement. Wade can see nothing of all
that, and when Lyne went out of his way to help
him to drop all the old, silly, disgraceful business
he yapped back a refusal.
-x * *
As it stands, the Wade Government is some-
thing that should be destroyed at the earliest
possible moment in the interests of N. S. Males’
dignity and Australia’s progress. In its personnel
it is frankly contemptible, representing more the
dregs than the selected best of the Parliament.
In its programme it is colorless and weak. Its
only outstanding feature is its antagonism to the
Commonwealth. Take away that, and all its
reason for existence vanishes. Unfortunately
there is no party, unless the Owe’Sullivan is able
to organise one, which can, or will, issue a chal-
lenge on that point. In moving a hopeless vote
of censure on the Government at the outset of the
session, McGowen was careful to dissociate him-
self from any suspicion of strong Australian
nationalism. He growled feebly over a number
of small points of administrative abuse, but
avoided coming to issue over the one great ques-
tion upon which the Government is clearly out of
sympathy with the people. On the fa.ce of things,
Wade will'be able to hold office during this Par-
liament, and continue to misrepresent N. S.
Wales in the Federation. The best that the
people can hope for is that at the next ballot the
Australian sentiment will have some organised
representation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Face of Lysaght and the Nose of Labor.
On Thursday night, last week, a majority of the
Federal Labor members proved that they had. in
their composition none of the stuff of which
statesmen are made, and that they are petty-
minded enough to sacrifice the interests of Aus-
tralia in their desire to get back on a Sydney
employer. There was feverish hurry on the part
of the foreign-traders to get the wire-netting duty
dealt with right away, and it proved that the
rush arose in some measure from the fact that a
large number of Labor members were so inflamed
for the moment against the Sydney firm of
Lysaght Bros, that they would vote for either a
low duty or no duty at all. The Lysaght firm has
had trouble with its employees, and some of the
Labor members confused the Lysaght firm with
the Australian wire-netting industry. Concerning
the sense of a firm which, having sunk a large sum
in a netting industry, promptly quarrelled with the
members of a party that held the successful run-
ning of the industry in the hollow of its hand, the
less said the better. It is the firm’s private con-
cern, and anyhow, even the calmest consideration
of the question is likely to lead this paper into
bad language. Instead of keeping on a high duty.


and then by means of the new Protection shak-
ing better wages and conditions out of the manu-
facturers, Labor took away most of the Protection,
and left the industry with a miserable 10 per cent,
against the world and 5 per cent, against Britain.
This was revenue on Lysaghts for having
quarrelled with their employees. And what the
Labor members think they have gained, only
Heaven and they know. Faced with fou'ign
competition, Lysaghts will probably oe unable
to improve the lot of their workers even were they
disposed to, and they are not likely to feel that
way under the circumstances. The industry will
en- vnne to straggle along, and the operatives
win be compelled by lack of other netting
factories to accept Lysaghts’ conditions, good,
bad, or indifferent, or find some other occupation.
The Labor members who voted for the reduced
duty have made the position of the whole in-
dustry bad, and the unfortunate workers will
have the worst end of the bnd condi' 1 ions, as they
always have. Had the duty been r rained, other
netting mills would have started, md created a
deman I for more wire worker-, and thereby in-
creased che value of each individual operative.
This would have enabled Lysaghts’ employees, if
not satisfied, to have found other openings. It
would have increased the number of hands em-
ployed at Lithgow, where the special land of
wire necessary for netting would have been manu-
factured, and it would have increased the demand
for the Australian spelt-t used to coat the
wire, and thereby made things better for the men
employed in that industry, and have benefited
Australian labor generally. But to spite the face
of Lysaght these precious Federal Labor mem-
bers have cut off the noses of quite an army of
their own supporters. And, curiously enough,
they seem to be proud of it, and imagine that it
is a stroke of statesmanship.
Condensed Milk and Idiocy.
That quaint old dodo, the Convinced Freetrader,
fluttered his moulting feathers with disastrous
results to the Cause the other day at Bathurst
(N.S.W.). Gr. Whalan his name was, and he had
been away on a holiday, and on his return wrote
unwarily of his travel impressions in the local
Reidite organ. As thus :
During my recent trip to Queensland, I paid a visit to
the Oresbrook Condensed Milk Factor} 7 , around which
has grown up a new township called Toogoolawab.
... The industry was started by Mr. T. C. McConnell, the
present member for Stanley in the Queensland Legisla-
tive Assembly, and illustrates what a big- landholder may
do for himself and neighbors, by cutting up his estate into
small holdings. It is said that Mr. MoConn ll had a large
property that was by no means as profitable to him as it
should be, when he cone ived the idea of the milk factory.
The land was subdivided and sold to bona fide occupiers,
and the works were placed in the midst of the holdings,
with the understanding that all the milk that could be
produced by the men who had taken up the land
would be purchased. The plan seems to have worked
admirably, and now in addition to a number of
prosperous farmers in the neighborhood, there is a
town of resp ctable size, with every indication that
it will shortly grow to considerable importance.
Now, I am given to understand by users that the quality
of the Cresbrook condensed milk is equal to the best of
the milk imported. ... All the tins required are manu-
factured on the premises, and quite a number of machines
are employed for this part of the business alone—cutting,
fitting, and soldering all being done by machinery. At
the time of my visit, there were about twenty suppliers,
who sent in a daily average of 2200 gallons of milk for
treatment. When tinned and labelled, the milk is packed
in cases containing four dozen tins each. The labelling is
done by girls, and, for the labels themselves, standing
orders of a million at a time are placed with a Brisbane
printing firm.
The springing up of this particular industry has
therefore :
(1) Caused a new township (Toogoolawah) to grow up.
(2) Automatically brought about the sub-division of
one of Bananaland’s big estates.
(3) Settled “a number of prosperous farmers in the
neighborhood.”
Also 2200 gallons of milk are treated daily ; the
quality of the Cresbrook condensed article “is
equal to the best of the milk imported ” ; all the
tins required are manufactured on the spot, and
labels for the said tins are supplied, a million at
a time, by a Brisbane firm employing Brisbane
girls at fair wages. Was it to be wondered at
that G-. Whalan wound up his screed with a
passionate demand for something of the sort in
Bathurst? And then a local Protectionist uprose
and muttered the one word, “Tariff.” You
could have felled Gr. Whalan with the shortest-
weighted tin of boracic acid and goat’s milk
labelled “Pure Swiss” that ever came out of
Amurrika. Seizing his stylo, he unloaded the
following upon the amazed and demoralised
editor: —
Sir, —When I wrote the little article about the Cres-
brook milk factory, I was not aware that the success of
the venture was in any connected with a Customs duty,
but I find, on reference to the Commonwealth tariff re-
cently in force, that there was a duty of Id. per lb. on
preserved milk, and this, I believe, is increased under the
new tariff with which this country is temporarily afflicted.
This, in my opinion, is not in the best interests of the
industry concerned, nor of the community as a whole.
As, however, your correspondent it still a Protectionist,
notwithstanding what appears to me to be the unanswer-
able arguments of those who take the other side, it will
be useless to pursue this correspondence farther, etc.
Now, what can you do with a person like G.
Whalan ?

Beer and Blather.
The “poor working man ” last week got a fine
object-lesson as to the real value of the importers’
tears that are shed on account of his woes. Under
the Federal tariff there is a duty of tenpence a
dozen on bottles. Consequently English bottled
beer has been put up from 11s. 3d. to 12s. per
dozen: and the poor working man, who doesn’t
drink English bottled beer once in three years as
a rule, was made quite moist and uncomfortable
through the Foreign-trade papers weeping over
him. Even these papers had to admit that, if the
poor working man called fora glass of “ English ”
at the bar, he wouldn’t have to pay more than he
had been paying hitherto ; also, as a rule, he
wouldn’t have to pay any more if he bought his
imported beer by the single bottle. He
would only have to pay the extra money if he
bought his bottled English by the dozen—and, as
everybody knows, the average poor working man
doesn’t get in twelve of bottled English (at 12s. a
dozen) oftener than twice a week as a general
thing. As for the poor working man who doesn’t
buy his bottled English by the dozen—
The Federal tariff on British and imported beers and
stouts has driven the consumer to buy colonial products—
This is from the interview with the man whom all
three Sydney Foreign-trade papers called upon as
the authority on the subject —
This has resulted in the latter (Australian beer) of late
developing a very high quality. This much Federation
has certainly been responsible for. Indeed, since 1901,
colonial ales and stouts have become so superior that
they are now competing favorably with the best brands
of the imported article on the market.
So the horrible infliction put upon the poor work-
ing man boils down to this : the tariff has resulted
in his getting a much better beer than he
used to get, and he gets the better beer for the
same money ; while as for the person who doesn’t
reckon himself a poor working man, and buys his
beer m a bottle, he is now getting for ss. 6d. a
dozen a beer equal to the best imported for which
he used to have to pay at least 11s. 3d. That means
that, admittedly as the result of the tariff and the
consequent development of the “colonial” beer
trade, the poor, distressed Australian is getting
two dozen bottles of beer for less than he used to
have to pay for one dozen, and the two dozen are
as good, if not better, and when he has got the
two dozen instead of the old one dozen he still
has 3d. left to buy a cigar with.
The Case of Reuben Ward.
The N.S.W. Government has made a compromise
between its conscience and the hangman by com-
muting the death sentence passed on Reuben
Ward, at Dubbo, to life imprisonment. It is
carefully remarked in the papers that the
matter will be reconsidered “should any
new evidence turn up.” But the trouble
lies rather in the want of any reasonable
turning-up on the part of the old evidence.
Ward was convicted solely on the statement of a
girl admittedly subject to fits—a girl who left a
hotel with him without any compulsion; who
went with him, without any compulsion, to
the railway carriage sheds at night; who
there got into a deserted railway carriage
with him, by persuasion, it may be,
but without compulsion; and who alleges
that what happened afterwards was done by force
and contrary to her wishes. It was on the bare,
blank, utterly uncorroborated statement of the
girl who did so many remarkable things that
Reuben Ward was convicted. And the law
tossed Reuben for his life, with a double-headed
coin by assuring the girl that even if she swore
falsely in the matter, and was bowled out, no
harm would come to her. It didn’t give that
assurance in words, but the constant immunity of
the terribly-frequent female who makes that kind
of charge and who is bowled out, was about as
solid a promise as a written indemnity would
have been. Under these conditions the Govern-
ment has compromised with its conscience by a
sentence of life imprisonment, “and the matter
may be further considered if some new evidence
turns up.”
The Speech that Wasn’t, but Should Have
Been.
The Bulletin has got its own copy of the Speech
at the opening of the N.S.W. Parliament. The
name is on the wrapper. None other genuine :
Gentlemen op the Leghslative Assembly, and Gentlemen
who, being unable to get into the Leg. Assembly,.
HAVE BEEN SENT TO THE LEG. COUNCIL, AND OTHERS.
My advisers would just as soon not meet you. Bm,
there would be a row if they didn’t.
However, they will get the thing- over as soon as they
can. They realise that the less you know of them, the.
better you will like them.
You will observe that J. H. Carruthnrs is not my
principal adviser any longer. The fact is, while my back
was turned, he induced the nice-looking old gentleman I
left in my shoes to agree to make an awful ass of N. 8.
Wales. Of course I couldn’t say much. And really the
poor little beggar got such a hiding when he appealed to
the country, and his colleagues were so nasty to him
about it, that I was glad afterwards I didn’t say much.
My late principal'adviser therefore found it advisable
to refrain from anything more exciting than the attempt-
to pick doubles at Randwick. If levity were not out of
place at such a time as this, I should remark that he
backed Collarit before the general elections, and found it
was the wrong horse.
Before the unfortunate outbreak of “ the meaner side-
of politics ” among my old advisers, they had arranged to-
fix up a Department of Agriculture. The idea was to
make two Ministers screw where only one screwed be-
fore. That would have fixed one more supporter for
certain. Unfortunately, they couldn’t agree upon the
supporter.
With regard to the Commonwealth of Australia, my;
new advisers are for the moment inclined to sit on a rail..-
It doesn’t pay, as the old crowd thought it would, to be
nasty to Australia. If you will give them two or three
months’grace they will make up their minds as to how -
disagreeable they intend to be on this question.
Gentlemen op the Legislative Assembly,—
Thank Heaven and the Constitution, the other old'
gentlemen haven’t got anything to do with the finances.
You only, therefore, are on in this act.
My old crowd had a ripping time last year. The chief
financial fakir figured out a surplus of £1,493,272. By
the new system you can have any size in surpluses that-
you like. We make them by the simple process of not
paying for things until after the surplus is declared. You.
will observe that, as we have to pay for the things after-
wards, we are not any better off than if we didn’t have a
surplus at all. Still even a bogus surplus catches some
votes.
Various remissions of taxes are proposed. Meantime,
we will go on selling over a million pounds’ worth of land
in the year and collaring the proceeds. In other words,
we make it easy for ourselves by making it darned hard
for our children. However, our children haven’t got
votes, so they don’t matter.
Still, between ourselves, don’t make too sure of the re-
mission of taxes. There’s a horrible slump in copper and
tin mining, and in several parts the crops look like turn-
ing out a failure. We seem to have had the last of the
run of good years.
You will miss my late chief adviser Joseph. I shall not
be here much longer myself, but I shall always remember
him as the young man who knew when to come in out of
the drought. Financial reputations have been very cheap
these last three years. Even the old Cyclorama Co.,
which fought the Battle of Gettysburg for so many years,
paid a dividend the other day, and it hadn’t done such a
thing before for 15 years. Joseph knew when to be ill.
Hon. Gentlemen of the Legislative Council and Gentle-
men of the Legislative Assembly,—
My advisers promised various things during the elec-
tions. They will make some sort of pretence to carry a
few of these promises into effect.
It really, however, isn’t worth mentioning what they
are. As my advisers are going to crawl into recess and
safety as soon as they can see an openings they do not
propose to seriously attempt legislation of any size.
You will now proceed to waste three weeks or so talk-
ing about everything under the sun on the pretence that
you are talking about these few remarks. You know it’s
a hollow fraud, this so-called speech of mine, and if I had
just telephoned down to Mr. Speaker to tell you to “ bog
in,” or if you had just “bogged in” without disturbing
me in my arduous duties of laying foundation stones,
things would have gone on exactly the same.
Gentlemen, bog in.

G. Reid.
The Bulletin does not profess to understand the
curious intricacies of legal etiquette, or the
wonderful code of legal ethics. Whenever it
attempts to consider such questions there arises a
quaint and noisy phantasmagoria. In the centre
a bill of Costs revolves and splutters like a huge
Catharine-wheel. From four corners bills of
Costs crackle and explode like that fireworks
design known to our infancy as “ the-devil-among-
the-tailoys.” Here and there grin Cheshire-cat-
faces of Costs. From all quarters issue dismal
howls of Costs. That is the Law as we see it. In a
spirit of humble inquiry, therefore, it is hereby
asked : How doesG. Reid, K.C.—aspirant to the
post of Prime Minister of the Commonwealth,
said to be aspirant to the post of Australian High
Commissioner —stand in regard to the Myerson
case ? The Myerson case, so far as it can be dis-
entangled from the Costs (which were enormous),
resolves itself into this: The two Myersons
bought goods on credit, promptly sold them at
auction, got together some £6OOO of ready money,
entrusted it to a female Myerson, and
then went bankrupt. The creditors, who
were all in the softgoods line, and who
hadn’t hearts to correspond with the nature of
their goods, alleged fraud, and the Myersons
(male) were put on trial. They sought legal help.
Two solicitors— Dan Levy (a “Reform” M.L.A.
in N. S. Wales) and E. Abigail —were engaged
(the latter seeming to drop out of the proceedings
at an early date), also G. Reid, K.C., Leader of
the Opposition in the Australian Parliament.
The legal help seems to have been enlisted by the
female Myerson, who paid the Costs in advance
in big notes. (“Big notes! Big notes!
When have we heard that phrase before?)
Levy and Abigail have been put under a robust
and rasping cross-examination by the Official
Assignee in the matter of taking those big notes.
That gentleman seemed to think that they ought
to have suspected that the lady with the big notes
was distributing money stolen from the creditors.
But so far G. Reid does not seem to have been
asked a question. Yet his share of the Costs was
enormous. He had a first fee of 150 guineas and
the daily refreshers of 50 guineas, the latter being
paid not through a solicitor but by the female
Myerson or her son in person. Again, The
Bulletin disavows any idea of dogmatising in a
matter of Costs. The subject is too vast, too
sacred, too much suggestive of the Eleusinian
mysteries. But —in a spirit of humble inquiry—
Is 150 guineas Brief fee and 50 guineas a day fair union
pay for a barrister defending a trumpery criminal case,
or should the huge fees offered have suggested to a man
of average intelligence that the money was a little shady?
Is G. Reid a man of average intelligence?
Is a barrister supposed to take his fees direct from the
client—in big notes—or is he supposed to get them
through a solicitor ?
Should the curiousness of the method of payment have
suggested to a man of common intelligence that the
Myerson money was fishy ?
'Supposing G. Reid knew that the Myerson female’s
money, tendered to him in big notes, in such irregular
fashion, was, in all human certainty, money with a
dubious history, and supposing that he recognised that
the fees paid to him were inordinately large, would that
have involved him in any resp Jnsibility to be coy about
taking them ?
It is on this last point that The Bulletin espe-
cially desires guidance. An answer would throw
•a little light on the mystery of Costs and the
ethics of a noble profession.

Perjury Committed While You Wait.
The woman who goes into the witness-box and
pours out a story of foul wrong and outrage which
the jury doesn’t believe is so common nowadays
that the daily papers don’t even mention her. At
Sydney Quarter Sessions last week a man was up
charged with robbery with violence. The woman
in this case told a circumstantial yarn of how she
j ust happened to be coming home from seeing a
friend off by a boat, when the man accosted her
in Elizabeth-street. After she had virtuously
told him she wanted none of his “funny busi-
ness,” he struck her in the face, dazed her, then
snatched a purse from her, and ran. She hadn’t,
she swore; seen him before on that evening,
though she had known him previously. Then,
one after the other, five people, men and women,
trooped into the box, and swore that they had
been with the couple for two hours before the
disturbance, and one of them was a girl
who had been sent into a hotel by the woman
with the tale of wrong to bring the prisoner out
•to her. Also, there was a fairly coherent story of
a quarrel the previous evening between the man
<and the woman, the woman accusing him of dally-
ing with a rival cat, and there xvas evidence of a
.recurrence of the bitter argument during this
,evening. Yet that woman swore she hadn’t seen
tyhe man on that evening previous to the alleged
-outrage. On a dozen other material points the
-evidence on which she was relying to get the man
tin gaol for four or five years was torn to pieces.
Fortunately that man could get witnesses, and he
~hasn’t gone to gaol; the jury didn’t take two
ijminutes to acquit. But it wasn’t the woman’s
fault. And nothing whatever has happened to
,her, and nothing is likely to happen. Two
•other women of the same profession were wit-
nesses, and they will know that nothing happens
to the woman. If they, in their turn, want to
. get even with a man who has crossed them—well,
if they use the old-fashioned poison or knife they
are very liable either to hang or to go to gaol for
it, whereas they can safely swear his liberty away.
If the event comes off. well and good ; if it doesn’t,
they are none the worse. Judges see this vile
form of perjury every month of the year—see it
so often that they seem nowadays to take no
notice of it; and the newspapers have got into the
same habit. As for the pobce, the equally
deplorable fact is that the female perjurers are
usually on the side cf the prosecution, and the
policeman is on that side practically every time.
He may not be prepared to swear that white is
black, but heis prepared toseedark-brownsmudges
on it. For instance, in the very case cited above
the question of the woman’s occupation arose. She
represented herself as a woman with a Bast cer-
tainly, but no Present. The police, propping up
her evidence, bore out that idea—the constable
“ hadn’t seen her for a considerable time past.”
Yet witness after witness, admittedly of this
class, referred to her without reproach as their
nightly companion. Of course the police said
nothing on which they could be charged with
perjury. They simply knew only what it suited
the prosecution to knew ; and though there is no
prospect of gaol for that sort of ignorance, there
ought to be an immediate prospect of the sack.
Then the administration of justice would be
several degrees purer, and one could look upon
the courts with much greater confidence—and
confidence in the courts is probably the very first
essential of civilisation.
Our Unfortunate Colored Brethren.
Considerable of a howl was raised a couple of
months ago by the Spruce Myth party of black
Australians and the missionary gang which is
always worrying about Tommy T anna’s soul, to
the effect that the deportation of the Kanakas
meant sending “ our unfortunate colored
brethren ” to certain death. It was explained
that the deported niggers were murdered by their
compatriots, who envied them the possessions
they brought back in their swags from Queens-
land. Certainly one contrary missionary got up
on his limbs and said that it was the other way
about—that the returning niggers brought guns
and tomahawks, and wiped out the Kerristianised
niggers on the islands who had been carefully
taught how not to fight. But at first his voice was
lost in the din, and be had as much chance as a
baby devil at the back of Hell. Now his voice is
no longer lost in the din. Terrible tales of
murder and rapine on the island of Malaita have
appeared in the Spruce Myth press, and the mis-
sion crowd has changed its tune, and declared that
the peaceful nigger from Queensland has returned
in a belltopper and a frock-coat, and is slaying
everybody promiscuously, and that our unchristian
White Australia policy is responsible. But
further investigation seems to show that there
are really no more murders than usual on the
islands. The returned Kanaka is neither more
nor less murderous than the one that stayed at
home; the trouble is that he is full of a coarse
public-house contempt for preachers. He knows
they aren’t gods. He believes he knows that
they are dirt. He has seen their empty churches
in Australia; he has seen them passing round the
frayed hat and grovelling for contributions ; he
has heard them called sin-shifters and sky-pilots
and devil-dodgers ; he has found them without
honor or credit or authority among the folk of
their own race; and with this knowledge he is
undermining the Church. So the parson holds
that the White Australia policy, which brings
back these niggers, is a sin, and he would
swear almost anything to persuade others to
the same belief. What wonder? His living is
threatened, and the muddy hand of the scoffer is
being wiped on his coat-tail.
Senator Pulsford’s letter, from England, cen-
suring N.S.W. Premier Carruthers for his
foolish wire-netting “coup,” contains some in-
structive passages :
Since the time when, in the Supreme Court of our
State, some four years ago, you sought for, and obtained,
a decision that goods imported by a State for the use of
its services were not liable to Customs duties, I have
wondered that the matter was allowed to rest, instead of
being resolutely pressed to a termination. Some time
ago, in the Senate, I asked certain questions on the sub-
ject, and, amongst other information, elicited the fact
that N.S.W., though successful in the action named, had
yet been obliged to pay not only its own costs, but those
of the Commonwealth also. This singular outcome of an
action at law resulted from the fact that the Common-
wealth is, by the Constitution, compelled to credit
and debit each State with its own Customs
revenue, and the expenditure in relation thereto. I
could not but observe at the time that, although N.S.W.
continued to pay duties on its imports, it was not out of
pocket by so doing, since the moneys paid as duties came
back again at the end of the month as revenue, and not
only so, but in cases where the duty was debited to loan
moneys it meant an actual increase of revenue. In view,
therefore, of the knowledge that, while the present
arrangement with regard to State revenue existed,
N.S.W., so far from losing, might even be gaining re-
venue, and that you, as Treasurer, apparently raised no
complaint, I hesitated to further deal with the subject in
the Senate.
This helps to explain why Carruthers didn’t
press to a determination that much-talked-
of action against the Commonwealth in support
of its “rights.” Carruthers was making money
out of State-imported material because he paid
for the imported railway material and so on out
of loans, and he charged the duty on the imported
railway material to loans, and then when the
duty (paid for out of loans) came back to Carru-
thers at the monthly division of the Federal
surplus, he put it into revenue and said with a
great flapping of his own banner, “ See what a
great financier lam ! ” So he was content to let
things go at that until he thought he saw a chance to
rake in some sorely-needed political advertisement
by being nasty with the Australian Government
on the subject. Also, the item shows the exact
value of the virtuous indignation of some legisla-
tors against anything savoring of “a viola-
tion of the Constitution.” Pulsford was
ready to shed his last vest button in defence of
the Constitution so long as he thought his high
moral attitude would pay his own State. But
when Pulsford found that the alleged “ viola-
tion” enabled the State Premier, who represented
his own old party, to do a villainous but profitable
political swindle by converting loan money into
revenue, Mr. Pulsford “ hesitated to further deal
with the subject.”

Up in Queensland the Nerang Shire Council has
sent around a circular, imploring other shire
councils to be indignant about the tariff and the
“ Federal Parliament’s grab” of extra pay. The
circular arrived at Balmoral lately, and the local
council was indignant at great length, and
according to request. One conspicuous ass, who
brayed louder than the rest, announced that “ the
grab” was plain robbery, because “ their salary
was fixed by the Constitution.” The Constitution
expressly gives power to the Parliament to fix its
own salary; but a small thing like that doesn’t
matter at Balmoral. On that basis of stupendous
ignorance, Balmoral settled the matter;
whereupon the rest of the business was rushed
through, and “ the matter of dealing with the
goat and dog tax was left with the chairman.”
The Bulletin approves of that, anyway; it
wouldn’t like to trust the Balmoral Shire Council
to deal with the matter of the goat tax.
Vive le Duma!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O ! The Czar has got the Duma,
And the Duma’s got the Czar ;
And the high conflicting parties
Do they know just “ where they are ?
For the one goes legislating
With an amplitude of jaw,
While the other’s abrogating
With a plenitude of paw.
Bones of Ivan and of Peter
(0 ! the million moujik slaves
It’s enough to make you wriggle
In the marble of your graves—
Just to see your poor descendant
How he trembles on the throne,
When the Duma says : “ The People
Have a sou} to call theit own ! ’
Shall the glorious Little Father,
Who was King and Lord and God,
Shall he stoop to take the counsel
Of the pedlar and the clod ?
Shall the Romanoff who lorded
As the sole surviving King,
Shall he meekly tread the measure
That his faithful subjects sing?
Know ye how they trained old Bruin
To the dance in days of yore ?
Plates of iron, duly heated,
Were his dainty dancing floor ;
And whene’er the bear went tripping
To delight the gaping street,
Twas the mem’ry of the iron
That inspired his dancing feet.
And before the Czar can trip it
As a modern monarch should,
He must pace the floor of torment
Till there rises in his blood
Such a love of dancing duly
In a strictly legal maze
To the tune, so soft and trancing.
That his piping Duma plays.
