The Great Shoddy Frost.
One of the saddest ceremonies for months was G.
Reid’s monster Tariff Demonstration, at Sydney
Town Hall, on Monday night. The Telegraph
preserved a wary silence the next morning as to
the number of citizens who pressed forward to
demonstrate; the S.M.H. opined there were
“about 500.” This scribe was on the
spot, and counted. There were 22 on
the platform. Thirty souls thronged ’the
left-hand gallery, and a solid 54 were massed
to the right. The body of the hall seethed with a
teeming congregation of 120 or 130, mostly females..
When this writer arrived, at about 8.30 p.m.,
G. Reid was explaining, in the midst of a tomb-
like silence, that the meeting was bound to have
been a frost anyhow, as it had been delayed too
long; “there would have been no end of enthu-
siasm,” is how the Telegraph reports him, “but
it was not to be expected when the people had
become accustomed to the tariff.” This state-
ment that the tariff had improved so vastly on
acquaintance that opposition was already almost
dead failed to appeal to a small man, who de-
manded hoarsely, “What erbout Nelken? Why
didn’t yer turn up at Nelken’s meeting, yer
treacherous cow ? Why don’t yer fight the tariff
in Melbourne?” He was soothed by a lady
friend, and G. Reid droned on, saying dread-
ful things like this :
No one would cry out if the stamp duty on receipts
were removed either, but the charming thing about Pro-
tection was that once the people got accustomed to a 20
per cent, duty, they had a great desire to be taxed
another 10 per cent., and a further 10 per cent, on top
of it.
This assurance that Protection only required to
be tried to become popular fell rather flat. At
9.10 p.m. this writer stole away, leaving behind
the indefinable “atmosphere” that so surely
connotes the presence of something dead.
Just about the time when S. M. Herald was sarcas-
tically asking, with special reference to the sugar
industry, what Australia was going to do with its
surplus products when local production, shoved
along by High Protection, has passed thp home
demand, N.S.W. Mines Minister was reading this
report from his State’s S’African Commercial
Agent Valuer
Our sugar trade with South Africa practically com-
menced in 1904, the imports from Australia in that year
amounting to 560 tons, of a value of £7OBO. This sugar
was pronounced by the buyers to be very suitable for the
market, and the trade steadily increased, the quantity
imported in 1905 being 844 tons, of the value of £14,492.
The trade was, however, hampered by preferential Cus-
toms duties which allowed Mauritius sugar to be admitted
as “ unrefined,” at 3s. 6d. per 1001 b.; but classed Austra-
lian sugar as “refined,” and imposed a duty of ss. per
1001 b. upon it. ... At the Customs conference in
March, 1906, the duty was altered to 3s. 6d. per 1001 b.
of all ordinary sugars, and ss. for special sugars, such as
loaf, icing, etc., no preference being given to any coun-
try. Although these duties only came into force during
the latter half of 1906, they had such an effect upon our
trade that the quantity imported from Australia in 1906
rose to 2849 tons, valued at £32,629. The total quantity
of sugar imported into South Africa during the year 1906
was 56,410 tons, valued at £613,687, so that there is
plenty of room for a further expansion.
Considering that within three years Australian
sugar exports to S’Africa alone climbed from
£7OBO worth to £32,629 worth—this despite (during
the greater part of the time) unfavorable Customs
treatment—and considering that on the 1906
figures there are still about £581,000 worth per
annum of trade for Australian sugar to cut into
in S’Africa ; and further, considering that what
has happened in S’Africa may also happen in other
countries which the Herald ignored in its pessi-
mistic wail, just as it ignored S’Africa, there
doesn’t seem to be much need for the Herald to
worry acutely about what Australia is going to
do with its surplus sugar. For an industry which
is allegedly ruined by Protection, the loss of its
Kanaka, high wages, scarcity of white labor,
and Government interference, the sugar biz. seems
to be struggling along all right.

Sydney Telegraph is shocked to find a duty
put upon illustrated books. Australia can’t dis-
pense with Milton, Shakspeare, etc., it says,
simply “in order to encourage local products.”
Who wants Australia to dispense with Milton,
Shakspeare, etc.? The United States doesn’t
dispense with either John or William ; yet the
U.S. have the same Customs regulations as Lyne
has introduced. The result is that all the big
European publishers re-issue their books in
N’York, and the U.S. get them that way.
Probably the Telegraph’s idea is that that means
a higher price, and that the people should get
their literature at the lowest possible price. In
that case, suppose the Telegraph starts at home.
It pays 30 or 40 per cent, dividends on its actual
capital. Suppose it cuts the div. down to 5 per
cent., and gives the reader the benefit of the con-
cession ? Or, if it isn’t prepared to do that, and
reckons that its readers are entitled to pay a
trifle to provide capitalists with good dividends,
it might anyhow knock off being a hypocrite, and
allow that readers are also entitled to, if neces-
sary, pay a trifle extra in order to provide Aus-
tralian workmen with Australian wages.

According to Col. H. C. Stanley, the Aus-
tralian representative of the big English ship-
building and gun-making firm of Armstrong,
Whitworth and Co., those people are anxious to
make a contract with the Federal Government to
build some of the vessels that will be required for
the Australian navy. Not only that, but they
want, he says, to become the pioneers of Aus-
tralia’s big shipbuilding industry, and to plant a
shipyard out beyond Williamstown (Vic.), where
the water is deep and there is a natural dock
which, with a little improvement, would accom-
modate a dozen or so large vessels. Furthermore,
this firm wants to use Australia’s iron and coal in
its business, and to train and employ Australian
mechanics. Colonel Stanley can’t talk details
so far, and he apparently isn’t in a position to say
a very definite “Yes” or “No” about anything';
but as far as he can go it is good, cheerful talk,
and Australia will encourage him to see Deakin
and get down to details.
A DEPUTATION which lately waited on N.S.W. rail-
way magnate Johnson had the same old complaint
to make. The deputation represented the iron
foundries and agricultural machinery makers in
country towns, and it came to complain of the
unfair advantage that is given to the Sydney im-
porter as against the country manufacturer. For
instance, the freight on iron, the raw material of
the machinery maker, to Orange was stated at
655. a ton. On finished goods, such as ploughs
and harvesters, it was 325. 6d. Two three-
furrow ploughs can go to Temora for 445.
The iron to make them would be charged
90s. The ploughs would be worth £52; the
iron £l2. Steel shafting, used in manufacture,
consigned to, say, Temora, would cost £6 11s. 2d.
per ton ; shafting made in America or Germany
and already made up into a finished machine
would be £2 4s. And while this discriminating
tariff was in operation against the Temora imple-
ment maker and his raw material, the Temora
farmer could send his raw material (grain) to
Sydney for 10s. 6d. a ton. Johnson, in reply,
said that the Great Backbone of the Country ex-
pected the railways to be run for his benefit, but
he (Johnson) would think hard on the question.

Gregory Wade, the new State Premier of
N.S.W., following in the footsteps of his pre-
decessor Carruthers, has stated :
I trust it will occur to him [Lyne] that it is very
desirable that the forbearance of this State [N.S.W.] and
its people should not be further strained.
But, like all the rest of the State Frights
weepers, Wade quite forgot to mention what
wrong had been done to N.S.W., or how
it had been strained. It was the lack
of any tangible wrong which prevented
Carruthers going to the High Court and de-
manding redress. It is the same complete lack
of any tangible wrong which prevents Wade
going to the High Court, and calling at the top
of his voice for redress. Carruthers is a lawyer.
Wade is a lawyer. The first idea of a lawyer, if
he has the ghost of a case, is to go to the court
and call at the top of his voice for redress. And
the fact that wild oxen won’t drag these two
screaming lawyers to the High Court to ask for
redress is a clear proof that they have no case,
and are perfectly aware of the fact.
*
That superior person, Ramsay Macdonald,
English Labor M.P., tells the poor, underpaid,
underfed millions of the country he legislates for
that Australian Laborites are “making a pro-
found mistake” in troubling about tariffs, and
Arbitration Courts, etc., instead of “getting a
hold upon the means of production, and starting
iron-works, etc.” This hifalutin talk about “the
means of production ”is mostly rubbish. What is
really worth worrying about —first, anyway—is,
not the means, but the fruits of production.
Macdonald, if he likes, can own the tree, if we
can make sure of the apples. Macdonald and
these other English snap-your-chains theorists are
not practical enough to see that. And naturally,
then, they are not practical enough to see also
that even if the State made iron and steel goods,
it would have to prohibit the importation of low-
wage-made iron and steel goods if it wanted the
Home market. Macdonald and his crowd, in
short, feed the underfed English millions on
phrases. The practical Australian Labor crowd,
on the other hand, prefers to feed its people on
something that you can cut with a knife, or eat
with a spoon.

The S. M. Herald nowadays encourages its
country correspondents to harass the Australian
Government with pinpricks. But they are
clumsy tools. One of them, at Collarendabri,
got in a yarn, the other day, about the disgust of
the backblocks because some telegraph stations
had been “ reduced to telephone offices.” There
is no secrecy, said the Herald’s stipendiary
mosquito ; “anyone who happens to be within 20
or 30 yards of the building can hear the operator’s
voice.” When the country correspondent talks
about snakes and bunyips the city has to believe
him, snakes and bunyips being off the city man’s
beat ; but when the country correspondent talks
about telephones, and says that the voice of the
operator can be heard 30yds. away—well, the city
remembers its own telephone bureaux, and
wonders why even the Herald doesn’t blush
to print such mischief-making rubbish.

Mrs. Molyneux Parkes, the feminine agitator
in the cause of Anti-Sosh, has been writing to the
Sydney dailies
I am in a position to make the following statement, on
competent authority : One of Europe’s foremost art coun-
tries had decided to arrange an exhibition of modern art
in Australia for a brief period during next year—an
exhibition which should have been of the greatest
value to our young artists. Since the Lyne tariff has
become known in Europe, and Australia thereby an-
nounced herself an enemy of art, the proposal has lapsed.
If passed, the Lyne tariff will, like the cold bleak wind
damaging the budding fruit-trees, retard the artistic
development of our youth for generations.
This makes the second wonderful exhibition that
nobody had ever heard about, but which was
just going to happen, only the tariff blighted it
in its infancy, so to speak. In this case the gloom
is deepened by the fact that even the name of the
“foremost art country ” is kept a dead secret, like
the name of the man who was going to invest
£700,000 9s. 2d. in a new industry, but didn’t.
Probably the great art centre is Bulgaria. In
that case Mrs. Parkes had better cable to Bul-
garia and tell it to go right ahead. The usual
practice is to refund the duty when the pictures
leave the country again, or else to hold the ex-
hibition in bond, so to speak.. It is quite a simple
business, and there is no reason why Mrs. M.
Parkes should worry.

Sydney Telegraph, commenting on the prag*
matical utterances of ex-Consul Iwasaki, when
he was being farewelled in Sydney, stated an
amazing principle of International Law in the
interests of its friend the Monkey :
The people of every country have every right, so long
as they are capable of enforcing it, to say who shall come
in and who shall not. But all who are already in, of
whatever nationality, are entitled to protection, and to
unimpeded facilities of pursuing their vocations.
The alleged principle is plain rubbish. Every
civilised Government exercises the right to deport
foreigners Whom it regards as dangerous, or
otherwise undesirable, by Executive order, with-
out proving or even alleging any specific offence
against them. Entire populations have been
deported in that way. The whole of the French
settlers in the Province of Acadie (now Nova
Scotia), after its surrender to Great Britain in
the middle of the 18th century, were deported ;
their offence was a refusal to become British sub-
jects, as provided by the terms of surrender.
Even when it comes to parting with a few
yards of soil for the especial purpose of helping
the State to defend the rest of their property, the
land sharks open their maws wide enough to
swallow a universe. The other day the Common-
wealth wan ced a strip of land near Hobart (Tas.)
for a rifle range, and this is the record of three of
the transactions: —
Colin Bain—wanted £8000; was offered £875 ; took
£l5OO.
O. Lord—wanted £l7OO ; took £920.
Hobart Golf Club —wanted £4500; finally accepted
£1230.
This last-mentioned superior shark actually issued
a writ for the £4500 it claimed, but wasn’t game
to fight the matter out publicly.
Grasshoppers are appearing in millions in some
districts. The wide-awake pastoralist is setting the
Tyree Spray to work. The results are wonderful. In a
few days the pest is thoroughly wiped out. The direc-
tions are : Spray the stony ridges where the grasshoppers
lay their eggs. It settles them completely. No necessity
to grow grasshoppers, or prickly pear, or lantana, or
briars, or blackberries, or any other pest. Get a few
Tyree Sprays and make money out of your land. Provi-
dence helps those who help themselves. Write to Tyree
Limited, 314 George Street, Sydney.*

“ The Midnight Wedding ” was revived on
Saturday night last, at Sydney Royal, after a
term of months. It is plain drammer, as dis-
tinguished from the drama it claims to be.
Drammer in uniform does not differ materially
from the same article in tweed or other suitings.
The scene, for example, in “The Private Chapel
of the Strelsburgs” where the Princess Astrea
weds Paul, son of the Crown Prince, might
equally represent Merely Mary Ann being married
to her Doom (Bill) in the Trinity Church of their
joint bailiwick. And so throughout. There
is no line of the dialogue the distinction of
which serves to hoist this particular cele-
bration out of the ruck. The humor is
a thing to grieve over. The necessity for the
wearisome dress-exchange turn by Kathie and
Bobo in the Barrack Square does not appear. It
is eminently innocuous, man and maid retiring to
a couple of modestly reversed and segregated
sentry boxes to do their undressing. But the fun
is pointless and long drawn out, and checks what-
ever action the play has ; while, as business, it is
old enough to fly the white whisker signal of
decrepitude.
• • * •
When the above has been recorded it must
go to the credit of the players that they make the
crude stuff they have to handle fairly interesting.
If Harcourt Beatty doesn’t quickly capture the
collective heart of the Sydney and suburban
matinee girl, it will be clear proof that the Tariff,
combined with the Wrongs inflicted on Ma State,
have caused that soulful young person to degene-
rate and run to seed. Beatty is the one actor
Australia has at the present time who is equal to
making Paul a more or less convincing person-
age ; and on Saturday night Beatty was at his
best. Gaston Mervale plays well the part of Von
Scarsbruck, a perfect devil of a fellow', with a
character surpassing in hideousness that of a
Sosli candidate as conceived by a Deform
League chairwoman. Miss Madge Mclntosh
acts with force and earnestness in the rdle
of the Astrea Princess. There is a sort
of stock line in those Anthony Hope princesses,
who are Zenda-born and bred, whatever country
the programme may allege is theirs; and they
don’t in consequence give scope for originality of
treatment. It is rather depressing to see this
Bland-Anderson sort of play interpreted by mimes
who should be essaying better things ; the mount-
ing of it is as artistic as the playing thereof.

Andrew Mack is now giving the old ones
an airing at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, and is fight-
ing his way to the Earldom of Kenmare nightly,
and shaking a nimble foot on the passage.
Andrew has a habit of being an Earl, but, any-
how, the new peer has points over the previous
one; he gets the necessary purse-full for
the journey from a vague mine out
Nevada way. The other Earl, the ragged
one, is lifted to magnificence through the
bounty of a faithful old retainer who has saved it
up in a stocking ; and even for melodrama pur-
poses that stocking is just about worn out. After
Andrew Mack has been well-established at Ken-
mare Castle,' “ Arrah-na-Pogue ” will be revived
for a week ; then “Tom Moore.”

“ The Southern Cross ” continues to shine,
twinkle, blaze, and constellate considerably in
the dramatic firmament at Sydney Criterion.
The purist may hold that this play twinkles a
shade more than it constellates. But what odds ?
The Criterion public wants to see the show' it
patronises blaze, and “The Southern Cross’’does
it. When it’s neither shining, twinkling, nor
constellating (which is nearly all the same), it is
blazing like a conflagration in Hades, with every-
body rushing madly for the door.
** * *
The Butt-Rumford pair is filling Sydney
Towm Hall with voice and persons. Two more
concerts are promised by the Taits, who are
smiling so w'idely over the success of the Great
•Volume of Lady that if they used glove-stretchers
their smiles couldn’t be wider. The Large
Mass of Lady lets go her big voice most
suitably in such harmonies as “ Divinities du
Styx”; but when she sings wee songs about
babies who have ten little toes and one little nose,
all the actual and latent parenthood in the audi-
ence is so touched that it becomes more terrible
with noise and flaunted hanky than an army with
banners. The Butt repertoire seems to be very
limited. Fortunately for the Great Voiced Party,
the great heart of the Public beats most quickly
for old friends..

Melbourne Princess’s indulged in four acts
of excited Conan Doyle story on Saturday night
and called it “ The Brigadier Gerard.” “The
Brigadier” is full of rush and bluster and bright
French uniforms and clash of arms. The first act
is an effective scene, and carries the only “real-
life ” tone in the outfit. Plimmer makes a great
point as the blusterous Major Olivier, then prac-
tically subsides for the rest of the play—except in
the last act, where he turns up again as an apology.
Julius, the Versatile, puts all the light of his blue
eyes into fine, flashing glances, and swaggers
through his part with tremendous “blow,”
and with the same voice wherewith he
used to beg “a little red rose.” Elbert Orton,
the Magnificent, is “in front ” for this play, and
the new leading lady, Miss Beatrice Irwin, has
her chance. The lady certainly does not rise to
expectations. There is much sensibility and
temperament in her work, but she struggles with
too much self-consciousness, and is inclined to
over-act. As the Countess de Roquelaure
there is room for occasional tense quietness,
but Miss Irwin flutters constantly in a distract-
ingly restless manner, like a captured butterfly.
She looks exquisitely pretty, and nervousness may
have much to do with her fluttering, but it would
be a relief if she changed the tone of her voice at
times. All through there is the same high,
tremulous timbre; no matter whether she be
imploring mercy from a tyrant or telling her
confident Brigadier that she loves him. Never-
theless, her work pleases the house, and she
receives enthusiastic acclamation at the end of
the play.. Bently does really good work. His
stiffness is lost (or permissible) as Major Bron,
and his Bron might have been born a soldier, so
effective is his commanding. Leslie Victor puts
an artist’s touch into his Talleyrand. It is rather
hard to have one’s Napoleon served up in
so large a dish. Hubert Willis has a fine concep-
tion of the Emperor part, but his light voice lacks
impressiveness, and his thin face and tall, padded
body do not help the eye sense. But the whole
play is finely dramatic, and is likely to have a
bounding run through its limit of eleven days.
** * *
“ The Great Rescue ” continues to be
made at the Theatre Royal (Melb.), to everyone’s
satisfaction. Red sparks fly out of the papier-
mache engine which the toy motor beats in a race
through 1500 yards of whirling canvas scenery,
and no realistic detail is neglected. Probably
the present drama will see Bland Holt to the end
of his present season, which is to close, with a
flourish of trumpets and amidst public lamenta-
tions, in about three weeks.
** * *
The Holloway co. this week wanders
through “The Shadows of a Great City,” a senile
melodrama introduced to Australia by Grattan
Riggs, some night in the long, long ago, when the
kangaroo still looked curiously down the shafts at
Bendigo and fled at night through the deserted
Exchange. The usual scrap-heap of broken hearts,
butchered humanity, and foolish infatuations has
been raked together by its author with the in-
evitable results. The “ Shadows” serves no good
purpose save to emphasise the good acting of the
people who struggle with the undertaking, and it
is a pity that such people should struggle with
such an undertaking. It is waste of people.
Miss Holloway is always pleasant, but as Nellie
Standish, the noble gyurl who stands rigidly by
her superfluous parent, she’s particularly
appealing, Harry Sweeney plays very capably
the swain who runs away from home and then
runs back again, and Godfrey Cass adds another
amazing study in stage villainy to his gallery.
For the rest, Biddy Roonan, the crone, is played
admirably by. J P. O’Neil, on the lines followed
by.the late lamented G. Riggs; and Jefferson
Taite and Mabel Russell give two tolerable
character sketches.

published from a Bathurst (N.S.W.) corres-

A judiciously spent £5O would have saved Young- his

fact a persistent rumor was about on the day of the elec-

backyard to save the barber’s sprat.

It has been discovered in London that the old Greek
■drama is “ intensely modern,” as well as being intensely
ancient and extremely intense. Therefore actor-manager
Martin Harvey, musing on the applause with which
select audiences have greeted Australian Gilbert Murray’s
translations from the Greek, talks of treating the com-
mon or peanut audience to a version of the adventures of
CEdipus, the gentleman (now dead) who answered the
riddle of the Sphinx—a “ competition ” fiend of that
period. But GUdipus had such a very early-Greek time of
it that he will probably need considerable bowdlerising
before he bursts on the vision of England’s sensitive
examiner of plays.
“Ruru” (M.L.) : The goings-on of Albani on the concert
platform fairly astonished Auckland. Compared with
the gam boilings of the elderly soprano, the friskings of
ithe lambs in the fields, or the kitten engaged in a chase
rafter mamma’s velvety tail, were as nought. We are not
•used to royal favorites. The lady bounded on like an
undiarubber ball, grabbing her skirt out of the way
■apparently just in time to prevent her falling over it.
•She ducked and dived and dipped her aigrette to all
.points of the compass. Accompanist Flint endeavored to
•do his duty in leading the lady on, but she always beat
•him by about a yard and a-half, though he ran like a
«wift Israelite bolting from Pharaoh. A local organist
iplayed the organ obligato to “ Crossing the Bar.” After-
wards he scrambled down, and was effacing himself as
'speedily as possible, when he was met in the narrow way
'by Albani leaping on to acknowledge the applause, with
'the accompanist and the violinist leaping after her. Or-
ganist Midgley was grabbed by the exuberant soprano and
rushed along to the front, where he looked astounded
and blushful while the lady shook his hand effusively, and
gesticulated amazingly. Electa Gifford (round with Jean
Gerardy) is the nearest approach we have had to the
Albani manner. But then Electa was youthful, tall and
slim, and her caracolings, while diverting, were not
comical. Some theatrical mimic in search of a novel turn
might make a good one out of the platform manner of
singers we have seen. She could style it “They have
their exits and their entrances.” There would be Melba,
slow of step, impressive, dignified (cavillers talk of cold-
ness, but how would they like to see Melba going on like
Albani ?) representing dignity ; Dolores would stand for
graciousness; Crossley, with her beams and hand-kissing,
would be the exponent of the chummy style ; Arral, with
dancing step, fascinating smile and Frenchy air, is charm ;
■and Albani—well, there would be no mistaking her ; she’
is just frisk. Albani is a stout sunbeam, a well-nourished
blossom dancing in the wind, a butterfly of weight, a
solid zephyr on two feet playing on the grass, or anythin"-
(ike that.
London has for the first time in its history a Yiddish
theatre. Sigmund Ferinman, the Yiddish tragedian,
secured a long lease, the other day, of the Pavilion
Theatre in the Mile-end Road (which is in the -heart of
Fogtown’s Ghetto), and played “The Jewish Hamlet”
last month to a suffering audience of compatriots. (“ The
Jewish Hamlet” is said, by those who have seen it on the
European continent, to be as bad as a Chinese opera, only
about 147 acts shorter.) It is now up to some
Sheeny mime or other to open with “ The Holy Sabbath”
opera at Potts Point.
Printed that Daisy Atherton, who has made such a
success in London in “ The Pocket Miss Hercules,” is a
daughter of Willie Edouin, who, half a century ago, was
the youngest of the three boys of “ The Edouin Family ”
—real name Bryer. The family toured Australia, India
and China with two sisters, Rose (Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis),
and Julia. The other “ boys,” Charlie (who married
Lizzie Naylor, afterwards Mrs. Edouin Bryer)
and John (who married Tilly Earl), are long since
dead. Willie Edouin’s first appearance on the boards
was in 1849 as a youngster, in a play called “ The Living
Marionettes.” He has played, he says, in every part of
the globe, and especially in Australia in the gold fever of
the ’fifties. Then he travelled from camp to camp (or
rush to rush) with his family in a van, on the sides of
which were inscribed in large red letters, “ The Cele-
brated Edouin Family, in Farce, Comedy and Burlesque.”
The performances mostly took place in tents, though
they sometimes transpired in the open air—so Willie told
an interviewer once.
“ G. 8.”: Having paid my 2s. for entrance to the Glaci-
arium and 6d. for the skates and 6d. for the boy who put
them on, I found myself wobbling through space and
clutching an attendant’s arm. My feet flung themselves
out at the back, kicked up in front, wobbled hesitatingly
and then shot out each side. Wobble ! Slide ! Wobble !
I grasped my attendant lovingly. ‘ ‘ The first time you’ve
been on the ice-skates, sir? ” he remarked pleasantly, as I
slithered and bumped and wobbled. I looked at him re-
proachfully. “ Yes,” I confirmed, trying to appear
cheerful, “it is the first time I’ve tried to walk on
knives.” We continued. He was a patient man, and we
scrambled round twice for 4d. “ Don’t leave me,” I
gasped, and then tried to smile courageously. I admired
that man. I admired, even while I envied, his serenity
and coolness on the ’ice. Again I clung to him.
“Don’t leave me,” I prayed; “I’ll have another
bob’s worth.” I loved the man and didn’t want
to let him go, but skating attendants are
as adamant to such clinging affection, and soon I was
left to the mercy of the ice, and the pitilessly critical
smiles of the onlookers. So I joined the great majority
who wriggle round the wall. I wriggled and wriggled,
manfully, strenuously, until I came face to face with a
fellow’-wriggler. His feet were placed one on either side
of mine. I was helpless. We glared at each other;
there is no brotherly love on the ice. “Will you please
move on?” I asked. “I can’t.” “Neither can I. Will
you please move on?” “You’ll have to sit down,” he
said, and added, mournfully, “ It’s so cold on the ice.” I
grabbed a passing attendant. “ Will you kindly help me
to sit down?” And we struggled to a seat. I smiled
ruefully. “It is the first time I have had to be assisted
to sit down.” Then the lookers-on pointed at me and
smiled with good-natured contempt. And 1 sat and
froze. I was dead up to my knees. “ Where are
the hot water pipes?” I asked. “How much must I
pay you to take me to the hot water pipes?” But I
never found them. On the ice the bad skaters and those
who can’t skate at all wriggle round the wall, but the good
skaters go up and down the centre, cutting threes and
figures of eight. Desperately I put my feet together and
shot out into the middle. There I circled round and
round midst the graceful, swaying skaters, making a
beautiful circle—round and round until I stopped dead in
the middle. There I grinned. Then I began again and
ended up each time at the grinning point. The fleeting,
aristocratic skaters who swished up and down the centre
began to look askance at me. An irresistible longing for
home seized me, but I couldn’t get off the ice. I longed
for the bell to ring, for someone to appear and order
everybody off, for the place to catch fire, or the ice to
melt—anything to end my misery. I began to freeze
again, so I shuffled back to the patient wrigglers. At
last the bell did ring. I was overjoyed. I was delighted.
I smiled at the wrigglers and beamed at the lookers-on.
Then I saw a large notice of the appearance whereof the
bell was only the herald : “ Everybody please to reverse.”
Sadly I began to wriggle round the other way.
“ F. 8.” : Joy Butt has placed her infant slipper in it.
While Mumsey was artlessly telling the reporter of one
great daily that Joy, cetat 6, always wept passionately
at the sound of the deep maternal boom, Joy was being
catechised by the representative of the other great daily
as thus : —“Do you ever hear mother sing?” “Often,”
replied Joy, happily. “I love to hear mother sing.”
What happened to Joy when these two totally different
statements confronted the agonised family at the break-
fast table next morning has not yet been given to the
press.
Edward Savral, the colored cakewalk pugilist, writes in
passionate denial of a Poverty Point rumor that he had
found religion. Edward, according to himself, plays
poker, German whist, 100, and two-up, and shakes the
dice when and wherever possible ; also he makes a book
on the Metrop. and such-like important events, and offers
doubles to the ultra-speculative. Between whiles he
writes torrid love songs (but no hymns), and in other
ways eschews the cold, hard, flat life. In fact, this par-
ticular coon is a perfect devil of a fellow, and doesn’t jud.
Gabled during the week that Jean Gerardy, the famous
’cellist, had married Miss Amelia McQuade, of Sydney,
the eldest daughter of the late Mr. Harry McQuade, of
“ Bomera,” Potts Point, and Mrs. Cecilie McQuade. The
bride’s mother used to do things on the stage. She was
introduced here by Miss Emily Soldene, who brought an
opera bouffe company to Sydney in 1877, with Miss Cecily
Durant as one of its members. The large and generous
lady mentions her in her “Recollections,” published in
1897, as a notable beauty.
“ D.” : A quaint absurdity of “ The Midnight Wedding,”
now nightly celebrating at Sydney Royal, is the punch-
ing business in Astrea’s bedchamber (Scene IV., Act II).
Here Paul Valmar (hero)set about the wicked Von Strels-
burg in a style reminiscent of L. Foley at his best.
But on the European continent (and that, presumably, is
where the action of all these “ uniform ” plays is laid) an
officer would not dream of using his fists on another’s
face. That is, in Continental eyes, a barbarous and savage
practice ; and a Court of Honor would make short work
of the male of the Junker class who attempted such a
thing. The conception of a couple of German or Austrian
militaires dealing it one to each other with bare knuckles
is rather more anomalous and impossible than that of
Peter Felix and Snowy Baker (say) meeting on Bondi
sands to exchange a couple of pistol shots at daybreak.
“ Billy T. South-street, Ballarat, threatens to develop
a plague for which poisoned baits will have to be laid in
the near future. The secretary declares that over a ton
of letters have passed through the post-offlee in connec-
tion with the present competitions ; £2OOO has been paid
for special trains ; and 5000 competitors have come before
the judges. Five thousand competitors; 5000 reciters,
singers, musicians, and immature actors! What an
army ! And what an instrument for repelling invasion
those 5000 desperate performers would make in the hands
of a great commander—-a man with original ideas, capable
of drawing the invaders’ fire with a sortie of reciters,
while attacking the right wing with brass bands, and
reciting “ The Deck Stood on the Burning Boy ” in strong
force to his left wing. But what troubles me is the fear
that if South-street continues the rest of us will cease to
think Australia worth defending.
“ Wexie ” : I happened to-day on a faded programme of
a benefit given to one-time Shakspearian tragedian (sic)
Bob Fuller, which took place at the old Royal Standard
about 12 years ago. Bob was supported by Clara
Stephenson, Charley Burford, and one or two raw (very
raw) amateurs. The affair, I remember, was regarded as
a good-natured joke by the profesh. The ghost scene
from “ Hamlet” was put on first, with Bob in the name
part, and Charley as his trusty pal, Horatio. The
ghost was amateur—quite amateur—and after a delay of
fully two minutes he came on at the wrong side, and
hovered anxiously about the coat-tails of Hamlet and
Horatio, trying hard to attract their attention. The
cold, bitter way in which he was told (after he had stum-
bled over something, and attracted Hamlet’s attention by
falling down) to go.round just as fast as he could gallop
and come on by the right entrance, was worth hearing.
Hamlet’s wig dropped off when he fell on his knees, but
that didn’t matter much. Later, Bob appeared as
Othello, and a very good and prim and proper young
Sunday-school elocutionist was Desdemona. She didn’t
understand stage realism worth a cent, and was so much
offended at the rude manner in which she was
asked for the handkerchief that she left the stage in high
dudgeon. Still ic was a fairly profitable benefit.
Both the old central figures have since passed out, and
Bob’s exit was dramatic. One Saturday night, a year or
so after the benefit, he had reached home from Paddy’s
Market (where he retailed doubtful pastry and sugar
ware), and was entertaining a select circle of friends over
a snack and “something.” Bob liked a drop of “some-
thing.” He was giving Mark Antony’s funeral oration,
and had reached the line : “My heart is in the coffin
therewith Caesar!” when his cronies were fairly dazzled
by his extra good form. He clutched his left breast con-
vincingly ; and the applause became uproarious when he
staggered towards where the supposed coffin was ; but it
died away into astonished silence when he fell, and hit
the floor hard. It couldn’t be the “something,” for Bob
had hardly started. He didn’t move, and his friends
leaped from the snack and lifted him up. Poor old Bob
needed a real coffin.

“ Brolga Bill ”: I don’t know which to admire most—
the contortions of Clara, the beauty of Butt, the mag-
nificence of Madam, or the splendid singing of Mrs. Rum-
ford. How different things would have been if Musgrove’s
German opera slingers had been able to bend at the
middle like the spacious gracious contralto ! By the way,
is she really a contralto ? The average critic will not be
game to give his real opinion ; he will form in line and
adulate with the rest of the importers’ papers. The lower
notes are good and serviceable ; but there is nothing of
the grand tone-poem about them. lam not a lover of
cavernous vocalism; but Ido expect the custodian of the
Voice of the Century to roll out some rich deep notes that
have the real, heart-catching contralto color.
. Harry : Can anyone say Why such dramatic effu"
sions as the notorious “ Uhlan Earts,”and the still more
notorious Fatal Wedding” are described as plays of
human interest ”? I had two six-penn’orths of “ Human
Hearts the other night, and I tried hard to discover
which character represented the “human interest.” It
certainly wasn’t the impossible village- blacksmith, who
resembled a muscular local preacher. It wasn’t the tramp
with the dislike for soap. It wasn’t the inhuman tart
who is described in the bills as being the possessor of a
Black Heart. It wasn’t the aristocratic villain, a cross
between Ned Kelly and a tailor’s model. It wasn’t other
people, too humorous to mention. So it must have been
either the good Salv’army sort of young lady who was
tru-u-ue, the idiot brother (by the way, which brother
was the idiot ?), or the cheeild. I think it must have been
the cheeild, for this infant prodigy accomplished the re-
formation of the tramp, touched the hearts of brutal
prison-warders, and won the affection of its ’ard-’earted
mother. Yes, the kid must have been the “ human in*
terest ” which caused the weeping of the gods.
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : I suppose it’s heresy, but I must
say it. Clara Butt’s voice seems to me to be, not the
contralto of the century—hardly a contralto at all—but
rather a most magnificent mezzo-soprano, lacking the
full organ-like lower notes that distinguish great con-
tralti. On Saturday night the voice showed a splendid
middle register and a lovely high register under perfect
control; but the low notes were disappointing—so much
so that I expected to read in the dailies that the famous
mother and singer was affected vocally by our sudden
Sydney climate. Patey and Crossley used to roll out
glorious golden notes below the staff that thrilled one
through and through. I have had to readjust my ideas
about C. Butt. Her voice is not so mighty as I was led
to expect, and her vocal method is much more artistic
than I bargained for. I maintain that C.B. is a magnifi-
cent mezzo—not a contralto. As a matter of .surprising
fact, her husband’s singing touched me quite as much as
her own fine efforts. His enunciation equals Philip New-
bury’s clean-cut delivery.

“ Hiphen ” : What’s the matter with the one and only
Bland Holt, anyhow ? Seems as if he isn’t the grand colt
he was. Paid him 3s. worth of visit this week, and found
him sadly shaky on his lines. It seemed much a matter
of conjecture with him as to what came next. He finished
up many bad breaks by remarking in the last act to the
Hook (there’s always a dook or two now—Bland’s getting
very “class”): “When your son Alice marries my
daughter Wilfred ” and corrected himself, when the gods
had ceased to smile, by: “ When my daughter Wilfred
marries your son Alice.” However, h'is hand has not lost
its cunning in the matter of staging. The great race
between the train and the motor-car w T as intensely realistic
and calculated to deceive a V.R.C. steward. The only
thing I objected to was that Blandolt seemed to think he
was riding to win on a bike. He bumped about on the seat
excitedly while his daughter Alice (who was Miss Ross to
those in the know) sat back calmly and watched the
scenery flash by as if she were a returned prima-donna
toot-tooting around her native landscape in a million-
guinea muddy bloater car. However, even if Bland for-
got all the lines he ever knew, or tried to say them all at
once, he’d still be worth going to see.

“ Shall we call this city the New York of
the Southern Hemisphere?” asked a pamphlet-
writing member of the recent Christian Endeavor
Conference in Melbourne. “ Shall we refer to it
as the Athens of the South?” Well, the idea
may seem absurd, but why not just call it Mel-
bourne. If a man’s name is Smith, he just calls
himself Smith as a rule. Rarely does he disguise
himself as “the new Jones of the Southern
Hemisphere,” or “the Wilkins of the South,” and
a city might as well follow the good example.
Justice Cohen, Justice O’Connor and
Justice Hoo'd, as Presidents of various descrip-
tions of Industrial Courts, all vacated their seats
without much ceremony. There was considerable
difficulty in inducing a Supreme Court Judge to
accept the N.S. W. position in the first instance,
and at present it is a District Court Judge who,
by a shuffle with the Arbitration Act, fills the
vacancy. A Judge, accustomed to the deferences
of the ordinary courts, finds an Arbitration Court
somewhat uncongenial. Then comment on de-
cisions is free and hearty, and his old sacrosanct
character is knocked about till it looks like a
bashed junior football.
The suggestion of the daily press to place
British Labor man Keir Hardie under police sur-
veillance when he arrives in Australia is supremely
silly. The only thing that Hardie wants is
to be shut out as an undesirable and horrible im-
migrant. But, unless the suggestion is repro-
bated, some Chief Secretary or other temporary
numskull may perpetrate the folly. When, during
the big_ strike, Labor man Thomas, of Broken
Hill, paid a visit to Queensland, Horace Tozer,
then Ministerial head of the police, ordered large,
conspicuous policemen to lumber ■ after him
wherever he went. And the worst they found
out about him was that he was a Methodist local
preacher who didn’t drink and hated tobacco.
Dr. Robert Scott, who recently died at
Summer Hill (Sydney), was a well-known figure
in Melbourne parsondoin some years'back. He
was an M. A. of Glasgow University, and
was appointed about 1880 to Chalmers’ Presby-
terian Church, East Melbourne, then one of the
most important “charges ”in those parts. Seven
years later he “got into holts” with his congrega-
tion about some church property, and threw up
not only his church but the whole sin-shifting
industry. Then though he was in sight of his half-
century, he went to Edinburgh to study medicine.
Having got his “ticket,” he returned in 1890, and
practised at Balmain and Redfern (N.S.W.). He
was aged 65 when he passed out.
There was a howl in Brisbane the other
day because “the Empire Cup,” won by a loc:il
Grammar School boy for an essay, was detained
for a few hours by the Customs, while inquiries
were made about it. The petty little Brisbane
Courier promptly jumped at the chance to sneer-
ingly ask the returning Queensland rifleman
Motton, winner of the Graphic Cup, whether he
intended paying duty on his trophy. It was a
very cheap sneer at the new tariff, though—a cheap
and ignorant sneer. For if the anti-Australian
Courier had read the thing it sneers at, it would
have known that “trophies won abroad” are
expressly exempt from duty.
Prahran (Yic.) Council has got itself dis-
liked by the contractors, who have hitherto been
doing the street cleaning, watering, etc., at
Bs. lOd. a day for a man. horse and diay. Hitherto
the council has obliged the contractors to make
sworn declaration that the men employed were
getting the minimum wage of 7s. a day, and the
contractors have always cheerfully sworn to that
effect. But lately the city surveyor smelt some-
thing, and got authority to himself pay the men
the 7s. a day and deduct the amount out of the
contract. And evidently the city surveyor’s nose
hadn’t deceived him, for the contractors there-
upon promptly threw up their undertaking and
forfeited their deposits.
The boycott by which the Melbourne
bakers won their strike was frenziedly condemned
by the Argus as an “immoral and wicked
weapon.” Two days later the same paper joy-
fully printed this letter from an alleged share-
holder in the Civil Service Co-operative Society,
which had sided with the men :
I have resolved for my part to have nothing to do with
the store for the future. . . I may mention that I am
in a position to secure for the store the custom of a large
institution, which would have been of considerable value
to the shareholders; but the store shall never have another
order from me.
Now, where’s the difference? Isn’t this also a
boycott ?
At this season of the year General Desire
is endeavoring to circumvent Ikey Mo. The old
warrior naturally consults an instructive beverage
for the purpose, and after a judicious quantity of
D.C.L. the genial imbiber usually forms a wise
opinion as to the respective merits of the Cup
horses, and if he doesn’t back the actual winner,
he gets very near it. The D.C.L. line of reasoning
goes straight to the point. There are other
whiskies, of course, but this is the Whisky of the
Wise.*
From an old officer :
A late cable message tells how the Germans on the
r rench border have “ recast the plans of their fortresses,
because the old plans have been sold to the French.”
The Germans have surely taken a long time to And that
out—or the cable correspondent has. I have in my pos-
session a copy of the “Manuel de Fortification,” by
Plessix and Legendre, used as a text book in the French
Staff College. It contains elaborate plans of the fortifi-
cations of Metz, Strasburg, and Neu Breisach.- It even
gives detailed plans of some of the detached forts, and
has been in circulation for several years. The same book
contains plans of the fortifications of Portsmouth
(England) and Antwerp (Belgium), showing what non-
sense it is to tr.v to make a mystery of the general con-
struction of fortresses. Anyone who understands the
business can tell the general plan and construction of a
place by looking at it without making a drawing on the
ground.
A LONG RUN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'He came from his provincial tour,
The actor-author splendid ;
His thirst that he could scarce endure,
All earthly things transcended.
He leaned, a m*st dejected man,
And waited the Samaritan.
A kind comedian bade him try
A taste of local liquor.
No spider springing on a fly
Closed on the quarry quicker.
<OOl ora the trams they heard him crunch
The fragments of the counter-lunch.
You had a long run with your play
Outback ? ” the other queried.
“ A very long run, I may say,
It left me rather wearied.”
The author lapsed in gloomy thought—
“ Thank God,” he said, “ I wasn’t caught! ”
VIC.
SILAS SNELL.
PROGRESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The spirit of a new age has touched and changed the
English village life. —So we hear.
Progress has bumped the villages,
And changed their quiet ways.
They’re hustled from the simple life—
These are progressive days.
The village inn, whose landlord once
Was known good beer to sell,
Has gone ; and in its place there stands
The Mudtown Grand Hotel.
The village choir, so simple once,
Now knows the care and fret
And constant bickerings that stir
The highly-paid quartette.
The creamy milk, that once we got
When tramping through the vale,
Less creamy is, and not so fresh,
The water makes it pale.
The daffodils, that once bedecked
The country-side with gold,
Don’t do it now. They’re all packed up
In bunches, to be sold.
No longer on the village green
Is dance and gay hubbub,
But in the city hall they hold
“ The Pansy Quadrille Club.”
The harvest home, that once did show
The farmer kind and just,
Is held no more—because the crop
Is bought up by the Trust.
Under the spreading - chestnut tree
The smithy stands no more ;
A brand-new motor shop is there,
With “ Garage ” o’er the door.
It’s meet that it should happen thus ;
These are progressive days ;
If simple country life you’d see—
You GO AND WATCH TUB PLAYS.
TIC.
W. L. SLACK.

AT THE GIPSY TEA.
EDWIN: “ Tell that man to go away at once.”
ANGLINA: “ Tell him yourself; he’s a perfect stranger to me.”
Police Magistrate Cresswell, of Melbourne,
fell on a ruffianly wife-beater lately with “one
month’s hard labor, with the second seven days
in solitary confinement.” It was such a shock
after the anticipated “10s. or three days,” that
the damaged wife didn’t recover her wits till the
court was just about to close. Then she earnestly
informed Cresswell that he was locking up
the sole support of herself and two babies.
And, as a matter of fact, that is just how
it stood ; the brutal wife-beater was going
to get shelter and food for four weeks, and
the woman, who had suffered too much already,
was going to drift away and starve. Still,
as the other magistrates had gone, Cresswell
couldn’t alter the sentence; and the best
he could do was to award the woman 10s.
a week out of the poor box. And even if
the other magistrates hadn’t gone, the law
wouldn’t have provided for the sensible thing—to
keep the husband hard at profitable work, and to
pay the wife the proceeds. It is a fool of a sys-
tem that condemns the offender to idleness and
the sufferer to hunger.

She flashed her beseeching orbs on the Bench, and

The Law in Victoria has just been intro-
duced to an unusual sort of “crime”-—viz., the
forging of a petition for a prisoner’s release. A
few years back one Quigley, having grossly
broken the law in the matter of a female school-
teacher, a perfect stranger, was ordered to be
hanged. The executive, however, altered this to
10 years’ “ hard.” Since Quigley’s incarceration,
petitions for release have drifted into the Crown
Law Office at the rate of about two a year. The
department proving obdurate, a fresh petition
arrived the other day bearing the school-teacher
victim's name, and setting out that, as “she was
quite satisfied with the punishment the prisoner
had received, it would do no harm to release
him.” The department maintained its obdurate
attitude, and notified the young woman to that
effect, whereupon she promptly replied that she
didn’t knojv what the fuss was about—she had
made no appeal, and, in fact, wasn’t in the least
interested in the prisoner. Then the department
inquired, and discovered that the sender of the
bogus petition was a 13-year-old niece of the
prisoner, who seems to have an idea that her
uncle, if released, will assist a very straitly-
circumstanced family to keep its head above
water. The Law is understood to be considering
whether it will prosecute the 13-year-old niece or
not; but it is safe to bet that it won’t. Those
petitions for release are a very delicate subject,
and a searching enquiry into such matters would
be very awkward for some people.

The Bar engaged to “ explain ” the will of
“ Tattersall ” Adams recalls the celebrated New-
town ejectment case, Devine v. several persons.
In it every man of note in the practising N.S.W.
Bar was engaged. In the Adams case, there are
five silk gowns and nine “ stuffs,” besides a whole
army of solicitors, and the carcase can’t be seen
for crows.
N.S.W. Speaker McCourt had many
bouquets thrown at him on his re-election,
Wade, in his undertaker’s fashion, wreathed the
Speaker with immortelles and cypress, and hung
rue upon him, and said how sorry they all were
that McCourt was dead. At least that was the
effect of it; the thud of the clod of eulogy upon
the coffin of McCourt was audible all down
Maequarie-street. Then Wade bogged in at
carving McCourt’s tombstone, and fixing up his
epitaph, and finally he drove the empty hearse
home weeping bitterly as he went. Then McGowen
had a turn at McCourt, and left people thinking
that he and Mac. had made boilers together in their
younger days. And finally Storey leaped to the
front as the tame wallaby leaps on the dining table
when dinner is just laid—onb could hear Storey's
tail getting into the soup —and smote his chest,
and said that the Speaker had crept into their
hearts, and that if he were only half as large as
their opinion of him all the hats in Storey’s
hattery rolled into one hat would merely balance
on one hair of McCourt’s head ; and he said they
would all rally round McCourt, and would die for
him if necessary (or, even if it wasn’t necessary,
they would do it for ornament), same as the
Scottish spearmen at Flodden, or that almost-
forgotten Austrian who rose up with 800 militia
and told a Sultan and 150,000 men to come on,
and added that the Sultan’s parents weren’t
married. It was a great time ; and if McCourt
can ever borrow from the House even 2s. in the £
of what the House seemed ready to lend him last
week, he will never want for some beer and a crust.

Australian Chief-Justice Griffith has been
talking some good horsehair sense on his native
Brisbanelieath. A man had been charged with doing
something in Mel bourne-street, and it happened
that the place where he did it wasn’t Melbourne-
street, that street having stopped being Mel-
bourne-street a few yards back and become
Victoria-place; whereupon the magistrates had
observed a fatal defect in the proceedings, and
acted accordingly. Apparently, also, other
Courts had applauded the magistrates’ keen
faculty for standing a hair on end and splitting
it, and then judging the hog by the hair that they
had found hanging to the beast. When Chief-
Justice Griffith considered the hog and the hair
relatively, he turned up section 48 of the Justices
Act—
No objection shall be taken or allowed to any com-
plaint ... for any alleged defect therein in substance or in
form, or for any variance between it and the evidence in
support thereof, and any such variance shall be amended
by order of the Justices at the hearing.
And a very disgusted C. J. asked what in thunder
magistrates thought Parliament passed sections
like that for if it wasn’t to prevent them making
asses of themselves, as they had done in this case.

* * * *
Adelaide is a little puzzled over the case of
Emil Benda, a popular little Frenchman, and, till
lately, a leading tobacconist. Three years ago,
bad times coming upon him, he put a bullet into
his head, and it stayed there, though without any
lasting result (apparently). However, he has now
been arrested by a compatriot who had discounted
for him several hundreds of pounds’ worth of bills
bearing the names of prominent merchants. All
of them were wild and reckless forgeries. Benda
has been sent for six years’ hard labor. Certainly
he ought to be under restraint, but the quarries of
the Stockade hardly seem the proper place
treatment of such a case. His imitations of the
signatures of well-known citizens looked about as
much like a serious attempt at the real thing as
George Lauri’s famous imitation of Goliath of
Gath.
» « * *
Adelaide is going to have the chance of
seeing its beautiful Botanic Gardens by moonlight
this coming Saturday—for the first time. For
just half a century they have been open every day
and all day, but closed at dusk; now a free
promenade concert is to celebrate the jubilee, and
if the citizens behave themselves well they may
get the run of the gardens for the future.

What though the Jappy settler is outside Australia’s

Motch mumbles in his bean-curds, with his solemn,

Turned upward—guileless child of New Japan !

At yellow skins, and bellows to his brother-whites to

Who holds along Australian coasts her cheap, corrupting
court;
Here are the tracks of dear, maligned Japan !
Sure, bitterness and hate should go with good and solid
fear
Of this—in outward seeming like a common sort o’ man—
This thing with sleepy eyes, that cloaks with Occident
veneer
The monkey-hide of savage Old Japan !

THE POEMS OP BROTHER TADGER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear B.—Rev. E. H. Sugden, M. A., one-time
henchman to the now-exploded Berruther Woggle,
has written “ a hymn of welcome to the delegates
to the ninth biennial Australasian Christian
Endeavor Convention.” Sugden is a humorous,
little pink chap, who wouldn’t la'll a fly except
with bis mouth, yet, as usual, he waves his little
legs in the face of the sun an : welcomes the
delegates with this —
Send a note of challenge
To the hosts of Hell,
Louder and yet louder
Let the trumpet swell,
Uttering our defiance
Of their utmost might;
With their listed legions
To the death we’ll fight.
Great poet is Sugden, and so indefinite that even
if he and his Convention do nothing, the pillars
of the Church who want Christian Endeavor as
much as anybody will find no offence. I think
I’ll enter for the second prize myself—
Onward ! Brother Tadger !
Shriek and tear your hair 1
Alter the Equator
With a ton of prayer !
Utter your defiance
To the Thing Accurst !
Hit the caterpillar—
If it strike you first!
Fight no vested interests,
For they fill the plate ;
Make no shocking charges,
’Gainst|,he callous great!
Utter y.dur defiance —
Dauntless everywhere—
Of the lame blackbeetle—
If he’s small and spare.
Rise up in your might, then,
’Gainst the “ Hosts of Hell.”
Specify no party—
Myths will do as well !
Fight them to a finish !
Let their graves be dug !
Onward, Brother Tadger !
Kill the friendless bug !

Sweater ! Merchant! Churchman !
/ In one man exist!
Crush the friendless sinner—
’Ware the ma.led fist !
Onward, Brother Tadger !
Cause small wrongs to die—
’Ware the wicked lion !
Kill the wicked fly !

“D.M.W.”: The Dominion newspaper
got out in Wellington on Dominion Day. A
lino, blew out on the night before, and later on
the machine started chewing up paper in great
style. However, the print got out by noon, and
a motor-car was engaged to deliver it to agents
round the town. Out of pure cussedness the
motor broke down and wouldn’t go. The fact is
that the stars in their courses fight against the
establishment of a Tory paper in Maoriland. All
the same, the literary staff of the concern is doing
well, and nothing very Tory has yet shown itself.
Maoriland could do with a newspaper. It has an
astonishing number of things printed, but——

“ J.G.” : Magdalene and her sisters Tottie
and Flossie are drifting hack to their old homes in
■“Lon ” and “Little Lon,” in Melbourne, and the
people who were responsible for the breaking up
of the hilarious family are not making any fresh
move towards dispersion. They begin to realise
that it is better to allow the Ten Tribes of Tottie
to congregate in one recognised quarter of the
city than to have them scattered broadcast over
the whole State. Tregear, Woodfull, and the
rest need not enter the tainted quarter unless
they like, and no harm will be done to their
morals if they carefully avoid the dangerous
locality. But when a fool beak like Panton
scatters the wicked all over the place, it is always
possible that Tregear or Woodfull, going to appa-
rently. respectable houses, in most respectable
localities, to deliver tracts and L.O.L. literature,
may happen on Flossie, and be hugged in de-
rision before he can tear himself from her warm,
plump arms, and flee for the gate, calling wildly
tfor the police and the fire-engine to come and
arrest and extinguish Flossie.
* * * *
A good many days have passed since the
last bad accident on Tait’s Victorian railways,
and the cause thereof still remains a mystery. In
a general way, however, people set it down in
part to over-lavish economy in the matter of
repairs. The rest of the responsibility is laid to
the fact that the driver was new, and didn’t know
the road, and took a down-grade at too great a
speed. Anyhow, an engine and 19 trucks fled in
a great comprehensive 30ft. leap into a creek,
killing the driver and guard under circumstances
of which the less said the better. The Argus
remarks that “the spot where the fatality
occurred is a dangerous one, and travellers on the
hue have often expressed doubts about it.” Tra-
vellers are still expressing doubts, and even the
bulkiness of the beautiful surplus doesn’t make
these doubts any less. It takes a wonderful
number of bad spots to make a surplus sometimes.
* * * *
“Maori Mac”: The Maoriland police—
except when in pursuit of a large pewter pot—are
about the slowest things outside the Great Tor-
toise Handicap. Their casualness is a subject of
wonder. In 1891 a man named Coker was sum-
moned to appear at the Christchurch court to
answer a charge of child desertion. Owing to
pressure of business, or some other cause, Coker
did not appear ; but, having mooned around for a
bit, he drifted out of the Cathedral township.
Apparently the police did nothing but wonder
vaguely re the whereabouts of their lost friend.
Coker brought up at Wanganui, where he engaged
in business with some success. Sixteen years
after the summons, he returned to Christchurch.
Somebody with a long memory recognised him,
and informed one of the local Sherlock Holmes
push, and Sherlock quickly found quite a number
of clues. So Coker was arrested and ordered
six months’ imprisonment, which filled him with
■such wild surprise that he had hardly begun to
protest before he found that he had started serv-
ing his sentence.

“ Flinderino ” : A “ Woman’s Letter ” (B.
12/9/’O7), in a paragraph about artist Streeton and
picture-buyers, scoffs at Fisherman’s Bend as a
Melbourne beauty spot. Let me state right here
that Fisherman’s Bend is a beauty spot. But
nine out of every, ten persons in Melbourne do
not know that such a place exists. This little
fishing village nestles behind clumps of tea-tree
and hillocks of sand and marram grass, on
the shore of Hobson’s Bay, between the
Port Melbourne piers and the entrance
to the Yarra. There are no streets —
just a collection of fishermen’s
cottages scattered promiscuously
on the beach in picturesque con-
fusion. The fishing nets hanging
along the front fences, and the
schooner - rigged clothes - lines,
suggest Yarmouth beach with
old Mr. Peggotty’s boathouse
in the distance. Any fine
morning one can stroll along
the shingle and watch the
brine-hardened toilers hauling
in the nets, or yarn with old
Crayfish Dan as he tars the
upturned Saucy Jane, and, at the same time,
listen to the melodious voice of the Australian
skylark ; for at no other place is our lark, more
plentiful or more melodious than at Fisherman’s
Bend.

“Jimmie Pannikin”: Some time ago I
applied to the council of the Public Service Asso
ciation to send me a box of magazines for a lonely
little bush school. My request was granted ; but
one mernber of the said council made a song about
a wee slice of slang, that put life and color into
my letter, written, by the way, at racing speed.
I sent the peculiar purist the following remarks
concerning slang, by H. A. Beers, Professor of
English Literature in Yale College (U.S.A.), but
received no reply. Please print the vigorous and
right remarks for the benefit of other thin-
blooded tinklers :
Schoolmasters’ English—the English which boys writ-
ing themes are taught to use—is carefully denuded of all
peculiarities, and among other things which it holds in
horror is slang. Pedants, prigs, purists, precisians, and
all dry-witted and thin-witted persons naturally hate
slang, because it is alive. But men of rich natures love
slang. It is the wild game of language. It abounds in
imagination, humor, strength, comes warm from the lips
of the people, and is the fresh product of the creative
impulses by which all language was originally made.
“Kaban ” : How is it that the authorities
who exercise control over suburban stables
and hen-houses don’t visit the 'establishment of
E. W. Cole in Bourke-street, Melbourne? Cole’s
second-hand department is practically the only
place of its kind in the City, atari those who love
to fossick among old books itaust perforce ascend
his winding stair. Now the smell of musty
volumes is Unpleasant enough by itself, but why
should the perfume and whiff of monkeys be
thrown in ? The Cole menagerie opens off the
stairway, and the result is disheartening, to say
the least. The monkey may smell pleasantly
enough in his native forest, but monkey in a shop
is unwholesome and an abomination. The
brother whose tail hangs down behind should be
chased outside the city limits.

The Kedron (Q.) Shire Council got out
of the rut lately when it was devising its new
by-laws, and provided for a £5 tax on pea-rifles.
But Premier Kidston looked first at the long list
of pea-rifle fatalities, and then turned his thoughts
north, where Australia’s trouble threatens to
come from, and sorrowfully took a blue pencil and
pushed the shire council back into the rut. So
Kedron won’t have its by-law after all. A pea-
rifle may not kill at 2000yds.; but it will probably
lead the youngster to presently handle a rifle that
can do so if it is held straight.

The sailing ship Port Jackson, which last
year brought out from England 100 boys who
were learning to be officers in the Bull marine, has
started on another trip. But though billets were
found, or promised, for the whole of the former
lot of marine students, the Port Jackson, on this
voyage, musters less than 50 cadets. Public sup-
port is scarce, and Bull, junior, is still quitting
the sea. The a venues of promotion are narrow in a
ship officer’s life, the pay is small and the life is hard.
A boy cannot get a master’s ticket until he is 21;
as a matter of fact, few can qualify for it until
about 25. Almost every big passenger line, and
a good few of the best tramp lines, refuse to give
a man a berth even as second mate unless he
holds a master’s ticket, and has served three or
four years in sailing vessels. Lately the com-
panies have been perhaps a shade less ferocious in
their insistence upon previous service in wind-
jammers, but the relief hasn’t amounted to much.
Then the pay of a second or first mate, even of a
P. and O. liner, is not worth the slavery involved
in rising to that giddy eminence. And by reason
of these things a famine in brass-bounders seems
to be quite within sight.

“ Sail ” : Was quite surprised, the Othet
day, to see in a paper that several Sydney
firms had been fined for allowing srhoke to issue
from their chimneys fob htore than a certain
number of minutes. One restaurant-keeper was
fined £2 for permitting thick smoke and soot to
pour from his funnel. Why, I have often noticed
soot coming into the room where I stoke-up at
midday, and settling in black masses on the plates
and tablecloth, and it always seemed the right
thing to expect. The waiter just flicked the dirt
off with his napkin, and remarked that “they’re
pretty bad to-day, sir.” There seems to be no
end to interference with private industry these
times. The Government will overdo it yet.
“ 10-2-4 ” (Sydney) writes :
Was favored last week with a view of the new
beach-bathing garment. The dress is neat without being
gaudy ; and to say merely that it is going to be efficient
from a moral standpoint is to state the case with miser-
able inadequacy. G. Reid could sun-bathe in this costume
and not look indecent. It is constructed throughout of some
strong, rugged serge-like substance. The entire body of the
swimmer will be enveloped, barring the head, face, hands
and feet. (Side-stunts which are earnestly recommended
—though they are not compulsory—are list boots reach-
ing well up the calf, and a woven eye-holed headpiece
having the appearance of the cap worn by the person who
is about to be obliterated by the last officer of the law.)
The would-be bather climbs into his new acquisition at
the back, and it is then fastened from behind by four
large bone buttons like dinner plates. There is also an
emergency lacing apparatus in case one or more of the
dinner-plates should come off. Surmounting all is the
triumphant and distinguishing mark of the costume, the
product of the combined intellects of the local coun-
cillors, assisted by the Mayor. A giant lampshade
a ballet dancer’s skirt, B. Smith’s frock-coat, a tariff
wall, all these things it suggests ; but none of them long.
It is in its own class. There is nothing else quite like it
on earth ; nor ever will be. In pleats and folds it droops
from the bather’s tummy, around which organ it is lashed,
to far below his knees, making of him a human calamity
that an African tree-dweller would heave bricks at and a
Jud weep joyous tears over. The new bathing garb is
great. It is unique. Someone ought to be drowned
in connection with it.

Says Dr. E. M. Inglis, health officer of
Kew (Vic.) municipality :
To my mind, the most likely cause of the continuance
of typhoid in Kew Asylum is the asylum rule or custom
which makes the patients use the dormitories as eating
rooms.
We should think ! If Thomas Bent was required
by law to have his breakfast in his bedroom as a
regular thing he would probably resign the
Premiership in disgust. And many a decent
lunatic doubtless feels inclined to resign his job
as an idiot and an insane madman for the same
reason.
Queensland, which includes so much of
that north country “where the white man can’t
live,” again came out top of the Australian death-
rate list last year. Of every 10,000 Victorians
124 provided another little job for the undertaker;
in N. S. Wales, 99 ; in Queensland, only 95. So
the further north you got the further you edged
from the grave. The grave lies down south. The
death rates of the cities tell the same tale.
Hobart, the southernmost city, has by far the
worst record among the capitals—l 73 per 10,000,
as against Brisbane’s 100. Unless the officials are
most outrageous liars, it is a lot easier to live in
the north than it is in the south.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
According to American reckoning — and the
U.S. Mint is probably the first; authority on the subject—
the world’s gold output last year was valued at
£82,292,149. In 1896 it was £41,713,715, so in 10 years
the output has all-but doubled. If there is any substance
at all in the theory on which the bimetallists built their
pretty castles a few years ago, this remarkable increase
in gold-production must affect prices in the way of send-
ing them up and keeping them up. In other words, the
phenomenal increase in the stock of gold must cheapen
that metal, which means that the world must give more
of it for a bale of wool or a ton of copper. To that extent
the average of old prices—say for copper, lead, or wool —
isn’t a reliable guide to probable future prices.
There has been a remarkable increase in prices,
anyway and however caused. Take as a basis the average
London prices, in 1901, f.o.b. Sydney, for the principal
Australian articles of export. CaA that basis 1000. Then,
according to the calculations of the N.S.W. Bureau of
Statistics, the average for last year was 1277; and
for August, 1907, it was 1313. Pastoral products have
advanced rather less and mineral products rather more
than the average. An advance like this plays chiefly into
the hands of adebtor country like Australia. On account
of the increase in prices of its articles of export, Australia
could now repay £1,000,000 of its debt as easily asin 1901 it
could have repaid £761,000 of it. That is one tremendous
reason why the present run of very high prices should be
seized upon to get out of the hands of the foreign creditor ;
with one ton of lead, for instance, Australia can, if it
seizes the opportunity, pay off as much debt as a few
years ago it could have paid off with two tons—and quite
likely as much as it will be able to pay off with two tons
in the future.
Even if the debt is converted from foreign into
Australian, something considerable is gained ; and in that
respect Victoria has been doing very well. Since Federa-
tion, its foreign liability has been decreased by £5,017,544;
the Melbourne liability has been increased by £8,050,258.
In other words, £3,032,714 of new debt has been incurred
and taken up in Melbourne, and £5,017,544 of old debt
has been bought from J. Bull Oohen and transferred to
Australian creditors.
* * * *
As the N. S. Wales Government has really to
pay only one-fourth (or less than one-fourth) of the duty
on the imported wire netting (it pays the four-fourths,
but the Australian Government hands back three-fourths
or more), State Ministers have decided only to charge the
farmers the one-fourth. Carruthers might have done
that from the start, but it didn’t suit his book ; he
wouldn’t then have been able, for election purposes, to
weep over the “ poor farmer,” and rave against the Aus-
tralian Government. But the most interesting feature
about the matter is the way the daily papers, which
helped Carruthers to rave, take the new decision.
“No merchant holding wire netting could do like-
wise, and in that way they are penalised,” says
the Sydney Telegraph. And it remarks that things
like this “ would shut up the importers,” where-
fore the whole business is abominable, and “ will not bear
looking into.” And this is the farmers’ friend! The
other day it nearly had hysterics because the “poor
farmer ” had to pay duty on wire-netting ; to-day, when
the Government says that he needn’t pay three-fourths
of the duty, it has hysterics again, and demands that he
should pay the whole duty lest some blessed importer
should suffer tuppence. It is the importer that really
matters; the “poor farmer” —well, he’s just good
enough to use as a tool for election purposes.
Now it is Portugal whose Government has
tired of seeing the country drained by foreign insurance
cos., without even getting from them any guarantee
that they will pay up if a disaster should strike them and
the country ; wherefore anew law' requires (1) a sub-
stantial deposit from every co. ; (2) a share in the profits.
At latest 33 cos., mostly English and American, were
filling Lisbon with their buzz, and assuring the Prime
Minister that the time was not ripe. Time seems every-
where to have that old habit of keeping perpetually green.
The Westralian Government has been roped in
for a loan of £35,000, nominally at 4 per cent., by the big
northern cattle men, to be put into freezing and canning
works at Wyndham or thereabouts. There is a pile of
money behind the crowd—the “ meat ring ” in the West
has filled their pockets—but they are not game to risk
the full cost of the works; so the Government is going to
put up half and stand out of interest for three years to
give the concern a start. If the ithing is a failure, as
mortgagee the State will have the scrap iron ; and if it is a
success, the State will get only the 4 per cent, that it will
have to itself pay for the money. From a business point
of view, it isn’t a glittering bargain. The Government
can’t possibly make a profit, and it stands a very good
chance of miking a loss. A 2 per cent, sinking fund is
provided for—and that’s all right, except perhaps
that in connection with very perishable works of
this kind it ought to be 3 per cent. But
the Westralian Government’s disgraceful conduct with
regard to the Goldfields Water Supply sinking fund
makes one suspicious. Mainly to furnish foreign mine-
owners with cheap water the Government carried out
this great undertaking, which was to provide a 3 per
cent, sinking fund ; but as soon as the money had been
spent, and the water was available, the Government
allowed the mineowners to squeeze it into reduced water-
rates, with the result that the Government has now to
dip into the pocket of the general taxpayer for the whole
of the sinking fund.
* * « *
The offer, so far as it goes, to provide the Vic-
torian Government with all the iron and steel it wants
“ at prices not higher than those of the imported article,”
comes from the New Direct Process Iron, Steel, and
Metals Corporation, which has the use of the Heskett-
Moore patents. The offer was made through Earle Her-
man. The corporation asks for a seven years’ contract
at current prices—no more. Earle Herman added in
a letter to Premier Bent—“ Thus the Government
is assured of its supplies of iron and steel pro
duced in the State, from Australian ores, up to-
the standard required by the Government.” Unfor-
tunately, it isn’t assured of anything of the sort.
When William Sandford got a contract in N. S. Wales he
was called upon to put up £SOOO as evidence that he would
do what he undertook. That was some guarantee. A
still greater guarantee was that he had been making
some iron—from “ scrap,” chiefly—for a quarter of a
century, and had the substantial part of a plant capable
of doing the Government work. The new co. has only
an experimental plant, and it mentions nothing about
guarantees. The process may be all right. But what
needs to be pasted on the cruet-stand on this country’s
lunch-table is that, if it fails, that fact won’t cast the
smallest reflection upon Australia’s ability to make its
own iron and steel. It will simply reflect upon a patent
process that the iron and steel makers of Europe and
America don’t use. Victoria’s Premier Bent is under-
stood, however, to be “ favorably disposed.” Possibly he
isn’t any less “ favorably disposed” because the co. men-
tions that Altona is the place where it would like to
establish its works.
* * * «
The usual meeting has been held at Cobar to
protest against the tariff iniquity. “ Mr. Blakemore, of
the Great Oobar, Ltd., said that in the case of the new
furnace and converter plant, it was impossible to
get this built in Australia.” Why? Where did Mt.
Morgan get its converter plant built ? The only reason
why it is “ impossible ” to get the Cobar plant
built in Australia is that “the makers refuse to even sell
the plans.” Well, then, the Great Cobar can get plans
also built in Australia. R. Shepherd prepared the plans
for the Morgan works. Later on he put up a converter
plant at the 0.K., in remote North Queensland;
and, in spite of the handicaps, it is as effective a
plant as foreign-director Kendall, of Great Cobar,
will send out to General Manager Blakemore. Moreover,
the chances are that the imported plant, made according
to stock plans, will be a good deal less effective—and will
certainly cost more money—than a plant which Blakemore
could provide himself with in Australia. On this ques-
tion of ineffective imported machinery, by the way, even
the Foreign-trade members of the Tariff Commission were
driven to make an astounding confession : “ There was a
great deal of imported machinery purchased for working
Westralian properties which was never used.” In most
cases it couldn’t be used. It was the same at Broken
Hill; imported managers imported vast quantities of
stuff that had to be promptly scrapped, and Australian-
made articles, suited to the special conditions, were sub-
stituted. G. H. Blakemore knows—he had to do some
of the scrapping.
The Cobar meeting of mine-owners was shocked
also at the effect of the 2s. duty on Oregon. “ Mr. W. J.
Hogan, chairman of the Occidental, said that the pro-
hibitive duty could have but one effect; it would come
back on the worker by decreasing the sources of
employment.” The last available report of the Occidental
shows that the timber bill was about £l5O for the six
months. That was at Cobar. Supposing the Occi-
dental used all Oregon, which it doesn’t, the value for
Customs purposes would be about £SO (the £l5O in-
cludes railway freight, etc.); and the 2s. per 100 ft.
duty on that quantity would amount to less
than £ls. Or take the Broken Hill Prop., the
biggest mining user of timber in Australia. In an average
half-year its mine-timber bill now is £25,000. The output
of ore is 300,000 tons. So if the duty were calculated
on this whole amount of £25,000 (which it isn’t),
and if it averaged 25 per cent, (and it
doesn’t), the duty would amount to a whole five-
pence per ton of ore. Moreover, “logs not sawn” and
“ spars in the rough ” are duty free ; so if the cos. want
to escape this ghastly extra duty, which probably
amounts on the average to quite two-pence per ton of
ore,, they can do it by importing Oregon logs and giving
Australian sawmillers a job.
