This State office was established late in 1896 to assist
farmers, and was organised as a branch of the Savings
Bank. The funds are provided by the sale of mortgage
bonds and debentures, some at 3, some at 3J per cent.
The discount at which these loans were issued was passed
into the balance-sheet as an asset. Of course it is not an
asset—it is simply something that the bank didn’t get,
but yet will have to pay back. The total discounts have
been £26,595 ; so far the bank itself has written off
£11,021 out of profits, leaving £15,574 of a paper asset in
the balance-sheet. In the table at the top of these re-
marks this has been deducted from the reserves shown
in the accounts, leaving £46,625 of true reserves.
The advances paid off during the year have ex-
ceeded the total of the new advances. Largely, this is
due to the fact that much less than the average number
of applications have been made for new advances ; the
total for the year was 550, as compared with 788 in the
previous 12 months, and 689 in 1905. Possibly this may
be due to the fact that times have been good and
assistance has not been required ; more likely it is due to
the fact that there have been ridiculously few new
farmers in Victoria—ridiculously, few considering the
area of the land locked up in big, poorly-used holdings.
Money outside this bank has also been cheap, and it has
been cheap for the farmer largely by reason of
the competition of this State bank, which makes
advances to him on very easy terms at 4£
p.c. For a while it had to charge 4f p.c.,
because in 1904 the bank had to pay £4 2s. Id. per cent,
for the money it u'as itself borrowing ; but the lending
rate has again been reduced to the old 4J per cent,
standard. So far, then, as this bank has forced other
banks into accommodating farmers at low rates, the full
service of this State institution to “ the backbone of the
country ” isn’t manifest in the published figures. Many
an Anti-Socialistic cocky who is paying the private shark
4£ per cent, for money which he could not have got till
recently for less than 7 per cent., doesn’t know his real
debt to this little bit of Socialism.
Eor one reason and another, therefore, the
year’s figures are not particularly satisfactory. For
instance: —
Expenses in
Mortgages Working proportion to

The advances outstanding include £423 which repre-
sents the whple of the properties in possession—
extremely satisfactory figures. Certainly this isn’t the
time when one looks for heavy and persistent arrears and
foreclosures ; they come, if they come at all, when times
are bad, and especially when bad times come after a
period of great prosperity and puffed-up values. Conse-
quently the fairly rapid growth of the reserves is a feature
of interest—they may be wanted some day. Anyhow,
whether they will be actually wanted or not they ought
to be there in case they are wanted.
While, therefore, the Victorian State Land
Bank can’t afford to lend at less than it has been doing—
the additions to reserves being none too large—by more
co-operation on the part of Victorian farmers who
need accommodation the lending -rate might in
course of time be brought down certainly to 4£
per cent, and probably to 4 per cent. The Maoriland
State Land Bank is in no better position to borrow,
and therefore ought to be in no better position
to lend. Yet because it had £3,551,876 out in
advances on March 31, 1907, its total management
expenses last year were only 4s. 9d. per cent, of advances.
It, therefore, could afford, on that account alone, to let
farmers have money at Bs. per cent, less interest than the
figure at which the Victorian bank can afford to do it.
This is one benefit that even the present Victorian farmers
will get from the sub-division of land in that State ; the
multiplication of those farmers, by the same processes
that have been adopted in Maoriland, will, or ought to,
mean bigger business for the State Land Bank, and that
in its turn will mean a lower rate for management ex-
penses ; and the borrowers will ultimately get the benefit
of that in reduced interest.

Bad luck is striking this co. in large lumps. First there
was the trouble over the Life funds. For many years the
shareholders had been pocketing the surpluses from the
old Life department. But two or three years ago the
law was invited to consider the matter, and Chief Justice
Madden delivered a judgment which would have involved
the repayment of over £113,000. Further appeals were
talked of, and as nearly everybody interested was old and
tired and didn’t want to be disturbed at dinner, a compro-
mise was arranged, at a cost to the shareholders of £55,521.
But that was only in the way of refund. The other part
of the cost will show up in future accounts ; for one
source of the shareholders’ profits has now been stopped.
Still the Life policy-holders don’t get all the profits of
their branch ; 25 per cent, still goes to shareholders. But
for that, things would look worse than they do for the
past year. The three branches (apart from Life) worked

But those last two bracketed lines call for an explanation.
As mentioned just now, an investigation of this branch
was made recently, and certain profits were ascertained,
derived no doubt from the usual sources- —excess of interest
earnings over those reckoned upon when the premium
tables were drawn up, and the greater vitality of the
policy-holders, so that they are paying up premiums
longer than was calculated upon. But while the totals
due respectively to the policy-holders (75 per cent.) and
the shareholders (25 per cent.) have been arrived at, the
policy-holders’ profits have not yet been cut up. The
snareholders’“ cut ” —£3o24—has in the foregoing been
added to the expenses ; the special bonus (£9661) due
to policy-holders, and which they may draw, has
been added to claims, though presumably the
bonuses need not be drawn in cash, in which
case the money falls back to swell the assurance
fund. The interest credited to this branch is £BO3I
(added to this are a few pounds of recoveries from
amounts written off). On the average of the assurance
fund, that means £3 16s. 6d. per cent. The assets rep-
resenting shareholders’ funds and the assurance fund are
not distinguished in the balance sheet; but as £205,371,
or 49 per cent, of all the assets, is on mortgage, it looks
as though there might be some over-valuation somewhere.

The Perpetual Executors and Trustees

1904 .. 2009 3 „ „ 900 9357 529
1905 .. 2198 3£ „ ~ 1050 ll4B 1677
1906 .. 2250 4j „ ~1275 975 2652
1907 .. 2392 5 „ ~1500 892 3544
This co., like so many more trustee cos., gives the public
a very poor idea of what is going on inside. Possibly,
shareholders can obtain a better view by getting close up
to the directors and manager, and probing them. But,
after all, shareholders are really as small potatoes in con-
nection with a trustee co. as the shareholders are in con-
nection with a bank; for £1 of their money, the co.
handles probably £2O or £IOO of other people’s money.
This co. in years back made large losses ; in 1903-4 it wrote
off £11,397. As in 1902 its reserves were only £9702, it is
pretty obvious that it had previously lost some of its capi-
tal. In other words, it is pretty certain that it didn’t
have a penny of the reserves it was supposed to have, and
didn’t have all the capital it had paid up ; and that isn’t
the sort of thing that ought to happen in connection
with any trustee co. It is an argument for much more
publicity than most of the cos. give. It is not certain
even now that the reserves are real; for 40 per cent, of
the assets are advances on mortgage “at book value ”
and when “ book value ” appears in a balance-sheet it re-
calls unpleasant things. And since the receipts from
commissions and interest are lumped, it isn’t possible to
even test the quality of the assets by the standard of the
return they are giving. From the clients’ point of view,
a satisfactory feature is the uncalled capital of £220,000 ;
and a sword of that size hanging over shareholders’ heads
is very liable to check imprudent habits.

£1424 was lost on realisations during the year. If that
means that £4284 (book value) realised £2860, the recent
average has been a little better than the five years’
average. The new business creeps up steadily, and it
hasn’t so far meant heavier management expenses ; five
years ago the management items totalled £1448, while
for last year they were £1446. Deposits are still moderate
compared with capital; and, though there is still con-
siderable cleaning-up to do, fair progress has been made
both as to that and in the matter of new business.

take over their assets.
* * * *
The mining share-markets are in a state of
nervous collapse. Copper and tin have tumbled with the
metal market; and silver-lead has been sagging for some
weeks. There were two reasons for that. (1) Speculators
found themselves overloaded, and something had to go.
The something that had suffered least was silver-lead, so
that was thrown on the market to raise the wind. (2) In
general, speculators had no hope that lead would stand
up while all other metals fell. Yet, though the market
had thus discounted a lead slump, when it actually began
to happen there was a rush into the market on
the part of those who hadn’t thought about the
matter till the news in the papers hit them and
woke them up. For a month past lead has
been hanging around £2l. At the middle of last week it
was still £2l 2s. 6d. By the end of the week it had been
sold for forward delivery at £lB 155.; or £l9 ss. prompt
delivery. Broken Hill Prop., 68s. a month ago, fell to
595. ; Souths (paid) from £6 12s. to £4 19s. ; Sulphide
Corporation from 15s. to 10s. 6d. ; and others in pro-
portion.

Of that 10,021 the B. H. Proprietary employs 3375, the

so on down the scale. Looking at the comparison set
out above, the paper that advises a man to move on to
Broken Hill and make the 10,022nd takes a big respon-
sibility.

Last month Broken Hill South Blocks turned
out £13,155 worth of concentrates, taking lead at £2O
12s. 3d. and silver at 2s. 9Jd. Costs ran to £7834, leaving
a profit of £5321. There were 1497 tons concentrates,
assaying 6ty per cent, lead and 3Joz. silver. That works
out at 917 tons lead and 48650 z. silver. At the average
metal figures quoted for the month, this output would be
worth about £19,560 ; so that, as the output is stated to
have been actually worth £13,155 to the co., the buyers
have deducted about £4 7s. 6d. per ton of concentrates to
cover smelting and realisation charges, etc. The co.’s
profit—£s32l on 917 tons of lead—was at the rate of £5
16s. per ton of lead. That is to say, assuming silver to
keep up at 2s. 9Jd. per oz., the South Blocks, working on
last month’s scale, would come out clear, showing
neither profit nor loss, if lead fell £5 16s. below £2O 12s.

* * * *
Block 14 is more calculated to bite the unwary
than any other Broken Hill stock just now. The mine
has admittedly done far beyond expectations. Two
things, working together, are wholly responsible—the
development of scattered bodies of carbonates, and the
phenomenal lead market. Carbonates, which can be
smelted direct, and at this mine are 32 per cent, lead,
command nowadays a high price, because very little of
this class of ore, which “runs like water” in the furnace,
is available. Mainly by rooting out this ore, Block 14
has been making £3OOO a week profit. But this ore is
essentially a surface ore ; there cannot possibly be more
than a few' thousand tons of it. The mine’s future must
rest on its sulphides ; and the sulphide developments
have been wretchedly poor, in spite of a few flashes in the
pan. For the past quarter, the co. will pay £26,500 in
uivs. on Nov. 13 ; that is, 2s. 6d. per share all round,
besides the preference shareholders’ 10 per cent. That
cannot, however, for the reasons stated, be taken as any
sort of a guide to the co.’s future payments.
* * * *
The little Gillies zinc plant, which has passed
through considerable tribulation at Broken Hill, is again
grinding away with one pan of doubtful identity and
mixed parentage. Its few tons of concentrates a week
a ‘® n °. t of high assay value (it is putting through 500 tons
of tailings), but still they are well above the marketable
grade ; and while this little lot is keeping the pot boiling
at Broken Hill, the directors in Melbourne are having a
tug-of-war with financial difficulties, and are completing
arrangements for the use of a satisfactory flotation
process. The De Bavay plant, which aspires to the pro-
duction of a 49 per cent, zinc concentrate, has a com-
pletely remodelled plant, but is not yet out of the
wood. Regular treatment at the rate of 200 tons of tail-
mgs per day, and the production of a 49 per cent, con-
centrate, is still a dream of the management.
The S. M. Herald published a wire the other
day about the Broken Hill North under the heading
Huge silver-lead lodes : Ore body 700 ft. wide.” Huge
—rather ! Divide it by 10, and the S. M. Herald would
stdl be a bit ahead of anything the B. H. North can show.
The Crowl Creek-Shuttleton (N.S.W.), recon-
structed only a few months ago, has struck trouble at its
banker’s : wherefore it had to rush up a notice at the end
of last week that all hands must go. It might have been
better if the notice had been posted a month earlier, for
wages have fallen heavily into arrear. Though the mines
haven’t lived up to surface prospects (copper shows very
rarely do), it is inconceivable that, with so much de-
velopment work done, the co. will be allowed to fall
through altogether. All that is the matter is that the
recent reconstruction was put through on a wrong basis,
the result, no doubt, of the abnormal metal-market con-
ditions prevailing at the time.
* * * *
.The Operator, Wyalong (N.S.W.), was the
scene, the other day, of a scramble for possession, the
lease having been cancelledfor non-observance of working
conditions. The object is not purely one of exploring
mother earth, but because of the fact that the Operator,
a lease of five acres, which has been fairly well developed
down to the 250 ft., contains valuable frontages to the
main street of West Wyalong. Hence the successful
applicant will doubtless endeavor to drop his heavy
burden into the lap of the Brookman Go. (late Red Flao-)
which co. in turn will secure the aforesaid valuable
frontages, and eventually dispose of them, by amalga-
mating the two properties, and concentrating the addi-
tional labor required in the Brookman co.’s present
workings. This opens up a prospect not contemplated
when the new Mining Act was being framed. The appli-
cations (26 in all) have been sent on to the Minister.
* * * -A
Although some of the stopes at Mt. David
(N.S.W.) have been worked out, there is nothing for the
market to take such a decided huff over. The shoot of
payable stone seems likely to go down, and is longer at
No. 2 level than at No. 1 ; in fact, the latter will have to
be driven some 70ft. to connect with the “ B ” rise from
No. 2. As to backs, they are, of course, limited ; the
management, in maintaining a heavy output, has hardly
had time to see to reserves.
The New Homeward Bound, Wyalong
(N.S. W.), has achieved its fourth “ threepenny.”
Metallurgist F. Drake, one of the first men to
tackle the concentration of Broken Hill ores, in the
early days of the Barrier field, goes back to Europe this
week with half-a-dozen N. S. Wales copper and silver-lead
options in his portmanteau. For some years after leaving
Australia, Drake represented large Anglo-French interests
in S'Africa, and, in spite of all the absurd yarns about
Socialistic legislation, those interests are inclined to turn
this way in search of investments. So far as the “ Social-
istic legislation ” is concerned, Mr. Drake, after nearly 12
months in Australia, is prepared to report to his people
that they needn’t have any misgivings.
The Australian gold output continues tailing
off ; for the first nine months of the year the aggregate
was estimated at 2,354,1000 z., as against 2,587,8510z] for
the same section of last year. There is a general shrink-
age ; but the Westralian shrinkage is the worst.

The total Westralian output of gold since 1886
to the end of September is 17,913,3870 z. fine, valued at
£76,090,326. Dividends paid have totalled £17,013,066, of
which £1,275,901 was paid between January and Septem-

have been Great Boulder, £2,578,675 ; Horseshoe
£2,430,000; Oroya-Brown Hill, £2,011,241; Ivanhoe’
£1,818,750 ; Great Fingall, £1,512,500 ; Boulder Persever-
ance, £1,286,250; Kalgurli, £540,000; Associated Northern,
£525,000; and Sons of Gwalia, £441,925. The October
distribution already reaches £142,500.
• * * *
Loch Mines Ltd. (W.A.) has gone into liquida-
tion. Thisco. was registered in Sept., 1905, to acquire
all the assets of a moribund concern which owned a
played-out mine, known as the Lady Loch, at Coolgardie.
Some £13,500 was subscribed, half of which was paid away
as consideration-money. The balance was melted in the
usual way. Finally, the co. reached the end of its tether,
and then the auctioneer came along and disposed of
everything in sight. The chairman explained, at the
recent meeting of shareholders, that their remaining
asset was stock held in the Jacoletti mine, Southern
Cross, and the Board thought it better to wind up the
co. and distribute these shares among shareholders. As
the Jacoletti is also a wild-cat, the outlook for share-
holders seems as black as ever.
* * * *
The Great Tower Hill Cos. (W. A.) is still looking
for fresh capital. In the meantime, local creditors are
being buoyed up with the managerial assurance that
every effort is being made to raise the wind, and that
everything will come out right in the end. Some of the
claimants, however, are getting very impatient.
* * * *
Explorer Frank Hann has again turned up in
the West, this time with an account of his latest journey
across the Never-Never from S. Australia. As on former
occasions, the ancient battler tells about crossing sand-
hills, meeting the tracks of others in perishable parts,
narrow squeaks from death through thirst, and poisoned
camels. He also volunteers the information that he saw
no mineral country on or near the border.

one. Other tributing parties had previously worked the

Nearly 2000 tons have been picked out of the
Karridale, at Burtville (W.A.), during the past four years,
and treated for an average of soz. per ton ; and ore of
even better grade is said to be on hand for crushing.
Judging by the time taken to get out the stone already

shoot doesn’t need to be very big or regular to give a
small party fine profits when the stuff averages £2O per

The Maori Queen lease, Yundamindera (W.A.),
has been sold to a Melbourne syndicate for a few thousand
pounds. Since this show was discovered, six years ago,
it has produced 24000 z. of gold over the plates, with an
average of about 9dwt. left in the tailings. It is esti-
mated that there is now nearly £20,000 worth of gold in
sight; but this remains to be proved.
* * » *
lhe old British King, at Darlot (W.A.), is again
earning money. This show was once owned by the Fingall
Keefs Extended Cos., of London, but was thrown up after
about 10,000 oz. of gold had been extracted from above
trie 160 ft. level. J. Bull seems to have cleared out in a
hurry, as he left a heap of sands behind which someone
else subsequently treated for a profit of about £2500.
The present owners are said to be doing well, but what
is a fortune to a small party of working miners is utter
poverty to an over-capitalised English company.

Ziegler’s old claim at Bung-Eye Gully, Norse-
man (W.A.), is again being worked with profitable
results. This show was first tested about eight years
ago by Ziegler, who took between 300 and 400 oz. of gold
out of it, and then left. Another man came along a few
years later, unearthed more gold, and also departed.
Now a third man and his mates have dollied over £4OO
worth of gold. They are still there.

The Emu Bay Railway 00. (the railway is in
Tas. and the shares are held in Melbourne and elsewhere)
is offering something novel in the way of reconstruction.
Besides a goodwill item—“ Rights acquired under the
oi„ °" ? Railway Act and other concessions,
£IOO,OOO —the co. had at Dec. 31 last two paper assets •
Preliminary exes., £36,125; and profit and loss deficit,
£30,552. The co. now proposes to wipe out the bulk of
these two latter items by writing off ss. per share
from the 260,000 ordinary shares, which will provide
£65,000. The other £IOO,OOO of paper won’t be disturbed.
The most rational way to knock that lot off its
perch would be to wipe out the 100,000 ordinary shares
which were issued against those rights. But though those
rights really were not worth 100,000 pence, no doubt
many of the shares have left the hands of the original
holders, so, for that reason among others, it isn’t feasible
to wipe them out. The public subscribed for 160,000
ordinary and 100,000 pref. shares. The present proposal
i® ' vipe ,. ss - off the vendor’s 100,000 and the public’s
160,000 ordinaries ; but “ notwithstanding the reduction,
the ordinary shares are to rank as regards dividend and
capital as at present.” In other words, though they will
look like 15s. shares in the balance-sheet, they will really
be 20s. shares so far as distributions of profit and capital
are concerned, and the difference between that and fakirv
a balance-sheet is too small to be worth troubling about
It is really a plan for getting rid of two awkward-looking
paper assets by nominally writing off capital which
actually isn’t written off. Why the directors when they
are about it don’t go the whole hog and write off the
whole £165,000 isn t clear. Since the writing off means
nothing whatever, it would be just as easy to write off
15s. as 55.; wherefore the moderation of the co. is, under
the circumstances, quite unintelligible and also quite
superfluous.
The position of the co. as at Dec. 31,1906, when
the last accounts were made up, was that, for the second
time in its history of nine years, a profit was shown after
paying interest on the £200,000 of debentures. The co.’s
freight is almost wholly in minerals, and the metal boom
was responsible for the good figures. Besides the deben-
tures just mentioned, the co. has £IOO,OOO of 8 per cent
preference capital, and a 4 per cent, dividend on this is
cumulative ; the accumulations on this account to Dec.
31 last were £27,207. Further, there is a standing charge
of £10,370 a year for the use of a section of the line owned
by the Emu Bay and Mt. Bisehoff Railway 00. ; and this
is a shocking drag during the lean years that come to
every mining field. Under the circumstances, the clean-
ing up, even if it were the leal thing, would be far from
being as thorough as the circumstances call for.

Gladstone (Tas.) was the pioneer in the tin-
dredging boom, and it is fittingly the pioneer in reaching
the div. stage. All it pays is a modest 25., but that’s a
beginning. The market didn’t even blink at the news,
for it is ready to accept anything now in the tin line since
the blow-out of the Brothers No. 1, and the 18-ton yield
of the Briseis Extended.
* * * #
Jumbunna Coal (Vic.) directors tell the share-
holders as follows This falling off in output (3022
tons) is notin any way due to a lessened capacity of the
mine, but solely to want of miners. Many of our best
men left the district owing to the action of‘some of their
fellow workmen, who are attempting to form and
register anew union under the Commonwealth Arbitra-
tion Act. The same reason kept new men from coming,
who would otherwise have taken their places. We are
opposing this registration, as are also the majority of our
miners, being quite sure that it is against the best
interests of the co. and the men.” That is to say, a
union which is being formed to raise wages and secure
better conditions for the worker is likely to do him harm !
This comes from a State-subsidised co., which alleges
that 13s. per ton for its coal is not a paying price. Its
accounts show that mine wages, “ material, plant, etc ”
amounted to £19,933 during the past half-year. Capital
expenditure, therefore, seems to have been lumped with
ordinary working expenses, which is a strange way of
doing things. No doubt it is done that way for a purpose.
Of course, the } T arn about tbe scarcity of miners is fudge.
That scarcity has nothing to do with'the local growth of
unionism—especially the brand of unionism that looks
to arbitration. It is because good miners are in demand
at Newcastle, Broken Hill, and elsewhere. They won’t
stop in a district where they are not to be allowed to take
steps to protect themselves, and where their billets
depend on their employers continuing to receive uncertain
State aid.
» * * •
Bendigo shows up poorly for the nine months.
The total (141,6990 z.) is the lowest since 1902 ; the highest
was in 1904, when 185,2600 z. was reached between January
1 and Sept. 30. Every month’s output this year has been
below that for the corresponding month of last .year.
Cause, the failure of the field to replace such gold-pro-
ducers as South New Moon, Specimen Hill, and New
Argus. The best mine for the moment is probably Vir-
ginia, but its dividends go into only half a dozen pockets.

Great Northern Extended Consols, Rutherglen
(Vic.), has a big name and small yields. It lives just
about as long as the Prentice and Southern, lower down
the lead, pumps for it: the co. has not at present the
strength of capital to keep the mine dry on its own.
Shareholders have put up £71,500, and gold has provided
£BI,OOO additional; so the mine hasn’t been treated badly.
If, however, the co. had another £IO,OOO cash in hand to
get on with drives, and to let the ground drain longer,
things would be very different; it might then get in the
dividend list with anything like the last half-year’s average.
Water and the need to get gold hamper operations,
though the Board runs the show so that every penny is
made to buy a full penn’orth. Hartley, the chairman, is
the biggest supporter of Rutherglen'mining just now,
and he looks for better times. But he must not discount
the fact that funds are short lower down the lead.
• * • »
The Argus nags about the poor yields from
two Daylesford shows—Nuggety Ajax and Sailors Falls.
Now, the Argus gave the Ajax boom a lift at the start,
and never said a word of warning till the boom fizzled.
Having been silent so long, it might have waited a
bit longer ; for, after all, it is early to say that the
Nuggety Ajax and the Sailors Falls are duffers, though
unfortunately the dreary Waste of Daylesford mining,
even with the Ajax thrown in, doesn’t give much hope of
better things. The whole question is, Why was a battery
put up to crush stone till some bulk work had been done'?
There is plenty of crushing power within reach, and a
battery test is worth yards of assay sampling. It is just
here that Ballarat messes up things. A prospect, then a
battery; after that, open up the mine. Things should
be the other way round. If the Argus wants to preach a
sermon, here is a text.

Peak Downs, the Central‘Queensland copper
bubble, is to have a meeting this week in Brisbane,
whereat the mysterious arrangement with the Mt.
Morgan crowd is to be unfolded. It is certain that when
the details are made public those unfortunate dupes who
have swallowed the Stock Exchange yarns, concerning
what Mt. Morgan is going to do for present shareholders,
will want to kick somebody. Whether the Mt. Morgan
crowd takes an option over Peak Downs or not, recon-
struction must certainly take place almost immediately
and development of the property will have to be carried
on for quite six months before anyone can decide whether
the property is worth hanging on to with copper at an
average price.
Mfc. Molloy (N.Q.) has suspended dividends for
clt'i lla (: year i y accounts to June 30 only now issued
Conner hnrf adlnß: ’ have changed since then.
Copper had averaged over £IOO up to that date, and the
° re , w .\ s nclle . r than it is now, and the output was
CwL h and the .optimistic punter was still nrevalent.
Costs have certainly been reduced these last few months ;
hut more definite information on the point will be needed
to stop the tendency to unload. Late returns, though
depressing, are not so bad as they seem at first sight.
More low-grade fluxing ore is now included, so the
average value of the ore and the costs have come down
together. If 500 tons of 5 per cent, silicious ore is fluxed
with 500 tons of 2 per cent, pyritic ore, instead of with
barren material, the average yield is reduced, but the
profit is increased. That is why Mt. Lyell, with a return
of 2 per cent, or thereabouts, does better than many 4
per cent, copper mines that have to drag' lime and iron-
stone long distances. Rapid progress will be made with
Molloy s connecting railway ; 1700 tons of rails have been
delivered.
* * * *
The Hampden-Cloncurry and Mt. Elliott people
have concluded some sort of an arrangement with the
Queensland Government regarding the railway to connect
those mines with the State line. Mt. Elliott is to put
up £60,000, Hampden £40,000, and the Government
£100,000; and the Government is to get 3| per cent,
out of the profits. So far no other particulars of con-
sequence are forthcoming ; and a few are needed.
What arrangement is there for the return of capital?
A railway built to take away the output of a mine must
sooner or later find itself terminating at a mere hole in
the ground ; and when the mine becomes a mere hole in
the ground, the railway' becomes a mere quantity of use-
less rusting steel and decaying timber. The peopie who
own the mine that is to become a mere hole in the
ground have hitherto mostly' made an agreement
with the State that just about the time when that
happens the State may or shall become the owner
of the railway. Apparently nothing of that sort is pro-
vided for here ; but it would be interesting' to know
whether Kidston has upended the usual arrangements,
and provided that the State shall be relieved of its Share
in the business by the time the business is likely to be
unprofitable. And for that purpose nothing less than a
5 per cent, sinking fund is called for.

Mt. Hector (O.) was stated, three or four weeks
ago, to have an overdraft of £4778, secured by uncalled
capital amounting to £4947. Shareholders met again in
Brisbane a few days ago and found that the debt was
growing, and there was trouble about meeting current
accounts. The bank, however, undertook to see the co.
out of that trouble, on certain guarantees being pro-
vided. So far the total liabilities exceed the uncalled
capital by' about £IOOO. Some shareholders hang on to
the hope that £2OOO worth of matte in transit will help
them through ; but the chances are that that matte has
been drawn against for its full value, if not more. So the
outlook is dismal.
* * * *
Mungana (N.Q.) has had a good output of
copper, lead, and silver lately, and the'Girofla shaft has
struck a patch of 30 per cent, lead ore a few feet below
the 410 ft. level. Metallurgist Kunze is trying to place
the treatment of Mungana’s complex sulphides on a satis-
factory basis before he leaves. The difficulties have been
under-rated. The ore is complex, because it is a mixture
of copper, lead and zinc combined with sulphur. Roast-
ing in Edwards’ furnace after the ore is crushed to a
powder, and converting in Huntingdon pots, may' make
the ore smelt better ; but how about the cost? There is
no parallel between the treatment of Broken Hill Prop.’s
rich concentrates in Huntingdon-Heberlein converters,
and that of Mungana’s 12 per cent, lead ore mixed with
copper and zinc.
* • * *
The fearfully and wonderfully managed J. Bull
co. that owns the Great Australian, Cloncurry, knocked
off most of its mine hands, the other day, and the chances
are that the rest will follow. Naturally, the first big
Cloncurry co. to funk on the copper slump is the one
that boasted most of what it was going to do. Still, if
the closing-down is only' temporary and results in a com-
plete change of methods, it will be a good thing for the
co. and the field. It would take a Mt. Morgan to stand
the methods that have prevailed hitherto.
* * * «
It is officially admitted now that the McGregor
leases (of the McGregor Cloncurry Cos.) have given “ in-
different ” results ; but the Wallaroo leases are represented
as being plums. The No. I shaft cut the lode at 84ft.
(water level), and it is announced that a crosscut proves
the lode there to be 28ft. wide. But what are the indica-
tions that it is anything more than a bulge ? The footwall
and hanging walls are heading in different directions, and
that is suspicious. An official report says that drives
have been put in north and south 50ft. on the foot-
wall, and the faces are still in ore. It might
have told shareholders what the ore is worth. South,
at any rate, the face on a recent date showed good
“ buck ” quartz. The north drive has exposed ore here
and there. Same with the drive north on the hanging
wall : jt has yielded ore, but the stuff broken has to be
picked, and picked again. Probably this much-picked
ore will be sent to Mt. Elliott smelters, or further afield ;
but the man who accepts the return as showing what the
28ft. lode is made of is liable to hurt himself.
There is a change for the better in the bottom
of the Vulcan (N.Q.), which is really about the first cheer-
ful thing 1 that has happened since the shares were cut up,
and the guileless working-men of Irvinebank let the
southerner in at high prices. Almost as cheerful is the
news that, in view of the changed outlook, the local
powers are more disposed to put in an aerial tramway, or
something of the sort, to get the stone to the battery.
But nobody dares to yet suggest that the mine should do
its own crushing.
The little Charters Towers co.—the Marble
Island Iron and Limestone Cos. —that owns the speck in
the ocean from which Mt. Morgan is drawing its iron flux
supplies, expects to make up its dividends to £4 per share
for this half-year, and to keep it at that while the
contract lasts. The big; co. is to take 100,000 tons at
Is. 6d. per ton, mining the stuff itself, and
improvements (wharves, etc.) are to go to the co.
that owns the island. The latest calculations set “ the
quantity of ore on the surface” at 3,000,000 tons;
and the last fair sample showed 62 per cent,
metallic iron—about the grade up to which iron-
ore is now being actually concentrated by magnetic
process in Sweden for English iron-masters. With iron-
ore of this quality on the Queensland coast, it will be
something to gamble about presently as to whether the
Mt. Morgan Cos. or the Broken Hill Proprietary (which
has a fine deposit at Iron Knob, whence it gets its flux
supplies) will establish an iron and steel making depart-
ment first. It is pretty certain that the idea is buzzing
in theß.H. Prop’s head, and that gen. manager Delprat
(who gets back to Australia this week) made special
inquiries into the matter in Europe.

* * * *
Tin, which opened this year at £195, got down
to £l4l 10s. for spot before the end of last week ; and
the situation at Tingha (N.S.W.) and other old fields is
precarious. There is no metal more unreliable ; the
average price for 1897—10 years ago—was no higher than
£6l Bs., and it had been lower than that. Absolutely
blind as to the future, yet remembering the tricks the
market has played hitherto, the N.S.W. dredges are
cutting down expenses, and have mostly knocked off their
timber-getters wherever they have got two or three
months’ supply of firewood on hand. They are not pre-
pared to risk what may happen beyond that time.
* * * *
“Caradoc” : The stoppage of work by the Tas-
manian Copper Cos. at Leigh’s Creek gives a serious bump
to S.A.’s far north copper mining, but it was a bubble
that was certain to be pricked pretty soon whether
copper kept over or under £7O. The fall in the metal
market was an excuse rather than a cause for the shutting,
down, as was patent to everybody who had watched the
events of the past four months and who knew the country’s
squibby nature. The whole of the north to Warrinais
ht only for scattered parties of gougers, and it is absurd
to suppose any big co. is going to make a“do ”of it. If
nalf-a-dozen of the squibs and gash veins were crowded
into an 80-acre section, there would then be some reason
tor erecting machinery and smelters on the spot; but the
cupriferous blobs occur erratically over an area of country
as big as a small State, and are not high-grade at that.
certainly in bygone years the-Blinman turned out a bit
of copper, as also did the Mount Rose ; but neither of
them is now wortlvls. an acre. All attempts to geton toa
continuation of shoots worked in earlier years have failed
—tailed because their shoots were of the squib variety.
Their squibs happened to be rather larger squibs than most
others, but still they were just squibs. To make things
pay, ore must be plentiful, worth at least 7 per cent.,
and metal not below £BO per ton.

The introduction into .the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small hand rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“hitches ” for timber, and squaring up shafts and levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has only one working part—the hammer—which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. Ir some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 24in. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [c]

Business Education.—A tree is known by its
fruit, a business school by its successes in public exami-
nations and the demand of business men for its gradu-
ates. In both these respects Stott and Hoare’s Business
College stands at the head. A single instance will ex-
emplify the first claim : At all Government Examina-
tions for Shorthand Writers and Typists, held during the
past five years, students have won first place. As to the
second, not only does every student as soon as competent
secure an appointment, but the demand for graduates
greatly exceeds the supply. It is a mistake in these
times to put a boy to business immediately he has passed
through his primary or high-school course ; years of
poorly-paid drudgery will be saved him, and his pros-
pects of advancement will be greatly enhanced by giving
him a sound modern business education at this well-
known institution. Prospectus post free. 28 Moore
Street, Sydney. *

“ The Prooshan ” : “ Midnight ” came in
from Chocklogatoo out-station, and with him came
his famous kangaroo dog Bosco. Meeting the boss
’rooing some five miles from the homestead. Mid-
night was invited to join the hunt. Now, the boss’s
dogs were good, especially a blue slut with a bob-
tail, and he chaffed the black good-humoredly as
they rode in quest of their quarry. “ Bobtail
beat Bosco bad, Midnight.” “No pfear, boss,
you wait see, Bothco best slut in dis country.”
By-p,nd-bye ,a big red’roo was sighted, and the
three dogs were off like the wind, followed by the
party, led by Midnight in full cry. At first the
blackfellow’s dog led easily. And his owner was
nearly frantic. “Good old Bothco. Can’t lick
old Bothco. Go it Bothco, yeheo oo ! ”
-Presently the ’roo tacked, and the bobtail got the
inside running, and eventually killed. As the
party rode home Midnight was gloomy and
thoughtful, but silent as the grave. The moment
they reached the homestead he dragged his slut up
to the woodheap, got her tail on a log, and
chopped it off with an axe. The slut made a
plunge, and, in a moment, was off up the dusty
road like lightning. Midnight looked after her
like a man watching the flight of a meteor.
'■Then, turning, he remarked regretfully to himself,
” Cutem too damn short.”

“ Gip ” : Should we eat pig, and is there
anything a pig ivont eat ? Once on the bank of a
creek I ran on to a big black snake so extremely
dead as to have become a blot on the landscape.
Rather a tame-looking pig followed me closely.
Even a dead snake has a certain fascination, and
after covering most of my face with my hanky, I
turned the reptile’s head over with a long stick.
The pig came up, grunting friendlily, and to my
horror proceeded hastily to swallow that yard or
two of deadiiness. She ate it all, every scrap, and
sniffed round for more. And (ye gods 1) she was
a “ baconer.” I have never eaten bacon since.
“ J.G.” : Jim Morrissey and his reptiles
remind me of another snake fool who used to
prowl round northern Victoria, picking up
lively tiger, brown, black, and other venom-
ous types. Having collected about 80, he
placed them in an old bran bag, hoisted the bag
on his shoulder, and marched away to Kerang.
There he trained for Bendigo, and stowed the bag
of snakes under the seat. At Bendigo lie dumped
his bagof reptiles downamong the other passengers’
luggage, and went off to have a drink. Presently
the all-seeing porter came along, and noticing the
wriggles of the bag, thought he had a case for col-
lecting excess luggage. He was opening the port-
manteau, and as he did so the voice of the snake-
charmer fell on his ear : “Look out, young feller,
them’s my snakes.” The porter looked out, and
also lighted out for the other end of the platform.
Then he regarded the snake man, a weird-
looking individual with one wooden leg, a
clean-shaven face, and long, matted hair, and
ordered him to get off the premises,
snakes and all, and also informed him that he
would not be allowed to take his reptiles on the
railway again unless they were properly boxed up.
The professor took his snakes away and opened a
show, where he explained that he had an antidote
for snake poison. He used to allow the snakes to
bite him and then apply his cure ; and, to prove
that the snakes were genuinely poisonous, he
requested the people to bring along mongrel dogs
and unnecessary cats. They came in crowds, and
the snakes had a good time. The reason the pro-
fessor had a wooden leg was that his real one had
been chopped off to save his life once when the
antidote didn’t get to work with sufficient velocity.
It was reported that, after he left Bendigo, he
lost an arm for the same reason. As he has
not been around since, it is presumed that his
head and body have also had to be amputated to
save his life.
* * » *
There was a curious mixture of our Sove-
reign Lord Edward and our Tickapenny Brother
Bill up at far-north Cooktown lately. Over
against Cooktown there is a large collection of
brother Bill and sister Mary, and they happen to
be a mixed lot, tribal laws being suspended for
cadging purposes. But the ancient customs
haven’t been blotted out altogether, wherefore
when Tiger was accused of spearing Missador, of
the Mclvor tribe, Tiger had to undergo the trial
by combat. Armed only with a woomera, he
had to stand off ahead of his own tribe and face
30 Mclvor spears. He parried the first volley ;
but one of a second volley beat him and caught
him in the hand. While Tiger was thinking over
the matter, Jacky, reckoning tribal proceedings
too slow, crept up and fatally speared Tiger in
the back. Then King Edward became interested
in the proceedings of King Billy, and Jacky
found himself charged with murder. A Cook-
town jury puzzled over the matter for a day, then
decided that Black Brother had best settle the
whole argument himself, wherefore Jacky was
turned adrift. And seeing that, after he had
thrown the spear in the unauthorised fashion,
Tiger’s tribesmen grabbed their spears and pursued
Jacky into the mangroves, it looks as though there
will be another dead nigger up there shortly—if
he isn’t dead already. On the whole, this paper
has an idea that black brother Jacky hasn’t had
quite a fair deal; it was a fair enough thing for
him to stand one trial, but to make him stand two
—King Edward’s and King Billy’s—is a bit too
much like asking him to lay two to one where
other men get an even chance.

Paddy Nash, of the Old Men’s Home, tore
the tobacco with his nails and the tips of his bony
fingers. “It worrit no drink as brought me ’ere ;
an it worn tno card-playin’
neither. It wus me good
luck ; that’s what brought
me ’ere. W’y, I wor all
right till luck come. I ’ad
mequid aweek
an’ foun’, an’
only a bit of
garden groun’
to look after.
But one day I
heerd people
talkin’ about
sweeps, an’ for
want o’ some-
thin’ else to do
at the time I
takes a ticket
an’ thinks no
more about it.
A month or so
after, however,
they shows me
’ow I won
£350. That
slewed me. In
a month no
one ’d know
me. In three
1 didn’t know
meself. I dun-
no ’ow many
gaols they put
me in. Then
it wor mad-
house after madhouse. I used to think as
they carried me about I wor Napoleon Bony-
parte. After a while I wor Garsfield. Then I
seemed to settle down to Brian Boru. They say
I wor Brian Boru before cornin’ ’ere. I suppose
I wor. But, ye can ax them, I niver took a drop
o’ dhrink the whole time. It wor just luck—
nothin’ in the world but luck.”

“G-.S.K.”: Within the south-western
region of Queensland there exist certain geological
mysteries called mud springs. Each of these con-
sists of a conical mound from 12ft. to 14ft. high
and from 14ft. to 16ft. through the base, whilst
the width across the top varies from 8ft. to 10ft.
The slope of the mound is grassy, and the topis
soft and muddy. Sheep often get bogged on the
top of a spring, and die there ; the remains of the
greater animals are also found in the slush. On
rare occasions the springs break forth into running
streams, and the bones of the perished animals,
together with large stones and sundries, are
carried over the sides, so that there is a collection
of debris at the base of each cone. Probably they
are outlets of the artesian water channels, and
break forth when the channels become over-
charged. Presumably they were once level with
the surrounding country, but the constant percola-
tion of muddy water through the ground built the
cones.

Diarrhoea, in its first stages, can be cured
by a few doses of that pleasant, reliable, and
effectual medicine, Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera,
and Diarrhoea Remedy. In many instances one
dose is sufficient, but as a rule three or four
doses are necessary. It is a good thing to keep
it in the house. Get a bottle to-day; it may save
a life.*

“10x2x4” (Sydney) is stirred to re-
miniscence by pars dealing with out-backers’
kindly treatment of strangers :
Was taken, one summer, by a fellow-toiler in this city
to stay with an up-country family, and shoot rabbits, lie,
fish, pet thrown off horses, and otherwise enjoy existence
as undergone in the utternrost recesses of Beyond. We
struck Awfulparirika—the place where the railway ended,
and the 50-mile coach and 15-mile waggonette drive, that
lay between us and our destination, began—at the hour
of 2 a.m.
My friend said, “ Come over to the Royal, and have a
drink with dear old Michael Hogan before the coach
starts. We have half-an-hour to wait, and Mick’s feel.ngs
’d be hurt if he heard I went through without calling on
him.” Agreed. A pallid barman let us in at a side r door
and brought whiskv, but refused affrightedly to disturb
Hogan, who, he said, had gone to bed, fighting drunk,
some hours earlier.
As we were having one more apiece, there came a rat-
tling sound from without like unto a bolting bottle-o-cart.
I queried whether it mightn’t be the coach. My friend
scouted the idea. “ You don’t seem,” he said, “to have
grasped the fact that I’m well known and liked about
here. I come every summer. Alfred, the coach-driver,
would as soon think of leaving me behind as he would
Rawson ; he’s seen my baggage, and that’s enough.” Our
drinks finished, we emerged upon the roadway.
“ Didn’t you two young fellers want the coach fer
Depression'Flat ? ” asked a railway employee. We ad-
mitted the fact as one man. “Well, yer missed ’er.
Alf ’lggins left quarter of an hour ago.”
Forcing an air of calmness, I asked when the next
coach left. “ We’n’sday,” the man rejoined. “ Twice a
week she goes ”—— At this my friend broke in : “ The
whole thing’s a mistake,” he explained. “ Higgins thinks
I’m inside. As soon as he finds his foolish error, he’ll
wait. What we’ve got to do now is to walk briskly after
him.”
I thought anything was better than staying three days
in that sort of place, and so—albeit apprehensively-
started out. We made good going for the first four miles,
as day hadn’t yet broken and it was fairly cool. Presently
the sun rose. Before us we saw a long, undulating,
magenta-colored road, but no Alf.
“ Simply inexplicable,” said my friend. “If you could
manage a gentle trot down the hills it might be a good
plan. He hadn’t much of a start, and I know he stops
for mails at a station a couple of miles from here.” We
ran swiftly down three hills. As we toiled perspiringly
to the summit of a fourth a sickening feeling of hopeless-
ness obsessed me. From this hill there was a fine view
of the surrounding country. Away below us, as far as
the eye could reach, stretched the road, innocent alike of
Alf or any vehicle.
“ My God ! ” said my friend ; “ we’ve taken the wrong
turning.” A big hpmestead—square, white and. cool in
its setting of willows and garden plots—shimmered in the
middle distance. “ That place,” he went on, mopping his
brow “belongs to the Smith-Smythes. I don’t exactly know
them, but I know relatives of theirs, and they know the
people we’re going to. Obviously there’s only one thing to
do, and that is go boldly in, explain the position, and ask
for the loan of a horse and trap.”
Under other circumstances, I might have hesitated, but
this case was desperate. The Smythe manage was abed
when we arrived, so we sat on a garden seat and waited. *
I regarded my friend, and then myself, and brooded as
to the chances of our being taken for horse thieves. I
dismissed the horse-thief theory (neither of us looked
horsey enough) ; and also, subsequently, the idea that
we could be tak n for any other sort of malefactors since
we both looked too foolish. I decided the average
observer would judge us to be lunatics.
We heard a blind snap-up, and a woman’s head ap-
peared at an open window, surmounting an ornate sac de
nuit. My friend rose, removed his hat with a certain
grace, and bowed, whereat there was a faint squeak, and
the window closed. “ Damn her,” he said irritably, and
sat down again.
Life began to stir about the place, and presently the
gravel crunched, and a middle-aged man of squire-like
aspect approached. It spoke powerfully for his courtesy
that he bore with us. My friend rendered our weird
story. Then he gave his name, which the squire had
never heard ; spoke of the people we were going to visit
(whom, it transpired; the squire didn’t know); and wound
up with a demand (1) for breakfast and (2) for a horse and
sulky, the same to be returned “ within the month.”
In dazed fashion I found myself at breakfast listening
to my friend putting in further good work on our behalf.
“ I may tell you, Mrs. Sniythe,” he said, affably, “ that I
know your aunt, Mrs. Browne, who lives at Manly”——-
“ I haven’t any aunt of that name, nor any relative living
in Manly,” she said. My friend became argumentative ",
the Smythe lady remained firm. As things bade fair to
get unpleasant I hastened outside.
The whole family, together with their retainers, saw us
off ; the oldsters funereally grave, the younger members
in a condition bordering on hysteria. “ Can either of you
drive?” asked Smith-Smythe hopelessly. “Hand me
the ribbons,” said my friend cheerfully. “ I was born in
the saddle-bow.” What he meant God knows, but
Smythe’s dejection deepened. As my friend was no
driver at all, and the old mare we sat behind was “light
in the mouth ” and tricky, our going aroused general in-
terest. We yawned drunkenly away, followed by 14 dogs
and most of the station hands, hit the gate-post smartly,
cannoned into the road, and lit out for the distant
horizon. I have never seen any of the Sm.vthes since.
As it chanced, they got their turnout back three weeks
later in a practically undamaged state.
Is there any other country on earth where two total and
suspicious-looking strangers could am, in the above
fashion and not get run in for trying to obtain horse-
flesh and carriage-ware under false pretences ? I doubt it.

“ The Prooshan ” : The most difficult man
to find in this country is the miner who is actually
on payable gold. Whenever you strike him it’s a
matter of only a week or two till he hopes to get
on the gutter, but if you go at the appointed time
the rock has risen, a bar has come in, she’s dipped
off, or something has happened ; but as soon as he
gets through that bar, he is confident he’ll touch
good gold ; and, indeed, he stoops down and picks
up a stone and jerks it over on to the very place
the real good gold is to lurk under. He is the
most sanguine of men, and even after 15 months
of futile effort and much starve, he seems to be
able to console himself fully by saying, “It ought
to have paid well enough.” He can show you
exactly where Billy Sluicebox made the mista’
and lie could have told Sammy Ealsebottom
“ there was no gold near the Falls,” and how Old
Ripple was mad to spend the time and money he
did on the Point; but as for himself, and his own
race-cutting and dam-making and wasted enter-
prise, well, he only knows “ there oughter bin
gold there.” One of the first things he does, as a
rule, is to dash off into another enterprise of great
importance, entailing more race-cutting, dam-
making, etc., and when six months more have
been spent in preparation he begins to prospect
the ground, just as a mere formality it would
seem.
THE RIVERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia is threatened with another drought. —Daily
Paper.
• All the rivers run into the sea.— Ecclesiastes.
The rivers run down to the sea all day,
And pause,not through the nights,
But bear to the salty brine the wealth
A careless nation slights.
And shadowy bales of fleecy wealth
Pass down when daylight fades.
A rustling crop of golden corn
Went out with the evening shades.
The rivers murmured against the banks,
And phantom pirates mocked.
For the rivers run down to the sea all day,
And never a stream is locked.
The shimmering heat on sunbaked plains
Will whiten the dying grass,
And blast with pitiless blaze the grain,
While rivers ceaseless pass.
The risers run down to the sea all day,
While gaunt, starved cattle die.
Was ever a curse so cursed as this
’Neath the dome of the sun-tracked sky ?
The rivers run down to the sea all day.
Will ever the people heed
And build a rampart against the waste
For the time of the country’s need?
The rivers rdn down to the sea all day,
And man, who should have worked,
Will pray in days of stricken need
To save where he has shirked.
The rivers run do\vn to the sea all day
With wealth that might have blest.
And they will—-till men arise and learn
This truth : ’Tis work prays best.
VIC.
W. L. SLACR

“Gip”: The jackass is a vindictive bird
at least, one of him is. Struck him fighting a
duel t’other morning. The scrap was an arranged
thing, and widely advertised, for the gum-trees
around swarmed with onlookers, and the bush
rang with heartless laughter. The knock-out peck
and scratch brought one of the combatants from a
high branch to the ground, almost at my feet.
His assailant, disregarding my indignant presence,
came at him savagely. By good luck I managed
to hit the attacking bird with a large stick. He
retired long enough to allow me to cover the
beaten one with a handy and holey bucket. A
sudden thought that the coined beef would be
boiled to rags and the dumplings to squash called
me home. I pushed a sardine tin of water under
the protecting bucket, and left. Returning in the
afternoon to liberate Jack, found the attacking
one still attacking. He had pecked a hole in the
ancient bucket almost large enough to admit him
to the presence of his injured relative. Again I
used the large stick with effect (my luck was in),
and, lifting the damaged bird, took him home for
treatment. He died on the way, however.

“ Aural ” : In reply to “ Snell’s ” question
(B. 3/10/’O7), I beg to state that you can identify a
bushie in town by pretending to be hard up and
asking him for half-a-crown. He will give it to
you if you really appear hard up. The townie
would see you in Hades first. The bushie’s sim-
plicity is flavored with the milk of human kind-
ness. The townie’s smartness is adulterated with
vinegar and gall.

TRAVELLER : “ What's yer charge fer bed an' breakfast, boss?”

“The Batcher”: Be “Larrikin” (B.
3/10/’O7) concerning the Australian bush-girl.
“Larrikin’s” brand is about the worst brand I
have heard of yet. Droughty conditions may be
responsible for the “man’s hat,” the “curtailed
skirt,” “the hob-nailed men’s boots,” which
belong, presumably, to some other members of
the family; but let “Larrikin” give the bush
parents credit for a little decent up-bringing
of their girls. The average bush-girl’s ancestors
take good care that their daughter does not
“shout like a man,” “swear like a trooper,”
“ ride astride,” “ kill a sheep and butcher it well,”
etc. Am glad to say that my experience of the
bush-girl does not coincide with “ Larrikin’s.”
“ The Prooshan ” : We had a real cunnin’
ole dart of a ’orse, called “ Buck,” once ; get inter
ennythin’, eat enny bloomin’ thin’, ’n’ jump enny
bloomin’ thin’. We uster side-line
’n’ ’obble ’irn—no bloomin’ use;
Buck’d jes’get out like a kintorshin
bloke at the serkis, that rolls roun’
th’ other side of ’imself, and looks
down ’is throat into ’is innards. If
’e couldn’ git clear, ’e’d bloomin’
well roll over the fence or bunt it
down. It licked orl. One Satdy
night, me sister Sarah and me was
ridin’ ’ome from the play over at
the township, and there wuz me
noble Buck farst asleep in the road
near the ’ouse. I nearly rid over
’im. “’Old this ’orse,” I sez to
Sarah, as I nips off like a bloomin’
snowflake. Then I gits a thun-
derin’ great bunch er stingin’
nettles close by, and claps ’em
under Buck’s tail close up. Well,
of orl the leps y’ ever see, that ’orse
beat orl. Tork about larf—l nearly
dide. There he wuz, goin’ like
’ell behind and proppin’ dead in
front. It licked oi l. Then he bolts
back’ards, and nigh rams a panel
outer the flower-garden fence;
roun’ the kitchen like mad he goes,
upsettin’ the tubs ’n’ corfin’ ’n’
sneezin’ like mad, ’n’ me bustin’
meself larfin’, and Sarah chasin’
Buck, tryin’ to pull out the nose-
gay, but Buck helt it down like
grim death. And mother comes
runnin’ out in her nightshirt, ’n’
father pokes his head outer the
winder and asts “ Wother ’ell’s
up?” I jes’ rolls on the sofa,
clean done, an’ me mother busts
out myin’, an’ sez she never thort
she’d ever see one of HER boys
come’ome drunk. Next day I
sees Buck at the back er the
paddick ; but he puts his ears
down when he sees me and bolts.
But I sees the nettles wuz gone. It licked orl.

“Elmore”: Someone asks what spirit
moved the individuals who named some places in
Australia ? After a long and searching investiga-
tion, I have come to the conclusion that it must
have been rum. On this side, Dry, Sandy and
Stony Creeks can he crossed every mile. One
notable exception is Boosey Creek. Then we have
Hell Hole and Devil's Elbow, Nine Miles in
galore, Byaduk and Wail (is this where our
anti-Socialists come from ?). Places that turn the
letter-sorters grey-headed are Euroa, Genoa,
Murtoa, Longwood, Longfood, Broadford, Brad-
ford, Kergunyah, Waligunyah, etc. Then we
have Won, Wron, and Wonwondah. It does,
indeed, make one wmnder.
“10-2-4”: “Jimmie Pannikin” sings
(B. 3/10/’O7) of the glories of our wattle-clad bush
in spring. But give me the Australian coast and
seascape every time. Consider the gorgeous
Stretch of headland, beach, and ocean that opens
up before the early morning wayfarer as he tops
(say) the hill leading northwards to Narrabeen
(N.S.W.) on whichever fair October morning he
may choose. The nor’-easter isn’t off Newcastle
yet. Soft and dove-colored lies the ocean below,
mirror-smooth except where, along league upon
league of sand, its rim is shattering to white pow-
der. The shining wet beach is edged with bowed
trees, and a gauzy haze of surf-spray ever drifting
back from the line of breakers isn’t sub-tropical
any more, but an island strand, with limitless
possibilities in the way of “combers’’and copra-
traders and gentle flower-crowned niggers com-
muning with something that is slight and velvet-
coated—the wraith of R.L.S. Only for a moment
though, and then, in the still distance beyond the
last of the great bluffs that stride away to the
skyline, comes cleaving the Newcastle steamer.
And South Sea illusions die, being impossible
things in the presence of a Newcastle steamer.
What a riot of purest opal, pinks and greys, and
faint blues, the whole sea and coast is now. The
shadowing hills are still quite black, but the just-
risen sun has touched and made divine the washed
grass and shrubs on the flats, so that they shimmer
with every tint of green, from sage to faintest
emerald. And so, too, the ocean—our Pacific
Ocean, the flaunting beauty among the world’s
oceans. Just past the surf-line and near at hand
the sea shows cool glimpses of white sand amidst
its soft, blue lights ; further out, where lie the
weed-covered rocks, it is shot dull purple by the
sun; and thereafter away stretches the dead
polished, dove-color of the deep water till the
half-dispelled morning mists are reached. No
hand of man can ever vulgarise altogether the
haunted beauty of our coasts. The roads that
wind by the sea take on a weird charm, from their
environment, the fishermen’s humpies—aye, even
the awful “ Marine Residences for Gentlefolk ”
contrive to blend somehow (God knows how !)
with the scheme of things. The garish wattle in
its drab setting can’t compare as a color scheme
with the magic clear lights that shimmer along
our fine and rugged shores these gracious spring
mornings. “ J.P.’s” sense of proportion is atro-
phied, or he rises late.
* * *
After some people had hunted up abori-
ginal legends to explain the name of Willaura, in
the Western District (Vic.), where closer settle-
ment is trying to crowd out the big landlord, it
transpired that the surveyor who “laid out ” the
townships thereabouts ran short of cognomens, so
combined his own and his best girl’s first names,
and stuck the result on the area. He wasn’t over-
burdened with invention, but still he might have
gone to some old country for a second-hand mon-
niker and done worse. It recalls Queensland’s
Degilbo. The surveyor was obliged to find some
name for the- place, so he turned the “ obliged ”
upside down, and called it that.
THE HILL-TRACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It olimbs its winding- way in the hills, and my spirit, too,
goes there.
Where the wind, with a note of the ocean, trills in aisles
of the upper air ;
Where great trees out of the brushwood lift a roof that is
high and green,
By huttrt ssed bole, and by root and rift, the track creeps
in between.
Through hazel scrub and the mountain musk, sassafras,
ash and fern ;
Deep-mossed nooks that are always dusk however the day
may burn;
Out and over the bald hill’s head, and a glimpse of the
homes of men—
The patchwork quilt of the farms, far spread—and into
the glades again 1
No furrow of plough has marked the soil, and the old
trees stand unmarred,
For these ways are apart from the ways of toil, and the
paths are steep and hard ;
The wild steers shoulder the thick scrub-box, and fee
dingo skulks and yells
As he hears, from his perch on the jutting rocks, the
clank of the cattle-bells.
This winding track that the beasts have worn in the
house of untrammelled things—
Beaten with hoof and cleared with horn through the halls
where the wild fowl sings—
Is a highway used, through the night and day, by the
feathered folk and furred,
The wallaby brown, and the squirrel grey, the fox and
the painted bird.
The black snake there leaves his scaly sign as he glides to
his bed of moss ;
The bandicoots snuffle and sniff and whine, as they
scuttle in fear across ;
The phalanger drops from his tree to hie in quest of a
daintier fare ;
And the dasyure slinks with a furtive eve at the sound of
the bird-notes there.
It’s a mystic way, the track in the hills, through the
house of the strange wild things,
Where a quavering note of the ocean trills in the halls
where the wild fowl sings ;
A narrow tortuous highway worn for the feathered folk
and furred—
Beaten by hoof and cleared by horn through the haunts
of beast and bird 1
VIO.
C. G. A. COLLES.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Handed over his baton at Bathurst
(N.S.W.) Sub-Inspector Parkinson (“Curly”),
aged 48. For 29 years he had been a limb
of the law. Deceased was, one time, prose-
cutor at the Sydney Water Police Court. He
held the world’s championship for remaining
under water alive—time, 4min. 6 l-ssec. —and the
high-diving record for Australia. Bathurst loses
a good policeman.
SAYLES, OF AUSTRALIA.
Victoria’s new County Court Judge
Moule’s first sentence was passed on a “ receiver”
who owned up to 15 previous convictions. His
Honor had intended to hand him three years’
hard labor, but, after perusing what he called a
“fair and nice” statement, and pondering over
the fact that accused had just married a young
wife, he gallantly reduced the term to 18 months.
Yet there are sure to be cynical people who will
say that what Judge Moule meant was that
marriage is equal to the other 18 months’ gaol.
It is hinted that a prominent Victorian
has found grace. A lot of people ejaculate : “So
much the worse for grace ! ”
Lysaght Bros, and Co., who own the wire-
netting factory that has been more talked about
in Federal politics this past week than all other
Australian industries put together, is mainly
Gibbs, Bright and Co. That much-pervading
firm nominates a majority of the directors on the
Lysaght Board.
One Frank Walker, of Hamilton (Vic.),
inherited £7OO last April. One day last week he
died with 305., his entire worldly possessions, in
his pocket. And there is no evidence that he
extracted a penn’orth of joy out of the other
£698 10s„ which mostly went in buying jimjams
at the fantod shop.
Parson Geo. Tregear, the anti-pub ex-
pounder of Melbourne, is an “amoosin’ little
cuss.” He issued a bombastic invitation to the
publicans of Footscray to attend a recent Beastly
Sunday Afternoon of his, adding that he would
brand them as contemptible cowards if they did
not attend. So a lot of them came, and when
George was quite through remarking that hotel-
keepers were all hogs and that anyone of them
would make his grandmother drunk to save 3d.,
one publican rose to say a few words in reply.
But immediately he opened his mouth Pastor
Tregear and his wild choir “ beefed out ” a hymn,
and drowned all criticism. Which is so very like
the preacher-man’s idea of a fair deal. He insults
the other fellow if he doesn’t come to his taber-
nacle ; he insults him when he does come; and
his push of hired bellowers is there to drown any
objection the other fellow may want to make over
being insulted.
Three pioneer miners went underground
at Ballarat last week. W. M. Gale was born in
the Isle of Man (where the cats have no tails) 70
years ago, and, after doing some sailoring, came
to the Victorian diggings in 1852. In a general
way of speaking, he bottomed on good stuff, and,
at one time or another, had interests in the Balla-
rat Banking Co., Gas Co., Mining Exchange Co.,
and other concerns. Edward Morey (cetat 75)
arrived in Adelaide in 1853, and moved on to
Ballarat with a mining party of sailors. Later,
he did some exploring in S.A. and Queensland,
and was owner of Linton Park Estate (Vic.). He
became M.L.C. for Wellington Province in 1899,
and was one of the establishers of Ballarat Stock
Exchange. He also found the wash fairly rich.
J. H. Williams came to Ballarat 50 years ago.
He was a director of the old Band and Albion
Consols Co., chairman of the North Woah Hawp
(Ballarat East) for 24 years, and was also asso-
ciated with the New Normanby and other con-
cerns. He helped to organise , the Ballarat
Miners’ Association, and to found the Ballarat
Woollen Mills Co. He died at the age of 83,
after panning out many good prospects, and it is
to be hoped that his experience in the next world
will run at least 3oz. to the ton.
Syd Kidman lately had a horse sale at
Kapunda (S. A.), and the auctioneer got an aver-
age of £l5 a head for 1300 animals, most of them
offered singly. Things have gone well with this
arm of the Kidman
family these last few
years. Besides one or
two not so well known,
there were three
brothers who bega to
get talked about only
12 or 15 years ago—
Sackville, Syd, and
Charley—all wonder-
ful judges of stock,
and fine bushmen.
Sack, reckoned the best
judge of cattle, died ;
and Charley (C.N. of
the turf) went West.
Syd cut in during the
drought, picked out
country that was
having a bad time, and
waited. Soon hestarted
out to advertise him-
self industriously as
“the Cattle King,”
which brought him
both buyers and sel-
lers ; then, picking his
drovers, the miles of
country he had got
hold of made it easy
for him to get the
hoofs to market. His
last purchase made
only the other week—
was Mt. Poole, for
years in the hands of
the Melbourne Langs,
and more or less famous
in the Far West be-
cause it was there that
explorer Sturt did his
historic “ perish.” The
Kidmans are in one
respect not a bit like
Billjim, the typical
Australian bushman.
Billjim can’t talk—the
Kidmans can talk from
Adelaide to Cape
York. And Syd es-
pecially knows the
great art of advt. At
his last big cattle sale in S. Aus., if a beast
ran up beyond the price he reckoned was a
fair thing, he would break in from his perch
alongside the auctioneer: “Fifteen quid! Too
much. Knock a quid off.” That is the sort of
thing to get a man talked of wherever they swap
lies and horses.
Premier Bent having boasted of his old-
time cricketing prowess, some enemy has fished
up an old copy of a “Cricketer’s Guide—Review
of the Season 1857-8 ” wherein appear the batting
averages of the Coast (Elsternwick) Club for
that season. The club comprised G. W.
Rusden, F. M. Selwyn, S. Lindray, F. J.
Price, E. Stephen, J. Goodson, T. Boxhall, two
Huddlestones, three Aggs and John and Thomas
Bent. The highest batting average was owned
by Goodson—ll innings for 165 runs —while
Thomas the Mighty of Old managed, in seven
innings, to knock up 41 runs, which works out at
the magnificent average of 5‘9. Still, this writer’s
record for his whole life was one innings and no
runs at all.
PARSIFAL’S PERILS.
The Methodist Conference passed a resolution warning members of the Church to abstain from dancing,
theatre going, and other questionable amusements, in view of the great moral and spiritual peril attaching
thereto.— Daily Paper.
That’s all right , hut what about the perils that lurk for young Parsifals in tea and
tennis afternoons ?
Plunkett, M.L.A., who bangs the Secesh
drum in the Queensland Assembly, lives at a
place called Tambourine. It’s appropriate, any-
way. The Bulletin never did think that Plun-
kett’s implement sounded important enough to be
a real drum.
Queensland Attorney-General Blair i.s not
obsessed by the dignity of his position. At
Ipswich, where he lives, there is a junior cricket
club named after him—Blair’s Cricket Club—and
“ Jimmy ” (everybody there calls him “Jimmy ”)
plays with it whenever he gets a chance. The
other day, in a match at Rosewood, he took seven
wickets for 28 runs; so his cricket is of the
strenuous variety.
Johnson, the N.S.W. Foreign-trader, has
been asking indignantly in the House of Reps, if
it is really true that the same duty is charged on
gin. lining boards as on gin. lining boards, and, if
so, why don’t the Heavens fall on Bill Lyne.
Yet Johnson is a man who knows so little of
timber that, up in the N. Territory, he sat down
to rest on an alligator thinking it was a tree.
“ Squidge ” holds forth
Little ’Arry VVeedon has been made Gaud Mayor of
Melbourne for the third year in succession. ’Arry, who
is rather bare in the matter of the letter “h,” and who
never finishes his sentences when he talks, represents the
compromise between Orange and Green in the Melbourne
City Council. He’s about the poorest thing in the way
of compromise that it would be possible to find,
but most compromises are. Anyway ’Arry is coming up
the straight for a knighthood. He wants the title
because he' thinks he deserves it. One of his failings
is his inability to see the “ why ”of anything. He thinks
that his financial genius, his bonhomie (he calls bonomi),
and h)s tense grip on public affairs have won him the
respect and affection of the people of Melbourne, and
that they delight to honor him. As a matter of fact, the
people of Melbourne think as much of him as of a fly on
the ceiling of the Town Hall. Still it is remarked on the
housetops that the G.-G. said he couldn’t do it last year,
but that he would do it if ’Arry scored three not out.
’Arry has scored the three, and now the G.-G. is to be
asked to toe the scratch. Four years ago it was 100 to 1
against Weedon ever being Gaud* Mayor. When he was
Gaud Mayor the first time it seemed 500 to 1 he would
never be chosen again. In his second year it looked
10,000 to 1 against a third term. The little G.-G. seems
to have gambled on the 10,000 to 1, and he’s lost. ’Arry
confidently expects to see his name among the birth-
day honors next month, and has got a bust sculped by
John White in anticipation.
“ G.H.” writes about Nelken :
Leo (not Leon) Nelken, whose monster Freetrade dem-
onstration at Sydney Town Hall declined to amount to a
cent, seems to have a Nemesis or some other fatal sub-
stance on his track. Brer Nelken, an American Jew, was
formerly connected with the Melbourne office of a Yankee
life assurance co. ; and in'those days Leo’s bell-topper
shed a solar radiance along that part of the Rue
Collins where the Willie-boys and chorus-girls most do
congregate. Three or four years ago, however, the con-
nection between Leo and the assurance co. was broken or
torn or sawn off or something, and The Block lost sight
for'a space of the ginger-complexioned little man with
the beautiful waist and the impressive hat.
After a while he bobbed up again, but in more subdued
raiment, as the grand chief promoter of Everybody’s of
Australia, a magazine on the same lines, so Nelken said,
as the American Everybody’s. Rumor said that its con-
tents were to flabbergast this continent. Deakin, so
the story ran, was to be editor-in-chief at a salary of
£2,000,000 per annum; W. H. Croker was to hold
forth on butter commissions and deportment; Newton
Griffiths was to, give his views on the Australian stage ;
a dozen other eminent persons were to shed vast quanti-
ties of light on special subjects ; and Mr. Leo Nelken—
the Lawson of Australia—was to give the assurance com-
panies Hell. But a slump occurred. It was rumored that
Deakin stuck out for an extra million per annum, or that
the Assurance companies, terrified by the threatened
revelations, had paid £7,000,000 to squash the project.
Then Brer Nelken—shaking Parliament House off his
feet—became a tender memory and a fleeting dream.
The Town Hall venture, where Leo was billed to heave
language and magic-lantern slides at the Australian
Government, seems to have been trodden on by some in-
visible foot with capital in its hand, and one pictures Leo
as flying before the avenging spectre of Splosh. It is a
pity; for Nelken’s song-and-dance, if it had occurred,
wouldn’t have hurt Australia much.
W. P. Crick rolls his swag for England in
December to argue his appeal before the Privy
Council against the judgment which made
him no longer a Gentlemen One before
N.S.W. Supreme Court. If he appears in the

making holiday in the country he will be:h revela-

probably put an immaculate bell-topper on the
