Schoolmaster G. Metcalfe maintains an
attitude of excitement and applause these days in
consequence of so many of his early pupils having
come to the front in N.B/W. politics; While
facing what he thinks to be an attentive world,
he counts on his fingers J. H. Carrqthers, ex-
Premier; Thomas Waddell, Treasurer; and the
semi-clerical Moore, who, takes over the Lands,
and then he gives three cheers for himself. The
result is fairly good, regarded as a matter of quan-
tity, for one simple bush schoolmaster, but the
quality might be improved upon.
Ebsworth, the Melbourne barrister who
was fined £lO for appearing in a Tas. court
without having bqen admitted to practice in that
small State, was formerly a seaman, and rose to
the rank of second mate before he started study-
ing law. Since he commenced practice in Mel-
bourne, Ebsworth has devoted himself mainly to
nautical matters, and was an eternal thorn in the
side of ex-P.M. Panton. As president of the
Vic. Court of Marine inquiry, Panton was par-
ticularly proud'of his knowledge of things marine,
and every time Ebsworth proved him to be a pit-
fall and a mere longshoreman and wharf lumper
he was really angry.
Died at Clayfield (Brisbane), on September
14, aged 79, Samuel Brown, who had more than
his share of bad luck. He came to Australia in
1849, and after a few months’ jackerooing near
Geelong (Vic.), went- to Queensland with a
brother-in-law who had been managing a station
of Ben Boyd’s, near Bathurst. The partnership
lasted 12 years, and in that time the pair got hold
of five stations. On one of them (Cooroora) 11,000
sheep died in three days in 1875, from cold rain
following upon a drought. Ten years later, on
the same station, Brown lost all his sheep, and
by-and-bye he lost most of his cash through
another drought. Left only with Ingleston, he
had just got things fairly going again when the
bank smash finally ruined him. He then got a
job in the Queensland Lands Department, where,
if he had less cash, he had more peace of mind.
OLD BILL LYNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Written before he backed down on the wire-netting.)
Who is this that, big and burly,
All the time, both late and early—
Be it wet or fine—
Faces all his foes right gamely,
Never takes an insult tamely ?
Why, it’s old Bill Lyne.
Forrest goes, and snarls while leaving ;
Says that Bill is aye deceiving—
Always wants to shine.
Back there comes an answer mocking,
With a noise that’s really shocking.
It’s from old Bill Lyne.
Cook and Reid are always barking,
Wilks, from Dailey, ’s often larking,
Brown and Wilson whine.
But they really do not matter ;
One and all they break and scatter—
Off from old Bill Lyne.
Like a lot of blow-flies droning,
Vou can hear Bill’s foes bemoaning,
Breathing threats condign.
Then they lay a trap to do it;
But their prey just blunders through it;
For it’s old Bill Lyne.
Nights on nights they growl and worry,
Want the tariff in a hurry,
Which is all moonshine.
But one man they cannot bustle,
Doesn’t matter how they hustle,
And it’s old Bill Lyne.
Who is it that’s never tired ?
Always ready when required ?
Stand up, friend o’ mine !
Solid, good, Australian fighter,
May he sit his seat still tighter,
Here’s to old Bill Lyne !
vie.
FRANK RADOLYFFE.
CARRUTHERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the leader of faction and fret
Is driven his post to vacate,
Though the state of his health we regret,
It’s good for the health of his State.
N.S.W.

The celebrated 1900 Vintage of “ Charles
Heidsieck ” Champagne, one of the very best of
late years, is still being shipped to Australia,
though, as stocks are getting low at Reims, the
agents —Messrs. Swift and Co., 32 O’Connell-
street, Sydney—inform us they expect a later
year’s Wine will be shortly coming forward. The
Champagne shipped here is similar to that sent
to London and principal European Cities, Mr.
Charles Heidsieck only vintaging one quality.
It has the Royal Warrant of H.M. King
Edward VII., and is supplied to the House of
'Commons,. British Government, etc.*

Cattle-duffing isn’t wholly a lost art. Ivan
Solly (15| years) burgled a heifer off one of Ade-
laide’s parks recently, and led it to the saleyards.
The animal fetched £llss. Flushed with victory,
Ivan snared a gelding (value £7 10s.), and, if a
policeman hadn’t arrested him about here, it is
quite possible he would have taken the loan of a
lion out of the Zoo or borrowed a tramcar and its
team.
SUPERFLUOUS HAlR.— This is the most dis-
tressing disfigurement a woman can have. Other-
wise beautiful women are made utterly miserable
because of this unhappy affliction. “ Rusmo ”is
the best hair killer in the world. It won’t, mind
you, remove those little hairs for ever and ever —
only electricity will do this—but Rusmo Hair
Killer will certainly make them instantly dis-
appear for the time, and so weaken their growth
that they will be slow to return. This alone is
great relief. Not only that, but all that is neces-
sary can be done at home in very simple fashion,
and in absolute privacy. Rusmo costs ss. 3d. a bottle.
It can be ordered from any chemist, or will be
supplied post free by Mile. Rubinstein, the Yalaze
Massage Institute, 158 Pitt-street, Sydney; or 274
Collins-street, Melbourne.*

SEMPER IDEM
FACILE PR/NCEPS
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 15, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
It has come at last. In a Melb. suburban court
case between mistress and servant, the other day,
the servant complained that her mistress had
made her bad-tempered by going out in her (the
servant’s) best dress.
Face decoration in all its forms of powdering,
panelling, fleur-de-lis or fleur-de-riz, and other
tracery, must have gone out of fashion here, as it
has in London—that is, amongst the Somebodies,
and the Anybodies who are so nearly Somebodies
that they can cut the Nobodies. A friend in the
mid stream of the society whirlers denounced of
Father Vaughan, writes me that the smartest
women in Bull Town have given up tapestry of
the face, and just dab the countenance with elder-
flower water, and afterwards dust it with
powdered oatmeal. At our recent bunch of
A.J.C. races our Australasian women wore the
complexions which it has pleased God to give
or leave them, Mostly they showed frank
signs of bereavement of the great ornament.
We must be living into an honester, uglier
time. Stage stars are noted for their no-paint-out-
of-trade-hours creed. Mrs. Maud Jeffries-Osborne
“ never put on any ” when off the stage, and now
her complexion is of paper whiteness. Her friends
wish that she would consume large quantities of
chops and claret, or else that some of her hus-
band’s complexion were catching. The Osbornes
are a ruddy clan, like the men in boots who came
over the Himalayas and conquered the men in
petticoats. Maud’s fair face got a still colder and
more marble-like tone from the deep agonised
mauve of her hat. Her raiment was otherwise in
tender, faint tones of mauve. But the super-
mauve roof was calamitous.
Miss Rawson’s frocking is getting so smart that
perhaps the First Girl in the State is beginning
to take notice. At the garden party given by her
father and self to taper off the race week, it was an
inspired confection of white crespe-de-chine,
Limerick lace, and satin, embroidered with roses
in pastel shades of silk. A big picture-hat, with
mauve and white flowers, was in accord with the
symphony.
A clutch of girls, who would be quite eligible
for The Lone Hand Beauty Quest, prankt the
lawns at the garden party (I hope “prankt” is a
suitable verb, though “prank,” as a proper word, is
scarcely realisable). Among them was the Dorothy
Bedford girl—she is niece of Mrs. Monty Severn.
Her frocking was in a diaphanous silky thing of
the shade known as honeysuckle yellow—when
the ’suckle withers, probably. It was more a
light bee shade. The bee carries several subtle
variations of yellow about its hardworking
person. Anyhow it was a sweet thing in yellow,
and the girl in it had yellow hair, and turquoises
in her ears. Then there were the Ina Cornish
girl, a packet of the S. W. Moore girls, and a
bunch of young married women—all eligible for
the Beauty Quest.
Heliotrope shows up well against green lawns
but there’s so much of that shade trailing over
the face of Nature that one wishes helio were
“off.” Rose du Barri, being a difficult shade of
pink to wear, gets a small innings and a lot of
notice (from the woman who can’t wear it). Mrs.
Reggie Broomfield, who looks as if she’d fed on
chocolates and coffee, easily stood the Rose du
Barri test.
At the vice-regal garden party Miss Beatrice
Grimshaw, the writer, took the collective eye in
grey chiffon, old honiton lace, and a black hat.
She is a slightly smaller daughter of Anak than
our other distinguished visitor, Clara Butt, but
she is distinctly a member of the Anak family.
“Midnight Wedding” Maud Mclntosh is
proving herself a dresser of the first grade. She
doesn’t greatly fancy “the eternal charm of
fluffiness,” and seems to cling to the clothes that
cling to her, rather than to those that billow and
float abroad on lawns and summer winds and into
the faces of passers-by.
On Saturday, at Randwick, the sportive wind—
not a breeze, but a bold, vulgar, gusty wind—
blew a damsel’s feather boa twice around a
stranger-man’s neck. It was a fluffy enough
anaconda to start with, but to make it more
frivolous, she had put small winged things like
day-old chickens at intervals in the three yards of
feathery fluff. The noosed male looked em-
barrassed. The damsel did as most women do
under such circumstances —she took on an expres-
sion of hostile propriety, as if to say, “You dare
to think that my intentions are not strictly
honorable ! ” Who was it said that “ woman is the
last thing to be civilised by man”? I wish he’d hurry
his civilising measures. On the crowded grand
stand on Saturday I moved to make room for a
lady (in black grenadine over white silk) whom I
mistook for an acquaintance, and she halted, gave
me an outraged eye, and passed on to (I am sure)
summon a policeman or consult “Etiquette for
Australians.”
But to get back to M. Me. Her achievement
on Saturday on the Randwick stage was an Irish
crochet gown with a dado of painted silk, and in
her close-fitting toque were’ three big rosettes of
tulle (or net) in black, petrol green, and
thundercloud blue. It was a really stormy
combination. A kind friend told me on
leaving the course that she had found out the
whatness of the muddy, bilious-looking “ petrol ”
green. It is the color of the oil of that name—
the oil itself, not the smell; the smell is brown.
I believe a chauffeur told her. The motor-car and
its etceteras have got into literature and the
fashions —to say nothing of the ditches and the
morgue. The black chiffon coat carried by M.
Me. was given her by Mrs. Humphrey Ward when
the artiste played in the novelist’s dramatisation
of her “Eleanore.”
The quantity of lilac muslins and the revival of
China crepe at recent A.J.C. races remind of a
description from Mrs. Waddjngton’s pleasant
memoirs, “First Glimpses of Diplomatic Life,”
lately begun in an American maga. She,
an American (married to a foreign Ambas-
sador), writing in 1857, remarks that she went
to the races “in my lilac muslin with the
three flounces, which we bought in Paris, and my
China shawl.” At a grand ball she wore
her wedding dress, cut low. It had three flounces
of lace. Her bouquet was of pink camellias and
heliotrope. It was very heavy, but smelt sweetly.
One of the noticeable frocks blowing about on
race week was Mrs. Milton King’s grey chiffon
with trails of grey-blue wistaria, embroidered in
raised silk all over the bodice, and on the skirt
hundreds of butterflies—a visitation of butterflies
of which no two were alike. It w 7 as the most
striped week in anyone’s recollection—every
third girl or woman went in for zebra effects
either in hair-line or stripes wide as a footpath.
Not many novelties were abroad. A stage lady
carried a Jap paper umbrella. Miss Gwladys
Bonsor, who was a “ striper,” had one of the new
throatlets made of lace and muslin thrust through
an ornamental slide of enamel and turquoise.
Mrs. Clara Butt is complaining to her entourage
(that’s a good word) that she is wilting under the
strain of being entertained. She finds it more of
a burden than subduing the Town Hall organ
with her Voice. Yesterday the Women’s Liberal
Club gave the melodious giantess a reception at
their difficult -to - find rooms in King-street.
Beautiful Butt stumbled in wearingsomethingblue,
silken and expensive, punctuated with guipure
and silver, and a big, inverted-bowl hat on the
back of her hair. When standing in repose the
artiste is that rare bird—a very tall woman who
isquite graceful. Shehas nohint of awkwardness or
stoop, but her walk is a stumble. Her audiences
in or out of the Town Hall can’t get a glimpse of
her feet, so long in front are her gowns. The Liberal
Club ladies justified their name by giving the
artiste a case of six gold teaspoons with Australian
fauna wrought on the handles. The emu tea-
spoon, the kangaroo teaspoon, and the ’possum
teaspoon—these will do for (respectively) little
Joy, Roy, and Toy, for so the Large Lady desig-
nates her young family. The other three tea-
spoons, platypus, magpie, and wallaby, will come
in handy for little Boy, Coy, and MarkFoy, when
they arrive. I make the Singer a present of these
words. If she wants rhyming names of one
syllable she’ll have to stop at the last, for there’s
a limited choice.
For the rest. I would like to know how rich
you must be before you can get gold teaspoons
and other valuable presentations ? Premieress
Wade, in a lace frock and a pink hat, seemed to
swoon behind the huge presentation bouquet as
she waited for the guest. Mrs. Minter, a charm-
ingly diplomatic and prettily-frocked Clubbite,
gave Mrs. Wade her moral support, while Mrs.
M. Parkes and a colossal-eyed lady named
McKinnon and Senatrix Gould helped in the
spreading of butter over the mammoth song bird.
Four debutantes spread their callow wings at
the Australian Club Ball—Miss Eileen Cumming,
a Rose Bay girl; Miss Phyllis Smith, of Molong ;
Miss Enid Bailey, and Miss Windeyer, of New-
castle. The club is said to have spent £7OO on its
floral decorations alone. If that’s a fib, it is, at
least, a flowery fib, and a bright, cheerful distor-
tion of fact.
The City Council is brooding upon a new
scheme —the erection of women’s waiting and
dressing rooms at the Quay and elsewhere. There
—when there is a there—women might get into
evening dress, or climb out of evening dress and
into “their other clothes,” do their hair, and
primp generally. It is to be hoped the City
Council’s wife or other feminine possessions will
tell it that one woman may occupy one mirror for
three hours, and have a lien on a second for view-
ing her back hair and the hang of her skirt.
Therefore, the Council must put in heaps and
stacks of mirrors. (Besides, you get a reduction
on taking a quantity of mirrors.) Most women
can dress in an hour and a half, but they must
have another hour or more to scrutinise the effect
of the dressing from all the points of the compass.
The other night, in a theatre dressing-room, a
woman held the strategic position opposite the
main mirror for 12 minutes while she put in and
took out her jewelled back-comb, holding a hand-
mirror the while. She tried it high, she tried it
low. There was no other way to try it except
medium, but she stuck and re-stuck until a man
put his head in at the door and said, “ The first
act is half over.” She replied, “ I shall not w 7 ear
the new comb until I have a chance of trying it
on at leisure. Will you put it in your overcoat
pocket ? ” Then she spent another eight minutes
over her theatre-coat, and finally marched into
the circle with one arm only in its sleeve. The
other sleeve she drew under her bare other arm,
doubtless thinking how unconventional and
picturesque she looked. Her troubles about the
play ! The intervals with the lights up was
what she went to the theatre for.
A distinguished visitor is staying with her
married daughter at Mosrnan—Queen Emma of
the New Hebrides. She mostly lives at New
Britain (if these statements won’t fit in with
history and geography, I disclaim all responsi-
bility), and is a most stately lady, who comes to
Sydney to choose fashionable clothes. Her con-
sort is a German gentleman named Kolbe.
While on the subject of potentates: King
George, of Tonga, is thinking of marrying
again, and may come to Auckland or Sydney for
a consort. There was some question of his
acquiring a German wife. He is said to be a
handsome man with more orders and medals on
his chest than the Emperor William. He is 33
years of age, and has been a widower for some
time, Queen Lavinia having left this world in
1902. George’s real name and title is Jioaji Tubou
11. Jioaji Tubou 1., his predecessor and great-
grandfather, tottered on the throne till he was
over 90. The population of the islands, I am
told, is mostly dead, and those who survive are
mostly Wesleyans. Also, British authority runs
the show to a great extent, and Tubou 11. is
rather a shadowy potentate. Therefore there is
less inducement to rush the billet than might be
supposed.
Baronet Wilfrid Peek, who is a passenger to
Sydney per Orontes —as is Dan O’Connor—has
just turned into h's 24t,h venr. His grandfather,
the first baronet, was the head of the firm of
Brothers, tea and coffee dealers and spice mer-
chants, and had an office in Eastcheap, London-
There isn’t any baroness —this over-girled com-
munity will be glad to learn.
Mrs. J. H. P. Murray left for Europe on Satur-
day, with her two children, by the Marathon.
The youngsters are going to be tutored in Switzer-
land, where Mrs. Murray will stay, among lakes
and glaciers, while her husband, Judge Murray,
is wiping the globules of heat from his forehead
in steamy Papua, where he is Administrator.
Mrs. Murray is a member of the Jenkins family.
Her father was a doctor, who wisely gave up
medicine for squatting. The seasons were kind
to him. Two of the big family runs—one in
Queensland and another on the Macintyre River,
in N.S.W.—are now in the market —both freehold
estates, with big musters of stock. Dr. Jenkins,
the well-known Macquarie-street medicine man,
is a son of the original doctor. Another son is
master of the magnificent Stonehenge property.
In the vestibule of N.S.W. State Parliament
House there hangs a portrait group of members of
the first N.S.W. Parliament. In this group figure
Dr. Jenkins and Terence Aubrey Murray, after-
wards Speaker—“ with a voice like a band
of music,” as Dan O’Connor once eloquently
observed—father of the present Papuan Adminis-
trator. The latter was a brilliant Oxford man,
and is master of many tongues. In addition he
is a mighty swordsman, and owns a great silver
cup, recording the fact that he was once the cham-
pion amateur boxer of England. Another brother,
George, smashed almost every scholastic record
at Oxford, was Professor of Greek at Glasgow at
24, married a daughter of a “belted earl,” the
Earl of Carlisle, and now produces Greek plays
for the Court Theatre. Harvard University
recently imported him to talk to the Yanks on
Greek. He has a polished literary manner in
prose and verse.
Millionairess Eadith Walker has earned some
money—los. consolation prize from the Sunday
Times for a line in a Limerick. The line is not
published, but the lady has been given half a
pound for it, and by now has most likely driven
around in her 1000-guinea motor-car to collect.
As it is probably the first money she has ever
earned, the lady will doubtless have it framed, or
something, to make it an heiressloom.
Lord Northcote has put up a Cup for inter-
state yachting contests ; so saltwater sports are
waving their tins with satisfaction, and throwing
garlands of fish and seaweed at his Excellency.
The first instalment of a Dress Symposium in
the November number of The Lone Hand will be
contributed by Miss Grace Palotta, who pleads
for Simplicity. (Accurately, “ Grace Palotta
preaches Simplicity.”) Miss H. C. McG. writes
on “ Dress and the Woman,” and Miss Conor-
O’Brien asks of “ The Hat: Is it a Devil ?”
At the opening of the State Parliament gambol
the new quarters for the Premier made the guests
break into enthusiastic comment that sounded like
a patter song. They are built on to the tennis
lawn in the style of a suburban villa, with a red-
tiled verandah, furnishings in modern style, cool-
looking walls in green and white, cathedral glass,
and a very comfortable bedroom. The walls are
adorned with various pictures, and in the hall are
models of our own particular fish and some
pedestals of Australian woods. These are the
quarters which Mr. Carruthers used to visit while
they were in course of construction, and con-
sider with the eye of the king who builds himself
a new palace. And there, it is said, he often
used to find Mr. M‘Gowen also considering the
premises with the aspect of the heir-apparent
who tags along behind the king when he goes to
look at his new palace, and suggests Death —
political Death.
Miss Daisy Doudney, who last month was
wedded to Mr. Sou tar at St. Philip’s, represented
a family that had quite a habit of going to that
place of worship on that errand. In 1820 her
great-grandfather, Mr. Thomas Glover, was
married in old St. Philip’s by Archdeacon
Cowper. Her grandfather was married at old
St. Philip’s, and her hither at new St. Philip’s.
Thomas Glover owned a property in Kent-street
(near the gasworks) before the street was cut
down, and when it was as humpy as a camel’s
back, and (like the beginning of Genesis) was with-
out form and void. These facts are supplied by an
old gentleman of my acquaintance, and if they
are inaccurate I am not responsible for them.
There are better things sometimes than being a
landowner and aristocrat —however bloated. In
Ireland an an ti-Home Rule peer, by name Ash town,
constitutes a living exemplar of the great truth
that life in the case of the British lord is far, far
from being all strong drink and chorus girl. The
Ashtown peer, who owns 22,000 acres of land in
Galway, arrived on his acres the other day
for some quiet shooting. What occurred? Was
he welcomed? Did the tenantry turn out and
present forelocks? Far from it. The very next
morning a melinite bomb was placed in the
front parlor of the Ashtown humpy and the
entire suite of furniture ruined. When Ash-
town walks about Woodlawn, his estate in
Co. Galway, he is (says Lloyds) “constantly
under police protection.” A constable assists the
family butler to open the hall-door —a job that
needs to be done with infinite caution and much
reconnoitring—and another member of the force
sleeps in a passage near the bloated one’s bedroom
door. When the owner of the surplus mass of
acres in Galway visits the local village for a shave
or anything, “ a policeman armed with a rifle
goes on ahead and another follows on a bicycle
close behind.” The life must be jumpy to a
degree, and recalls the state of affairs the Anti-
Sosh crowd prophesied last year for the Common-
wealth unless G. Reid was immediately made
Prime Minister.
The British Barts, feel they have been put upon
of late, and not given their due meed of respect.
In fact, as a crowd, gang, or conglomeration,
their grievances have for long been as multi-
tudinous and bitter as those of Australia’s
agonised old Ma State herself. They had a meet-
ing the other day (at least some of them had) and
vehemently shook the finger of scorn and dislike
at the type of outcast who is wont to masquerade
as a Bart, without really being one. Also, they
orated longand loudly onother subjects, and passed
resolutions. That meeting wasn’t the Jawbone-
Neild-Nelken sort of fizzle that our anti-tariffists
in these parts are accustoming us to, but has been
productive of a few very useful results. Take, for
example, the dogged attitude it has induced in
him whom Debrett styles “the Hon. Sir S.
Maryon-Wilson, Bart., of Kent.” Some im-
possible creature wrote to “ the Hon. Sir S.” etc.,
and began the screed with the words “Dear Sir”!
Whereat the outraged Bart, administered to the
writer a rebuke, sharp as it was neat. The letter,
says John Bull —an English publication which
has forgotten more about Barts, and knights than
the normal un-Bull-like person ever knew—was
returned, with the word “Dear” struck firmly out
“by a bold red cross.” And the following printed
form was attached :
There is a strong feeling amongst baronets that the
expression “ Dear Sir,” which is constantly used in coin-
men eirig a letter nowadays by, and to, upper servants,
clerks in small offices, and many other persons who are
much below the social status of a baronet, is not only in-
correct and incongruous, but is also very discourteous
when used to Members of this Ancient and Hereditary
Degree. I will therefore ask you in future to kindly com-
mence your letters to me “Sir Baronet” instead of
“ Dear Sir.”
If that doesn’t fix the Barts, in their correct social
niche, nothing will.
“ Mario,” from Brisbane :
The viceroy and his dame, having departed for southern
climes, leaving the Du Oane in charge, that lady, along
with Mrs. Ramsay, of Harrow, shed a faint flicker on the
October meeting of the Q.T.O. on Saturday, when the
premier club ran a sparsely-attended afternoon of sport.
The last race was productive of a mishap that might
easily have been a tragedy. Lismore fell right in the
middle of the ruck. How the other neddies got clear,
and how the rider escaped with a scratch or two, good-
ness only knows.
The mission ffite, which fizzled feebly on the grounds
at Bishopsbourne on Saturdays, was the same old
rehash of the same old faces, and the owners of the fac< s
were done for 9d. in every Is. expended in the same old
way.
There is talk of much trouble in the Central City
Refuge. As the Archbishop is the Chief Executive
Officer, and the President of Committee is an ex-Matron
of the Children’s Hospital, and one at least of the Com-
mittee is a duly'certificated nurse, Heaven alone knows
how the muddlement occurred. The fact remains, how-
ever, that the infant mortality is a staggerer. If the
rumor is true that £7OOO lies at the disposal of those in
authority for improvements, why don’t the reforms get
attended to ?
The knot of the Nelson-Bird alliance was tied in the
sweltering heat of Thursday last at St. Luke’s, with no
end of frill. The wedding toilette was of chiffon gauze over
satin, and veiled with Limerick lace. The two maids,
the Misses O’Reilly, budded in girly-girly white muslin
frocks, carrying baskets of pink sweet pea, and were just
the tiling. Bride and groom are now residing at the
Hotel Australia.
The Children’s Ball, in aid of the procrastinating
Children’s Hospital, was a dead frost, which not even the
presence of the vice-regal buds in floral array could
thaw.
“ Rosna” writes from Adelaide :
The Hunt Club races were a great and festive picnic.
All the pedigreed folk took the best table-linen out of
its glass case, filled up the motor with champagne and
oysters, and had luncheon in picturesque and jural
simplicity. When you are accustomed, as we in Adelaide
are, to 16 powdered footmen behind your chair o’ meals,
it is great fun to pass the salad personally by hand, and
rescue cross and drowning spiders from the dressing.
There was some goodly frocking abroad, mostly of the
pinafore order, with millinery flower-laden, and lurching
weirdly over the back-hair. Lady 7 Le Hunte was there
in a pretty tussore silk and cream toque, with Miss Clowes
in blue and white muslin. Mrs. Richard Hawker, chief
hostess of the affair, wore cream silk muslin ; and Mrs.
Albion Tolley was in dreamy, flowery, chiffony raiment.
Mrs. J. H. A. Lee decorated a navy silk gown and green
toque, and Mrs. L. Bonython a very glorious erection of
pale blue, embroidered silk, with a blue and pink crown-
ing glory. One of the most striking frocks was a browny-
blue shot silk, worn by Mrs. Bosley Jenkins, who had
some long waving feathers in her brown hat.
Mrs. William Campbell, Mrs. Lendon, Mrs. Wigg, and
Mrs. Gill collaborated in a fancy-dress ball, the other
evening, for the gilded infants of the State. (I use the
term infants in its legal and not literal sense.) There
were many quaintly-garbed kiddies, and one or two quite
gorgeously arrayed. The two little Poulton girls were
short-waisted, early Victorian maidens, quaintly coiffed.
Dorothy Lendon made an adorable Marquise. Alice
Campbell, resplendent in yellow satin and a plumed hat,
was a tall and graceful Anne Hyde (she who married
James 11. or one of the other Rexes of Britain), and
wielded her lengthy train most scientifically 7. Dorothy
Young, in a wisteria-embroidered kimona, made the Jap-
piest of little Japs. The young he-population went
mostly as bushrangers and other bloodthirsty ruffians.
Their Exes, have been rusticating with the C. 11.
Angases at Lindsay Park, where the sheep grow (or is it
kine ?). The place really has a park, and is not called one
because there is a loquat tree in the middle of the lawn.
From there his Ex. took an exciting drive or two to the
neighboring townships, surveyed the wheat crops, patted
the local schools on the head, and had the “Song of Aus-
tralia ” sung at him.
The Herbert Rymills are rejoicing over the arrival of a
son and heir, which reminds me that Adelaide artist
Hayley Lever has laden his defenceless infant with the
names “Elener Domenico.” Truly these are splendid
encumbrances. The sponsors are, I believe, a distinguished
painter and a celebrated musician, and if Elener Domenico
ever manages to stagger to manhood he will certainly be
expected to wear long hair and a blue sash.
Lizzie Newbold fell in for a piece of good fortune when
she rushed home from these parts to warble her best bird
notes for Melba the Glorious. The expressed the
opinion that the youthful nightingale’s voice is a charm-
ing one, and forthwith sat herself down and gave her a
20-minutes lesson, and requested to hear her again in a
month’s time. When one remembers the hundreds of
would-be songstresses who would give their ears to sing a
line for Melba, and the horde of clamoring parents who
beset the path of that good-natured Queen of Song in
the hope of enlisting her interest, one may conclude that
the little Newbold girl was considerably in luck. I read
somewhere that in Edward’s Isle Melba often has as
many as 200 requests a day from people who only want
her to hear their top G’s, and give an opinion on ’em.
And this record is bound to be beaten in Australia,
where voices grow wild on the trees and hedges.
Yours Affectionately, AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 14, 1907.
My Dear Yarrik, —
A very famous Australienne considers the
dubious joys of matrimony—at least it is so
alleged. The happy man (so they say), who may
have the privilege of being curtain-lectured by
a voice more precious than a King’s ransom, is a
widower—a Roman-nosed widower, with a fortune
and a cherub or two as mementoes of his defunct
wife. He dances constant courtly attendance
upon his divinity, which proceedings she tolerates
with intoxicating grace. But only to a grand
attack will the goddess capitulate. It must be
hard to be masterful to a singing angel, yet that’s
the only way to capture women for slaves and
arch deities for wives.
Lady Northcote gave a charming musical “ At
Home” on Friday. Music and chatter were
softly wedded in one of the prettiest of the draw-
ing-rooms—with big, comfy blue-clad lounges in
serried sedateness from one end to the other and a
church aisle between. Mr. Clarionet Mortimer,
Elvins at the piano, played a concerto.
1 hen Miss Carmen Pinschof and Miss Hesketh
Jones, backed by Mr. Wade and Mr. Dun-
lop, sang the Brahms “ Waltzes.” Sweet
songs they are, and the voices were as
sweet. Carmen Pinschof looked mightily pretty,
too, with the soft light from a stained-glass
window behind her, and a halo-hat of sea-colored
silk, and huge, fluffy, soft white plumes like
spindrift.
After the waltzes came an interval, and
there a was frilly, fluffy rustle into tea. No
buffet scramble, but just the billiard-room full of
dainty tables, and the windows wide open to the
terrace and lawn. The Northcote dame has a
fine sense of decoration, and is fond of using wild
flowers for adornment. The room was full of
wild boronia, with its fine fragrance and ivory-
tinted flowers.
After tea, the company drifted back for more
music. Heard some of the men-folk whispering
anxiously if it were “ polite to go now ? ” But
they were hauled back to the blue seats, and
forced to listen to ‘‘Trombone Trios.” But
things brightened considerably when some very
pretty girls, accompanied by more pretty girls at
and piano, sang something delicate about
Snow.” It is a cantata with which the
Exhibition Choir intends to bombard an audience
later.
The Caulfield Guineas struggled hard for its
usual weather—which is Bad—but resolved itself
into quite a passable afternoon. For half-an-hour
or so the frilly frockers bloomed about the lawn
like roses, while the tailor-mades sweltered and
wondered why they hadn’t frocked. Suddenly a
squalling, shrieking wind would blast—quite im-
promptu. Then was the triumphant hour of the
tailor-mades, who smiled complacently at the
awful exhibition of unhappy swirl among the
flimsies.
The authorities have put a fresh back to the
vice-regal pen. Now it is as sheltered and deep
as a nice sleepy family pew in an ancient church,
ihe Northcote Lady topped her powder blue
draperies with funereal black plumes. Mrs.
Vallange was all sweet and droopy in brown, with
floating fringe like seaweed ; even her hat had a
shimmering, drooping, sea-plant air. It was
brown, with soft dead pinks and blues
ana greens in the osprey drooping in the
fashionable tilt over one ear. Ruby Madden
was just as assertive as Sister Vallange was
pensive. She wore oranges in her huge
hat; startling big golden oranges. The
youthful Leslie maiden draped herself ,in a
much-trimmed matronly-looking black dress,
with a rather squelching black hat and
the snow-streak hair well in evidence. Mrs.
“Bob” Power (her lioness was not there) was
quite subdued in cream color. She had such a
pretty girl with her part of the day—a girl with
a small dark face framed in a grey veil, and her
slim body tailored in pale grey. One could hear
Mrs. Norton Grim wade’s suit round the corner ;
the check was a .frantic shout and the hat a
hurrah. The frame, like a cartwheel, held purple
plumes standing on end in unholy horror, and the
rest of the fixings were fuchsias—lurid red and
purple fuchsias. The Manifolds were in quiet
evidence. Popper’s horse Subterranean did not do
what was expected of it at the Guineas; but there’s
lots of talk for the Cup. How giddy some grey-
haired folk get in race gowns ! Mrs. Harold Kent,
tall and dignified, wore a toque with horrible
indigestible green cherries bobbing and dangling
in her eyes and over her ears, and, flying in long
streamers, was scarlet ribbon, just to contrast the
unripeness of the cherry bobs. Mrs. Justice
Isaacs flaunted her handsome Oriental beauty in a
sort of harvest-home dress, with sheaves of
wheat bang over the fashionable ear.
Mrs. “Bob” Power combined Bridge and
Melba as a powerful attraction on Friday even-
ing. Just a very swagger party, and Melba sang.
Mrs “Bob” is a real general. Her lions always
turn up, and they always roar. She has never
been known to fail. A rude man had a heavy bet
on the Melba card being successfully played, and
he lost badly. The diva sang and sang, perhaps
ten or a dozen songs, with delightful bird-like
abandon. “The Spring is here —the Spring is
here —and I sing” was the general tone of her
unbending. No doling out in short-cut perform-
ances.
Mrs. Stewart Balmain, lately the large and
lovely Miss Alison Fairbairn, has presented a
brand-new Balmain to the world at large. It is
of the selfish gender. Mrs. Balmain is up at a
Hill station, not far from Simla, where her lord
is with his regiment. Mamma and papa George
Fairbairn will probably fly off to see the new
grandson as soon as the House of Reps, goes into
recess.
That interesting daughter of our somewhat old
nobility, Miss Phyllis Clarke, moves quietly about
town, looking out at this new world with wide
and serious eyes. Under her comely aunt’s guid-
ance, she explores sassiety occasionally ; but for
the most part the plump little person wanders in
palpable search of inspiration. The damsel is
earnest-minded, and desires that her gold-mounted
pen may one day scrawl her into fame. Already
she has given one of her literary experiments
to a local editor, who loudly declares that he finds
much merit in it. Pa Rupert watches his offspring
in a surprised sort of way.
The French Ball —the annual ball of the Alliance
—was a gorgeous shout of the Tricolor. The ladies
of the club spent hours over the decorations them-
selves, instead of leaving the scheme to an idea-
exhausted florist. That made for originality.
An army of little red-white-and-blue sailors from
the French boat Oceanien were attached as
helpers. There’s a good deal of life in a French
dance, and it has quite a bubbling, champagny
stir about it all the time. Even the arriving
element seemed to froth and frill and billow out
of cabs and carriages in a style of its own. And
the advance down the long entrance hall ! The
Englishwomen seemed to sweep along with a long-
drawn swish of drapery—serenely, like ships in
full sail. The Frenchwomen literally danced
along with lovely frills perked at Frenehy angles
of jauntiness. Military men were much in evi-
dence to add to the gaiety of color.
Mrs. Holyrood, wife of the ex-Justice, was
there—beaming and smiling through the whole
evening. Such an energetic old lady she is—
scarcely ever misses a social function. Mrs.
Masseran and Miss Bruggmann electrified a bevy
of steward-acting boys into the most active ser-
vice, and wall-flowers were cut out of the decora-
tions.
A French girl who danced beautifully, and was
much rushed by partners, looked suprisingly like
La Sylphe. who undulates at Rickards’. Notice-
able was Mrs. Morris Mendes—beautiful, Oriental
looking, and perfectly dressed in black and white,
with a comet’s tail streaking from the dusk of
her hair. She was one of the handsome Sullivan
chicks before the wealthy confectionery Mendes
annexed her. Pretty little blue-eyed Louise
Smith chattered in French nearly all the evening.
She is a clever linguist and a devotee of the serious
literary evenings of the Alliance.
One Frenchily-coiffured madam had • horribly
expensive lily-of-the-valley in her hair (it costs
about a shilling a bud so early). Each bloom was
skewered through with wire, and looked like a
fat white lawn grub.
Rumor declares, with every appearance of
authority, that an eagerly-looked-for divorce case,
in which the star performers are all young and
sparkling, is not to be staged this season.
Whereat the good-natured friends of the trio are
much distressed. (A scandal gives such a splendid
opportunity to one’s friends to show sympathy
and say “I told you so,” or “I always thought
so ! ”) The husband who thought himself wronged
finds that the wife, if she wronged him, didn’t do
it ostentatiously. Also the supposed villain of the
piece promises to prove an alibi for every hour
spent, according to half-a-dozen highly-paid ’tecs,
in dalliance with the lady. And the husband,
remembering that a ’tec is paid to find out some-
thing, and, therefore, has to find it out or lose his
reputation for keenness, reflects, and decides that
it is better not to bring the accusation into court.
“ Tryphena ” from Paris :
A study of the French Fifi after a close acquaintance with
the English miss is instructive and amusing. Fifi is an
artiste—or a genius, rather—in her infinite capacity for
taking pains. In the adjustment of a shoe-lace, in the
tilt of her hat, in the knot of her motor-scarf, in the grip
of her waist buckle, in the crimp of her back-hair, in the
hang of her gossamer, in the great scheme of the com-
plexion, in the gleam of her ivories, in the pencilled arch
of her brow, she is the consummate genius. See her glide
into a cafb. No serpent of the Nile ever slid into notice
so fascinatingly. There’s no frou-frou about her raiment.
She rustles not, neither does she jingle. There’s a soft,
swift stealth about all her movements. She seems to be
gliding into the cafb with the wish to be unobserved.
That is her naivete. There’s a gentle look in her varn-
ished and belladonnaed eye. That also is her naivete.
There’s a severe limit to her smile—at first; that also is
her naivete. But no one is taken in for long. If Monsieur
—any Monsieur—does not materialise after the hors
d’ ceuvre or before she starts to nibble her wing of poulet,
Fifi starts a flirtation with the gargon and insists on his
being amused. Then she clatters and chirps like a canary
whose cage hangs in the rain, or a sparrow at spring bed-
time. Her teeth, her hands, her eyes, her millinery are
all factors in her animation.
Fifi is distinctly not to be encouraged. But she look*
alluringly sweet, and the hundred and one details of her
get-up put the globe-trotter under a partial spell. To sit
and watch the play of Fifi’s hands, to see her gleaming
eyes, to follow the line of pearl at her smiling, to take in
the airs and graces of her vivacity is to be fascinated into
all forgetfulness of food and drink. But it’s dangerous.
Fifi is quite equal to an audacious remark. So you wake
up and attack your turbot with gusto. And you think
on Fifi’s charm and all-conquering style, andknow why she
scores above the English miss. It is her capacity for taking-
pains over the details—the little etceteras of her toilette.
Angelina, of Merrie England, has no thought for
detail. She pays like a princess for a creation—a frock
of expression—and has it hooked and eyed on by an
attentive Hebe. Then she has her hat skewered on, and
she considers she’ll do. There’s no self-consciousness—
none of the microscopic vanities—about Angelina. She
doesn’t know in what she’s lacking. She doesn’t study
the line of her eyebrows, or the height of her ears, or
the length of her smile, or the shape of her temper.
She doesn’t look in a series of profile and back-view
mirrors for the line of figure or head from all
points of a beholder. She doesn’t weigh the
value of a darkened eyelash. She wouldn’t deign to con-
sider the seductiveness of a rouged lip. She hasn’t any
thought for the play of a lace hanky or the pinking of her
filbert nails. She doesn’t act her exits and entrances in
a hotel or cafe or theatre. She wouldn’t dream of turn-
ing to inquisitive view the better side of her countenance
(there is always a good and a bad or a medium side to the
human dial). Angelina is too proud to get up to all these
tricks. There’s no emotion in her dressing.
Women golfers are rushing the special Tourna-
ment for the sake of the permission to play at the
severely masculine Victoria Links. It’s “the
grass in the other paddock ” again, also the
distant hill that looks green, and the distant
uncle who looks affluent. The Victorias are
utterly undesirable as links go—dusty, dirty,
hard to reach. But so far they are the only ones
forbidden to women, and Eve is resolved to have
the apple or know the reason why.
Cards are fluttering out for the biggest thing in
naval and military balls. ’Tis to be on Friday
night-before-Derby, and it takes fully half-an-
hour to read the invitation card—the list of hosts
is so grandiloquent and the “to meet ” takes up
another half-dozen lines, and includes a Governor
or so, and the Admiral and other race visitors.
In fact, about 300 people invite Smith to meet
about 400 people, and Smith naturally feels as if
he were the pea that one elephant is pointing out
to another. The event is called a ball, so natu-
rally one expects dancing. But everybody gold-
laced in military and naval affairs (from every
otate) will be there. Then a solid wedge of 300'
cards go forth to Government people before the
list niters down to Smith, the mild and numerous,
ordinary blaclc-clad citizen. And it looks as if
there wouldn’t be much room for fantastic gyra-
tions and saltatory exercise.
By the time the Women’s Exhibition opens and.
bursts into bloom there will be many petals bat-
tered off by the storms. The din and clash of
battle rage round the Ex. like wild animals on a.
verandah. A few days ago the Women’s choir—
the whole 1300 of ’em—resigned in one unani-
mous top-note. The management hurriedly re-
considered the point at issue, and the 1300 white-
clad chookies fluttered back to their perch. Gentle
little Mrs. “ Prof.” Peterson, who has been going
through whirlwinds as conductor, tutoring the
choir for months back, was suddenly to be de-
posed, and Mrs. Hewett was to inherit the ebony
stick. Mrs. Hewett has written an Ode for the
choir. Mrs. Peterson gracefully suggested that
the lady should conduct her own ode, and the inch
was being stretched to so many yards that it
began to appear that there would be nothing left
for Mrs. Peterson to conduct. The lady, however,
again has the black stick in her correct white-
gloved hands.
Further, all the Tea ladies have resigned and
refused to feed the hungry herd at Our Exhibi-
tion, and now some common or garden man-
caterer is pushing his Masculinity into the Femi-
nine show. Three or so of the most swagger she-
caterers were to divide the work, and the waitresses
were to be “ quite naice ” people and the prettiest
girls in Melbourne. Now, for some reason, the
three Tea madams have sat them down suddenly,
firmly, flatly, indignantly, and the Big Order has
shattered to fragments. And the damage won’t
come out of Mary Ann’s wages either.
There was a party with a certain element of
quaintness at Bishopscourt last week. His
gaiters, Lowther Clarke, and his helpmate in-
vited the Anglican Church and its wife to meet
all the available vice-regalities who dawdle in
Melbourne for the moment. The Federal pair,
with acting-Governor Madden and his lady,
arrived early, and nobly did their duty to the
Church. It was an edifying sight to see the
mouse-like missuses of the rural clergy exchanging
ideas and observations with worthy citizens who
ordinarily confine their interests to Cup weights
and chorus lights. And the Tooraketty folk who
spent a serious afternoon among the herd of godly
black-coats, questioning them upon creche man-
agement and foreign missions—they, too, were a
delight to the cynical. Mrs. Lowther Clarke did
her duty to the archdiocese in grey silk and black
satin, while her energetic daughter moved about
in pale grey voile. Clerical wives wore modest
colors, and lay wives wore their second-best party
frocks.
The Melbourne Bicycle Club will hold its big
nieeting this year at the M.C.C. Ground ; which
is a welcome change after suffering Austral days
at the Exhibition. The club is waking up to the
necessity for new attractions, and valiantly
searches for them. It offers a prize of £lOO (more
or less) to anyone who suggests a novel feature.
It needn’t relate to a cycle whizz —it need only be
original, and the right kind of original. So far,
secretary McCullagh has received nearly 1000
letters; but never a writer in the thousand has
proposed a plan that would amuse even a babbling
infant. However, there is still a month during
which the nimble-minded may conceive and publish
a scheme worth paying for.
The Mooltan brings the Ernest Keep couple
and their bud home in time for Cup week gaieties.
Madam always may be depended upon to adorn a
festivity with her bright humor and her most
decorative figure and frillings.
The Hickman Molesworths are disposing of
their household gods, preparatory to a flight to
England. Meantime they stay at the George
Hotel. It’s improbable that they’ll return to Aus-
tralia again, except as visitors.
Last week there arrived here, unheralded and
without a brass band, a man with claims to fame.
He is Hornell, the Scawtch painter. He is a
quiet, grey notability, with a huge store of infor-
mation, a Doric burr, and an eye hungry for
fresh beauties, and he enthuses in a sane way
over the wonders of Australia. Mr. Hornell
is conspicuous as a painter of genre pictures.
Children are his pet subjects, and he shows them
as miracles of truthfulness —little men and women
whose significance and humanity are painted with
as much success as their dirt or their starchy
frills. In spite of his silent coming, the Edin-
boro’ master is being besieged by anxious artists,
eager for admiration or admonition (which he
gives with superb candor). He goes to Sydney
anon, then further afield to the real Australia
Felix.
Miss Ida Gresham had a pitiful experience last
week. Her mother, a charming old lady of much
interest, died during an afternoon. That night,
as no understudy was sufficiently prepared, the
daughter had to play her part, as a reckless lady
of bad morals, in “Why Woman Sins,” with
the Holloway co.
The Greatest Show on Earth calls itself “ Wirth
Brothers.” To my thinking, it should be “ Wirth
Brothers and Mrs. Wirth”—or something even
more prominent for the lady. Mrs. Wirth is a
young woman of much moment to that show.
She travels everywhere with it, and personally
sees to all the gay dressing. The circus will open
this month. For 16 solid weeks Mrs. Wirth has
been settled in Melbourne working like a pretty,
fair-haired tiger, with many assistants, at miles
of gorgeous dresses and tapestry. They include
not only cunning costumes for acrobats and tight-
rope walkers, and ladies who jump through
blazing rings, and maidens who ride on tigers,
but also triumphant trappings for the elephants
and proud horses and camels (the difficulty of
fitting a camel’s hump is considerable, and a
camel in slop clothes looks untidy). There is a
mighty gold-studded velvet dress for each of the
eight elephants—Topsy, Glory, Plodder, and the
rest.
Mrs. Wirth is an Englishwoman—of real Saxon
fairness. Her husband and brother-in-law are
Australians.
Princes Court is arrayed again in full armor for
the summer campaign. On Friday evening, a
Lord Mayor or so and many Importants gathered
by special invitation to see the new Roller Rink
and various other novelty shows that are to
act as sugar to the swarming summer ants. An
expert rinker cut out some fancy work on the new
floor.
Mrs. Ada Whiting is showing some exquisite
miniatures—intended for the Women’s Exhibi-
tion. The clever little artist’s most immediate
work consists of ivory miniatures of Melba and
Melba s son and Melba’s son’s wife. This im-
pressive order has just descended upon her. Mrs.
Whiting painted a miniature of Melba in Paris.
Yours affectionately, JOHANNA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Quite the nastiest criticism Carruthers has
yet endured was offered by the Camden Park and
Waverley Onslow, in N.S.W. Assembly, last
week. Owe’Sullivan was referring in bitter tones
to the late wire-netting steal, when thus suddenly
the person of many names and tenants
It was the best thing Carruthers ever did !
For a Premier to work himself to a standstill in a
frantic attempt to hold on to office, and then to
have the identical ghastly mistake that led to his
being forced to chuck the said office blazoned, by
an alleged supporter, as the most satisfactory per-
formance of his administration, is cruel.
GRUMBLERS' CAVERN.
Mr. Irvine is one of those nondescripts who have
formed a little cave into which they invite any of the
elements of discontent to enter.—Age.
The two ways of putting it. Sydney
Evening News had to announce the other day
that Bryant and May had decided to “ erect a
large factory in Australia for the manufacture of
wax vestas, matches, etc., in consequence of the
Protective duty.” It printed that among the
puff-paragraphs about somebody’s ear-drums,
under this bald heading :
Locally Made Matches.
But it also had to announce that bottled English
beer had gone up from 11s. 3d. to 12s. per dozen ;
and that got under these headings
The Poor Man’s Beer.
Prices Gone Up in Sydney.
The Reason Why.
Another Effect of the Tariff.
And the once Protectionist Star—well, the Star
was much too busy announcing that Bill Squires
would stay in America till he could return to
Australia a winner to concern itself about such a
small thing as battling for Australian industry.
London cable :
_The priests at Ispahan, in Persia, are preaching a boy-
cott of foreign goods, and trade is at a standstill. All
the roads in the vicinity of the city are in the hands of
brigands.
It looks from that as though the Persian importer
had turned quite naturally to the other branch of
his profession.
Vic. M.L.A. Bromley has given notice of
an amendment to the Factories Bill, to the effect
that Tramway employees may have a Wages
Board if they apply for it. It is long odds that
the motion will never come to a vote in the pre-
sent Assembly; but, if it should, the division list
will be worth studying.
Bent’s thoughts must have been running
on rural subjects the other day, when a railway
deputation called on him. “Look here,” roared
the King of Victoria, when the plot had de-
veloped and things were warmed, “ I’m going to
pull those Commissioners up.” If Bent had been
a selector in his youth, he probably would have
threatened to burn them off or something like
that, but he didn’t —he said he’d pull them up,
same as vegetables at Brighton.
‘‘ Nawman ” Bayles, M.L.A. for Toorak
(Vic.), has decent impulses sometimes. While
his dreary Tory friends were opposing the exten-
sion of Wages Board possibilities to carters and
drivers, Nawman spoke up. “I’ll support it,”
quoth he ; “ there’s a paw chap leaves parcels at
mai haouse up to eleven at naight, and I think it’s
a shame that the paw beggah should have to work
so late.” Nawman’s liberal tendencies were a bit
patronising, but he got there just the same.
“In view of its inquisitorial and un-
British character,” says the Herald on the
NvS.W. Government programme, “the income
tax as a whole might, and in our present circum-
stances could, disappear with general benefit.”
Half-a-dozen lines lower in the same leader the
same Herald complains that Wade, by proposing
to only “ materially ” reduce the tax on earned
incomes, is falling back upon a British precedent.
Wade has already begun to laugh at the Herald
as a political teacher. He is un-British one
minute’ and pro-British the next. To save the
Fairfaxes from income-tax is evidently exciting
work, and makes the leader-writer dizzy.
Certain people are very mad about the
results of the Local Option vote in N.S.W., and a
great effort will probably be made toabolish the new
practice of taking the Cold Tea poll and the State
Parliamentary poll together. The new departure
had the good effect of making Local Option a live
question and gathering in a big vote. But, on
the other hand, it so filled the campaign with
beer clamor and anti-beer clamor that some poli-
ticians who were not beer clamorers could hardly
make themselves heard above the din. And
when they think of what the public lost through
not hearing them, they are sorry for the public.
All through the unsatisfactory debate in
the Reps, on the wire-netting duty the Foreign-
traders scraped away on the one string—“We
mustn’t handicap the primary producer.” They
silenced the coke industry with that gag for
years ; and in 1891 the great primary industry of
mining had to pay £4 17s. 6d. per ton for the
186,060 tons of coke it had to import. Last year,
after a duty had set the coke business on its feet,
only 6104 tons were imported, and the Australian
coke works’ average price for coke was 11s. lid.
So the duty supplied Australian works, and the
Australian works supply eight tons of coke for
what the Australian used to pay for one ton.
That is how protective duties “ handicap ” the
primary producer.
“ W.” : The worst of that Newfoundland
business (B. 3/10/’O7) is that, after promising that
nothing should be done without full
consultation with the little island, the
British Government framed the Con-
vention with Uncle Sam right over
its head. Had it not knuckled down,
Bull would probably have sent a cruiser
to enforce the agreement as against the
local laws, which have now been
“voluntarily” suspended. The calm
ignoring of Newfoundland in the nego-
tiations was abominably like the treat-
ment of Australia in the matter of the
New Hebrides. One of these days we
are likely to find Australian law being
declared from the deck of the Pa’hful,
instead of from the office of A. Deakin,
or J. C. Watson, or whoever happens
to be running this insignificant country
at the moment. The Great Power which
tumbled to its knees before the U.S. A.
over both the Jamaica and Newfound-
land incidents is just as liable to find
the dust with its stomach when some-
body else—even a Tailed somebody—
talks truculently.
G. H. Reid, on the tobacco excise
duties :
He was sorry that the young girls employed in the
cigarette factories were not engaged in making something
more useful.
Well, as far as N.S.W. is concerned, who pre-
vented the young girls in the cigarette factories
from making “something more useful”? Reid
encouraged the young girls to make cigarettes by
heavily protecting the tobacco industry ; but, at
the same time, Reid barred the young girls from
making “something more useful” by putting that
article on the free list.
Digby Denham, who ratted before the
elections because he didn’t approve of Kidston’s
socialistic tendencies, made a speech in the
Queensland Assembly the other evening. After
he had completed his remarks-in-chief, in which
he asked the Government to do a few things, he
threw in this list of odds and ends, previously
omitted : —(1) The State should assist the coffee
industry; (2) the State should actively combat
red water ; (3) the State should finance farmers to
build silos; (4) the State should eradicate prickly
pear. Then he added that Socialism was a raging
lion or a roaring elephant or a blue giraffe with a
green tail, or something, and that the State should
look with a kindly eye upon private railways.
TREADING ON HIS CORNS.
Mr. W. H Irvine, M.H.R., has made a splenetic attack on the Acting Prime Minister.... Mr. Irvine said it
was time someone trod on that gentleman’s corns, and trod on them vigorously.—Age.
Surely the whirligig of time was never
fitted with such multi-cylinder power as in New
South Wales. R. D. Meagher and John Norton
who, a few brief years ago, were carrying on hos-
tilities in the streets of Sydney, now sit side by
side in the Assembly as a “Fifth Party,” the
other four being the Ministerial, the Labor, the
O’Sullivan, and the David Storey parties. That
much-talked-of consummation —the return to the
two-party system'—seems to grow more remote
every day.
The best thing that has happened in Bris-
bane for a long while has been the flutter that the
Wages Board Bill made in the clothing factories
and dressmaking shops of the local sweaters.
They didn’t wait for the Bill to shoot; they just
came down with revised wages-sheets. Four-fifths
of them, at least, have done this. No doubt some
beautiful old Tory will note this, and announce
by-and-bye that this sort of legislation is totally
unnecessary, and in proof will point to the fact
that wages were raised before the Act was passed.
The best point made by N.S.W. Opposi-
tion-leader McGowen, the chronic loser of the
’bus, in his attack on the Wade Government, was
this extract from the manifesto issued by the
Carruthers-Wade Liberal and Deform Associa-
tion at the time when that chookey-hen gathering
was trying to shove Twaddle out of office :
His, (Waddell’s) record as Treasurer shows him to be
unfit for the position, and his Ministry to be unworthy of
trust. Electors, how can you give such a failure as Mr.
Waddell another trial ? Surely three years’ experience of
his blundering finance is quite enough for any sane man
or woman.
And now Twaddle is back at the Treasury as the
financial genius whom Wade and Carruthers have
just unearthed.
The Argus made a great discovery the
other day, and announced it this way
If the whole of the sugar consumed in the Common-
wealth last year had been imported, and had paid the
existing rate Of import duty, people would have had to
pay no more for their sugar, and the Commonwealth
would have received an additional sum of £980,000 in
sugar duties.
Likewise, of course, if Australia will import its
wheat, wool, coal, butter, newspapers, etc., etc.,
and will put a tidy import duty on those articles,
it will have to pay no more for its wheat, wool,
coal, etc., than at present, and the Commonwealth
will, moreover,, get about £10,000,000 more re-
venue. In fact, Heaven only knows why Aus-
tralia should trouble to produce anything at
all, when it can import every article of merchan-
dise, paying just as much as at present,
and get a huge Federal revenue to splash round in
addition. How it would pay for the imported
goods, and how it would pay the duty on them,
when it made nothing and bought everything
from abroad, the Argus (alas !) forgets to men-
tion. At different times the importers’ press has
set forth that it would be cheaper to shut up so
many dozen industries, and import the goods,
and even pension off the workmen, wherefore this
question is becoming urgent.
These are items from that interview with
publican Power which the Sydney Telegraph
headed with a great shriek “The Price of Beer;
Increase in Bottled Goods as a Result of the
Tariff.”
My view is that the tariff means the disuse of all im-
ported bottled beers and lagers. In this direction we will
in future consume our own products, and into the bargain
we will be able to produce some of the finest beers and
lagers in the world. An additional fact in favor of the
local product is that the New South Wales Board of
Health forbids adulteration of locally-produced beers.
As an additional result of the taxation imposed in 1901
nearly all the Scotch whisky importers have opened
branches in Victoria and New South Wales. Whisky in
bulk is sent to them, and it is thus bottled, cased, and
capsuled in Australia, while the labels are also printed
here. This means the giving of employment to hundreds
of hands, including clerical staffs and hundreds of boys
and girls.
One direct result is that the people of Australia get
five-year-old whisky out here in the wood, and the trip
through the tropics in wood, rather than bottles as for-
merly, adds three years to the maturity. The fact that
the whisky firms, too, have sent out bottling and clerical
staffs has largely assisted immigration.
In fact the yarn was a blessing upon the tariff,
but that didn’t make any difference in the head-
lines. The Telegraph doesn’t seem to worry
about making the headlines suit the matter; all
it demands is that they shall suit the importer. It
would probably head the report of a church ser-
vice “ Remarkable Dogfight ” if it thought it
could damage William Lyne by so doing.
Rhetorical flight of the Owe’Sullivan, fore-
telling the doom of N.S.W. Premier Wade :
The present Administration may blunder along for a
few months, but while looking at stars they will stumble
over pebbles—and then the ambulance will take them to
the hospital, where the grinning skulls of their prede-
cessors are stored.
Regarding the ha’penny crisis in S.
Australia :
It was Roberts, M.L.A., whose sudden change to the
other side in December, 1899, brought the Kingston
Government to grief. Now he is leading the Labor party
in its revolt against the Price Government over the
Franchise agreement arrived at with the Council.
In each instance the Franchise Bill has been the cause of
the trouble. In 1899 Kingston wanted to force a
double dissolution, and was voted out of office by
members frightened of losing-their seats. Thistime Price
has attempted to avert a double dissolution by accepting
a compromise offered by the Council, and Roberts thinks
it will pay the Labor party better “to keep the sore
open” and go to the country. One quaint reason put
forward by the revolting members of the Labor party for
refusing the £l7 franchise offered (the present franchise
is £25) is that hundreds of people are wrongfully on the
roll already, and so the number will not be greatly in-
creased by lowering- the rental qualification. In the Port
Adelaide district alone, it is said, 570 persons whose
names are on the roll do not pay the rent required by the
Constitution Act. Both sides, however, are frightened
to purge the rolls lest the names struck off should be
those of their own supporters. Under the compromise a
statutory declaration has to be taken that the person
claiming to vote, possesses the qualification stipulated,
and there is a penalty for a false declaration. It is
actually feared that under these conditions the drop in
rental value from £25 to £l7 will leave the number of
Council electors very much where it is now.
THE GREAT WADE PARROT.
Mr. Wade made some observations yesterday. “ As to
the Federal attitude,” the Premier said, “I hold very
strong views of the rights of this State as a component
part of the Federation, and that those .rights should be
observed loyally and sacredly. I have every hope that
Mr. Lyne realises that the present Government has at
heart the interests of the State in this respect, and I
trust it will occur to him that it is very desirable that
the forbearance of the State and its people should not be
further strained.”

From Senator Pulsford’s letter of censure
to Premier Carruthers, after the wire-netting
piracy : —
There have not been wanting those who have declared
that the Commonwealth ought to place its capital where
access to it could be obtained independent of N.S.W.
territory'. How can this argument be met if the State
employs policemen instead of the Courts to maintain its
rights against the Commonwealth ?
In other words, is it a good thing to place the
Federal Capital where one small, parochial
politician like Carruthers might at any time make
it practically a beleaguered city ? If the capital
were wholly surrounded by N.S.W. territory, that
is how matters would stand.

GOOD COMPANY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sweating prevalent showed the need for teaching
the duty of a Christian to his neighbor. The Church
must lead. —Piece spoken at the Melbourne Diocesan
Synod (S/10/’07.)
Where Labor’s riddled banner waves triumphant in the
van,
Where march the men-at-arms who push the Brotherhood
of Man,
Where limp the broken heroes, with their anxious eyes
ashine
To see the dawn of freedom in the sweatshop and the
mine—
Hooray ! —a hurried cohort comes to share the triumph-
song,
To whack the joy and glory and the pride of righted
wrong,
A motley mob, perspiring in a frantic burst of speed—
It is the Church belated, hustling up to take the lead 1
Along the aisles of sacred piles, with knee-stools left and
right,
New Labor-leaders stride, each one a bright and shining
light,
And fierce, emphatic females, murdered fowls in every
toque,
Come dusting down to watch the Church take on poor
Labor’s yoke ;
Where once the bright tithe-sovereigns of the merchant
princes shone
Upon the plate, a cassocked army shoves the movement
on,
And out across the crimsoned fields, where still the
eagles feed,
The Church, with crumpled surplice, struggles up to
take the lead I
The river of red years has swept a thousand souls away
Who, with ten thousand foes to watch, had barely time
to pray—
That sturdy bunch of grim, unhaloed leaders, tried and
strong,
Who worked unreckoned overtime to push the Cause
along;
And now, when glory’s in the air, and Sweat’s no longer
Sin,
Fat feet in large, imported boots come boldly marching
in—
When now the pierced hands of toil have all but ceased
to bleed,
The Church waves high the banner and, applauding,
takes the lead!

SPORTING
NOTIONS
A. J. O. week atoms. The £20,030 prize
money was shared by 45 owners ; £15,245 remains in
N.S.W., £2359 goes to Victoria, £1909 to Maoriland, and
£517 to (Queensland. The biggest winners were : —J.
McDonald (owner of Mountain King), £3298 ; J. Samuels
(owner of Mooltan), £2556; and Hon. A Wynne (Vic.,
owner of Melodrama), £2059. Most successful trainers
were : —J. Burton, four wins; and I. Earnshaw, three
wins. Jockeys F. Hickey, T. Clayton, R. W. Bren-
nan, and W. li. Smith rode three winners each ; and the
progeny of Wallace and imported Grafton and Sir Tris-
tram each succeeded thrice.
* * * *
The second A.J.C. steeplechase was a great
tussle, and will live long- in the 1 memory of those who saw
it. A curious feature was that Marjorie was the only one
of the six starters which did not hail from Maoriland.
Haydn was favorite at 2 to 1 against, and Phaeton tis,
though crushed with 13 stone, was largely fancied by
reason of the way he won the first steeple. He came
down at the nine-furlong post, and, losing his rider, ran
the rest of the race on his own, negotiating four
jumps in great style. Haydn was leading at the
home turn, and the excitement happened when
Swimmer (5 to 1 against) caught him. Swimmer’s effort
to wipe out a dozen lengths had tired him, and Haydn’s
struggle to keep in front had just about exhausted him.
A great effort ended in Swimmer being 1 beaten by half a
length. Both horses seemed to have been ridden to a
standstill, and reeled as though they drunk. The
well-backed Eclair came a cropper at the first obstacle, a
brush fence, and though Pipi and Marjorie completed
the course they never had a hope. Marjorie was ridden by
her owner, 101 b. overweight, which was a foolish super-
fluity of ballast.
Unquestionably the great performer of the
A.J.C. meeting was the big (16‘3) Wallace youngster,
Mountain King. Through the donkey-race manner in
which the earlier stages of the Derby were run it was
impossible, despite his win, to form any opinion of his
capabilities on the strength of that performance. In the
New Stakes on the second day Mountain King did no
more than the touts and the rest of the authorities looked
for, but the gilded Craven Plate (1| mile) on Wednesday
provided a real test; and the King got through it as only
a horse of high degree could. Just as that race placed
Mountain King high in the estimation of the punters, so
did it stagger their opinion of last year’s wonder,
Poseidon. Only a point separated Poseidon and
Mountain King in the public favor, and the latter
had the call. There was heaps of gold for Antonio at 7
to 2 against, while 25 to 1 that Collarit couldn’t win went
begging. Collarit, as is his w’ont. showed the way from the
jump, with lolaire running close. Just before rounding
for home C Barit's tyre blew out, or his sparker went
wrong, or his steam fizzled away, and Mountain King and
lolaire battled bitterly to the end, where the Derby
winner got half his head —no more—in front, and struck
tile judge’s eye first. How unexpected lolaire’s
position was is made manifest, as the Scripture
sayeth, by the fact that hundreds to three against
him were available for the asking. There was not
even a suggestion of brilliance in Poseidon’s effort,
and now it is said at sporting resorts that he never
was the remarkable animal Australia considered him ; he
was the best of last year’s inferior cattle, and nothing
more. Which is plain rot. Twelve months ago Poseidon
did great things as only a thorough racehorse could.
Antonio disappointed his backers, who were numerous,
but it transpired that in addition to a gaine leg on one
side of him he had a strained shoulder on the other. He
will not appear again for some time. Possibly he will not
appear any more.
* * # ■*
Christian Moses is at his nefarious tricks again.
Of late the punter has kept his end of the log up fairly
well, and the fact seems to worry Moses, so now he is
busy hatching schemes for the undoing of the public.
Though that £3ouo to £6O didn’t make Mooltan win the
Epsom, it is understood that the same lever will be
applied to Apologue for the Caulfield Cup. That horse’s
display in the Metrop. gives him a good look in for either
the V.A.T.C. or the V.R.C. Cups. Apologue occupies
exactly the same position with regard to the Caulfield
Cup as did Mooltan for the Epsom. He has been fancied
and backed for the Melbourne Cup just as Mooltan was
for the Metropolitan ; and just as Mooltan was not fancied
for the Epsom, neither !s Apologue thought much
of for the Caulfield, the general impression being that
he is after the bigger prize. Now it is whispered
that Christian Isaacs is holding out the tempting bait of
£5OOO to a merely nominal sum, and the offer is “being
Considered.” So those in the know are rushing to get on
to Apologue at current rates before the offer is finally
considered, when Apologue will become a shockingly
short-priced favorite. Final acceptances for the Caulfield
Cup, which have just been published, show that Apo-
logue is still in, so apparently Christian Abraham has
scored as far as is possible at this time of the year.
* * * *
Guineas day at Caulfield left the “ students of
local form ” with very little to stand upon for the Cup,
which is due on Saturday. A week earlier, nothing had
seemed more certain —as certainties go in racing—than
the claim of Subterranean to local favor. With the worst
of the weights, Subterranean ran away from the three-
year-olds—Mazarin and Master Foote —as though they
were not in the same class with him. In a subsequent
race over a like distance (10 furlongs), Yabba Gabba and
Ellis fought out a desperate finish two lengths clear of
the field, but their performance was not nearly so impres-
sive as that of the six-year-old in the earlier event. Con-
sequently, Subterranean went to a very short price for
the Cup after Poseidon had managed" to get so badly
beaten in Sydney. And punters not unnaturally rushed
to take 5 to 4 about the Maorilander for the Caulfield
Stakes on Saturday last.
But in this case he appeared to be a moderate
animal. Pitted at w.f.a. against Ebullition (in wonderful
condition) and Ellis, the favorite could only run a dead-
heat with the latter, whilst- Ebullition was winning
easily from the pair of them. And Yabba Gabba, who
ought to have been preferred to Ellis, went out
unbacked and finished nowhere. On this “form’
Ebullition (if he can stay) should beat Subter-
ranean, who (if he can stay) should be no better
than Ellis (if he can stay) in the hustle for the rich IJ-
mile race. Also Yabba Gabba should be as good as any
of them, provided that the fact of her being unbacked in
the Caulfield Stakes meant that the mare was not fancied
with an unfashionable jockey on her back. When Yabba
gave 21b. and a half-head beating to Ellis she was ridden
by the all-conquering Turner. But when Yabba received
51b. and a severe doing from Ellis a week later the jockey
was E. Stone.
In this Stakes race Bendigo was unplaced and
unbacked, although on his previous running with the
favorite the handsome offspring of Benvolio should have
been a public fancy. To this scribe’s thinking, Bendigo
is likely to prove the best of that little lot in the near
future. He seems a bit above himself, and probably he
will not be fit enough, or sufficiently well jockeyed to
win next Saturday. But, considered as a Melbourne Cup
horse, with the capacity for running on when some of the
more slippery ones are beginning to tire, Bendigo fills the
eye.
The chances are that —save Bendigo—none of
the first mentioned lot can stay. The fast 12 furlongs at
Caulfield may be too far for them. Subterranean seems
best worth backing, because he won at 10 and was a
disappointment at nine furlongs. But after looking from
all points of view at the Victorian horses that have lately
shaped bewilderingly on their native soil, one is forced to
the conclusion that something fresh from Randwick will
win the Caulfield Cup. Poseidon, Apologue, lolaire, Sub-
terranean, Bendigo, and Ebullition are the most trust-
worthy of the well-performed ones, and of the others
there is still a glamor of great possibility about
Rhubarb, an imported 6-year-old that looks just lovely,
in a ladylike way of speaking. The recent defeat of
Poseidon at Randwick suggests that he is not now, nor
ever will be again, the champion he was 12 months ago.
Still he is a quadruped of consistent excellence as com-
pared with the in-and-out performers that help to fatten
the bookmakers in Victoria. Unless last year’s winner is
more off than has been suspected, he should go near to
repeating the trick. Nevertheless, Apologue should beat
him at the weights, and lolaire at 7st. 91b. looks equally
dangerous. This prophecy, then, is—
-1 Apologue
2 lolaire
3 Poseidon
4 Subterranean
