The starting at Caulfield last Saturday was bad
in two or three events. The rich Debutant Stakes, worth
£lOOO to the winner, was at the mercy of five (out of 14)
youngsters from the moment the barrier rose. The lucky
quintette changed their order of position in the running,
but from start to finish they were a long way ahead of
the other nine.
» * * *
Said that the reason Poseidon showed up so
badly in the Craven Plate was that there was a collision
between him and Antonio. It put Poseidon out of the
race, and damaged Antonio’s sound shoulder, so that
that splendid horse is probably out of racing for ever.
Also alleged that Poseidon’s owner still has faith in him,
and has backed him to win a huge sum—somewhere
about £lO,OO0 —on the Caulfield Cup.
* * * *
“H.R.”: Although I would not recommend
any man to raise a mortgage over the ancestral home or
pawn the family jewels to do it, the man who has money
to burn might do worse than back Tartan for Melbourne
Cup. The way he gathered up the long-distance flat
race, Randwick Plate, at the A.J.C. meeting was a
revelation. He travelled the last quarter of the 24-
miles at a pace that convinced those who watched
that he can stay as well as ever. His weight in the
Melbourne Cup is 9st. 91b., but on his form at Randwick he
can hump it. Though not quite at top form, he is near
enough to be thoroughly well on Melbourne Cup day.
Carbine, Archer, and Malua won the same event with
lOst. 51b., lOst. 21b., and 9st. 91b. respectively. Tartan
has been over the course, as he ran third to Blue Spec and
Scot Free two years ago, and the ancient dog is the
animal for the hard road. It is a bumpy race, and Tartan
is the horse for a bumpy race. Saturday’s race was
Tartan’s first win since he scooped the Plate at A.J.C.
Autumn meeting last vear.
* * * *
“Billo”: A dangerous-looking opponent of
Mountain King for the V.R.C. Derby is Master Foote,
first foal of Debutant Stakes winner Wigelmar. As a
yearling he cost the present owner, Mr. Leonard, 1600
guineas. He won Caulfield Guineas on Saturday in the
record time for the race of lmin. 41 Jsec. Still, he has not
been an unalloyed delight; he failed as a two-year-old, and
shaped poorly in the V.R.C. weight-for-age "race after he
had won at Caulfield. But his record run in the Caul-
field Guineas wipes out these matters, and as he carries
6st. 111 b. in both Cups, he has a possible for either of
them.
-* * * •
Adelaide Hunt Cup was this year won easily by
Vigil, a five-year-old from the “south-east” (Mount
Gambier district). Vigil shared top weight (12st. 81b.)
with Seawave, last year’s winner: started at 6 to 4
against in a field of eight, led throughout, and finished
the three miles 25 lengths to the good.
* ■* * *
As anticipated herein, the final of the Soccer
sudden death competition between Broadmeadow and
Pyrmont for the possession of the Gardiner Cup was won
by the former, the country club, the score being three
goals to two. The game started fast, and became faster
still as it progressed. Broadmeadow drew first blood,
but Pyrmont, with a great piece of combination, managed
to equalise the scores. Thenceforward for a while both
teams played desperately, and the game swayed
about the middle of the ground, till Broadmeadow
bullocked down to Pyrmont’s goal and took shot after
shot. These goal-keeper Spencer, with amazing skill and
celerity, managed to block, till half-back Maxwell came
at him and booted it through. The first half ended with
Broadmeadow leading by two to one. The second half
started with a roar, and both teams attacked vigorously,
the leather travelling up and down the ground at a great
rate till Pyrmont scored, and made the record two all.
Time ran out without either side making any further
points, and it was decided to play to a finish, two spells
of 10 minutes each being played from opposite ends of
the ground. The end of the first spell found the
teams still equal, and half-way through the
second one. they were still struggling, without having
scored. Then Broadmeadow back, Doyle, kicked a goal
amidst tremendous applause. The Pyrmont men made
frantic efforts to equalise the score. Broadmeadow
struggled as hard to make its victory even more decisive.
The light began to fail, but they toiled on through
the twilight and deepening gloom, and the murk in the
hearts of the Pyrmont men was darker than the field on
which the ball became gradually undistinguishable. It
was dark when Broadmeadow, breathless and gasping,
marched off the field winners by three goals to two, and
owner of the Gardiner Cup.
* * * *
On Saturday last a hard nor’-easter swept over
Sydney Harbor, and hands that during the winter rest
had lost their cunning in the management of the canvas,
brought trouble on their owners’ heads. The chief event
was the opening handicap of the Sydney Sailing Club.
Making for the starting line the Mona came to grief and
gave warning that it was a day of danger. At Steel Point
the Wonga capsized ; but the crowning disaster was that
of the Zena, which upset just after rounding the buoy at
Clark Island. The steamer Bingarra, on its way to
Manly, was steering to cross the stern of the Zena when
the boat turned over. This brought the little craft right
in front of the Manly steamer, and the Bingarra’s engines
immediately went astern, or not a man on board the
overturned craft would haye escaped. Two of the Zena’s
crew dived clear of the oncoming steamer, but the suc-
tion of the screw appears to have drawn the others under.
The Watson’s Bay boat and the Lady Ariel launch came
in to render assistance, but the men were rolled about
like sticks in the heavy wash, and life buoys
were thrown around in wild profusion. Eight of the
nine men on board the Zena were saved, the owner (J. M.
Firth) being in an unconscious state. One man (Edgar
Woods) was just reaching for a rope when he disappeared
under the steamer and was not seen afterwards. At time
of writing, the body had not been recovered. Two naval
men sailing round Garden Island were capsized, and one
was drowned. The pilot steamer was coming past
Chowder Bay when the skipper heard cries in the dark-
ness, and, proceeding to investigate, found five men
clinging to an upturned boat. They had been there an
hour.
Tho smash-up of the Zena and the death of
Edgar Woods took all interest out of the race. When
Zena went over, Crescent was seconds ahead of Scot,
which was about as much in front of Pheasant, with Aus-
tralian and Eileen bringing up the rear, Donelly having
retired. At Taylor Bay Orescent was 56sec. ahead, and
the other positions unchanged. Crossing the stream
Scot drew alongside Crescent, and then the mast sprung,
putting the boat out of the race. Pheasant sustained a
similar injury ; and Crescent flew on to win with ease
and a margin of lmin. 29sec. from Australia, while the
damaged Scot was third, and Pheasant fourth.
« * ■* *
N.S.W. Rowing Association’s Championship
Regatta was held on the upper reaches of the Parra-
matta River. The interest centred in the Champion
Eights, and the Sydney Club was the defender. Leich-
hardt, University and Mercantile clubs were the attack-
ing force. Mercantile got away with a beautiful start,
but Leichhardt and University came with a mighty
swing, and the champion boat, through bad
steering, began to fall behind. At the mile
Leichhardt was leading University, and Sydney
was leading Mercantile, which showed signs of weaken-
ing. At Gascoigne Bay UniversiT '■ challenged Leich-
hardt, and they both left Sydney behind by about
half a length. Then the Sydney captain was heard
shouting, his men began to move, and the old boat went
leaping through the water. Stroke by stroke it climbed
up alongside the struggling leaders, ran level with
them, and then drew away amidst wild applause. But
Leichhardt came again, and ran level with the champion
crew for a little way, University being a length and a
half behind, and Mercantile away to glory in the rear.
Another spurt sent Sydney to the front, and the crowd
was already cheering the winners when faulty steering
gave Leichhardt and University another chance. They
snapped it. All in a flash they were alongside, but Syd-
ney made a supreme effort, and just managed to swing to
the front as the winning line was crossed.
m •' * *
The popular fallacy that sharks do not venture
into surf received another damaging knock at Merewether
Beach, near Newcastle, Sunday morning. Edward Nolan,
diving out to meet an incoming roller, suddenly found
himself face to face with a shark, which dived under him,
turned on its side and grabbed his arm. He managed to
wrench the limb away, and make for the shore. His
friends rushed into the water to his assistance, and the
shark, scared by the fuss, made off. What between
sharks, undertow, backwash and dress ordinances, the
surf bather has a harried time.
* * * *
Something like a rebellion is pending in Sydney,
but the subject is not State Frights, wire netting or the
capital question. It is bathing costume. Mayor Watkins,
of Waverley, and practically every other waterside
mayor, have ukased that men who bathe shall wear
skirts, and sun bathing on the beaches is to be strictly
prohibited. The bather shall undress and don his bathing
costume in the place provided for the purpose, then make
a bee-line at a dog-trot to the water, When he or she
emerges from the water, he or she or it shall make a bee-
line at a dog-trot for the dressing-box, speaking to
no one on the way, This is imperative. The dress
is to consist of a tunic and trunks reaching from
the shoulder to the knee, and fastened round the waist
and descending to the knees a kilt, kirtle, skirt
draggle-tail, or embankment. Samples are on view
at all the suburban town halls, where intending
bathers may see and take patterns. The question
of fencing in certain portions of the beaches for
sun-bathing purposes is being considered, but at present
Mayor Watkins and other big dogs are of opinion that
sun bathing should be confined to the back yard. The
trouble is that the average back yard in a Sydney suburb
is about as public a place as any beach.
* * « *
What resembled, as far as the spectators were
concerned, a dress rehearsal of the “Australian Henley”
transpired on the Yarra last Saturday, when the question
which public school was entitled to call itself “ head of
the river” for the year was decided. There were
five entries —Melbourne Grammar, Scotch College,
Wesley Grammar, Xavier College, and Geelong
Grammar; and as Melbourne’s boss trickle only
accommodates three competitors at once, the preliminary
heats had been decided the day before. Geelong had
then won by a third of a length from Wesley. Melbourne
Grammar looked to be winning, when No. 5 caught a
crab and upset the whole combination. Scotch College
had beaten Xavier handily. The Geelong lads averaged
list., and when they met Scotch (who averaged list.
lOJlb.) on Saturday, the weight and length of reach told.
Scotch collared a lead at the start, and increased it
steadily, and though the lighter eight made a
strenuous spurt at three-quarter distance they couldn’t
reduce the lead, and finished, dead beat, 2J lengths to
the bad. This is Scotch College’s first appearance as Boss
of the River, Wesley College having held that proud
position since the race was inaugurated as an eight-oar
scramble in 1901.
* * • *
The remarkable feature of Sydney cricket last
week was the evenness of the scores. Redfern and
Paddington tied ; Waverley beat University by only 12
runs on the first innings ; Glebe walloped Petersham by
five runs on the same basis ; Central Cumberland smashed
up Burwood by 80, also on the first innings ; and North
Sydney downed Gordon by 52 (first innings likewise). The
only big defeats of the week were those of Balmain and
Sydney. Callan Park scrunched the former by eight
wickets, and Middle Harbor accounted for Sydney by
four wickets and two disputed runs.
• * * *
The only century-maker was Hickson, of
Gordon, who smacked up 113 against North Sydney
before Clarke bowled him. Other good scores were
Bardsley’s 91 against Petersham, while A. E. Johnston
made 84 for Burwood against Central Cumberland, and
then went down to a straight one from Cranney.
The result of the upheaval was to place North
Sydney at the head of the competition with five points,
the nearest rivals being Central Cumberland and
Waverley, which are dead level in second place with twm
points each. The rest nowhere.
* * * »
The argument between the V.C.A. and the
M.C.C. as to the sum to be paid for using the Melbourne
Cricket Ground for two “test” matches and two Eng-
land v. Victoria matches is still proceeding. The club
sticks to its demand of 15 per cent, of the gross takings—
plus, of course, free entry for its members and their lady
friends. The Cricket Association has sprung its first
offer of 5 per cent, to a sort of sliding scale covering 5 per
cent, if the gross takings are less than £7OOO, 7J per cent,
if they are between £7OOO and £9OOO ; 10 per cent, if
between £9OOO and £lO,OOO, and 15 per cent, if over that
sum. The M.C.C. points chirpily to the 15 per
cent, guaranteed in Adelaide and the 18 per cent, in
Sydney. But the cases are not on the same footing. All
the profits at Adelaide go to the S.A. Association, while
in Sydney the Board of Control deals with the ground
trustees direct, and, not having such a huge mob of club
deadheads to cater for, has a larger percentage of paying
public to levy on. The point that the M.C.C. seems to
have overlooked is that it is demanding 15 per cent, of
the gross receipts for the use of a ground that doesn’t
belong to it at all. The Marylebone C.C. gets 50 per cent,
of the gross receipts, and if the Melb. C.C. is to get 15 per
cent, there will be a poor little 35 per cent, with which
the Board of Control can pay expenses amounting to
some £2OOO, and face any other contingencies—including
the £lO,OOO guarantee—that may occur, besides doing all
the graft and taking all the risk. At latest the ground
trustees had asked club and association to appoint three
delegates each, to talk matters over with them before the
modus vivendi gets fractured beyond repair, in a manner
of speaking.
* * * *
The League of Victorian Wheelmen decided
that Dobie, winner of the Warrnambool to Melbourne road
race, was not paced by motor cyclists, and that the real
caitiff in the case was one named Halit, who has been de-
prived of his prize and passed out for 12 months. Two days
before the Warrnambool-Melbourne race was run the
Dunlop people discovered tacks on a section of the track.
Having traced the alleged offender the Dunlop folk are
talking legal action.
* * ’
That cablegram from Macfarland, Pye, and
Clarke which arrived from Suva, requesting that they be
entered for the sports on Eight Hour Day, created con-
siderable surprise.- These cyclists are changing 1 their
habits. Time was when Mac wouldn’t.go across the road
unless the fact was extensively advertised and properly
boomed. In spite of his remarkable running, and all the
rest of it, one can’t help feeling good towards Macfarland
He was such a manty sort of humbug 1 , and when he had
made his tale he stuck to it with a determination that
was as admirable as it was exasperating. He is thorough
in his hatred of niggers, and when the League had rubbed
it into him Mac chewed a straw philosophically till
Mclntosh, of the League, was leaving the room. Outside,
the long Mac touched the short one on the shoulder, and
a Yankee twang smote the stars : “ Sa-a-a-y, Mac, by the
time y’ know as much about niggers as I do, you’ll like
the black sons of dogs about as much as I do.” And then
he spat with great emphasis.
* * • *
Major Taylor’s Christianity was a heavy han-
dicap when he was hustling on the track. When it came
to finesse on board of a bike there wasn’t a man on the
Australian tracks who could hold a candle to him.
But he slid he always felt the foot of de Loard
beside his own, driving the pedal, an’ he couldn't
use the foot of de Loard in bad work. “Well,
get the Loard to bog into it on his own ! ” said one
impious jigger. And that night the Loard did.
MacFarland was trying to bustle Taylor. He used to
trade on Taylor’s respect for the Loard’s foot. But just
for once he miscalculated. The black man gave a waggle,
his machine made an almost invisible side swing, and
next moment most of the spokes were ripped out of
Mac’s wheel, and that lengthy wheeler, cursing magnifi-
cently, was compelled to run off on to the grass, while
Taylor, with a sardonic smile, flew on and won his heat.
MacFarland’s respect for Taylor increased about 500 per
cent, on account of that little caper, and he always spoke
of it in terms of profound admiration.
• * * •
The supporters of N.S.W. sculler Richard Tres-
Idder are not altogether satisfied with the articles signed
and sent over by Webb and his backers. The papers said
nothing about expenses, and were silent as to the division
of the steamer takings, and so that there may be no mis-
understandings on these most important matters, Webb's
manager is to be written to. In the meantime canvassers
are to be appointed to try to rake in the remaining £250
of the£sooistake. So far Newcastle district has provided all
the monev promised, and as the championship is an nll-
Australian matter, it certainly does seem unfair that
Newcastle should have to find the whole of the boodle.
What has gone wrong with N.S.W., anyhow, when a
recognised sculler has to practically beg the stake to en-
title him to try to bring the championship back to
N.S.W. and Australia?
* * * »
On of about January 13 next, 26 champion
bowling Goliaths will leave Maoriland for N.S.W., with a
View to repeating the severe hiding the same crowd last
year inflicted on the Cornstalk bowlers. The Rugby
infatuation has not spread to the bowlers of Maoriland,
and the invading force will wear not one suggestion of
black about it, and will be known as “ The All White ’*
team. At the same time N.S.W. will probably send a
team to Tasmania. The first contest of the new Season
will be that for the John Young shield, which will be held
by the winning team for one year, and then put up for
another struggle. The contest will be open to all clubs,
and will be a sudden-death affair. The whole of the
games will be played on one day, and the club that wins
by the biggest margin of points is to take the shield.
* * * *
Dick Kernick and Tom Fennessey, the two
heftiest “boys” available, were staged in a 20-round
battle at Melbourne Pavilion the other Monday evening.
Kernick is well up towards 40, and Fennessey is a bald
boxer of 38 or so, but boxers are “boys” and “lads” as
long as they are not so old as to be in the second
perambulator stage. This fight was not exactly a
scientific object lesson, but it contained avast amount of
stoush, most of which fell on the somewhat piratical
countenance of Mr. Kernick, who, at the finish, had an
ear like an uncooked shoulder of lamb, and a crumpled
nose, not to mention other facial injuries necessi-
tating an unceremonious call at the hospital next
door. Kernick was badly beaten, Fennessey winning at
least 16 out of the 20 rounds ; but when the small, fierce
referee yelled : “ Kernick the winner ! ” nobody was sur-
prised—the crowd hooted from force of habit, but it felt
no astonishment. Patrons have become used to these
quaint eccentricities on the part of referees in Melbourne.
Until boxing encounters can be refereed by a machine,
the issue of battles should be decided by shaking the
competitors’ names in a hat. This would leave a great
deal to chance, but chance is more reliable than the
average referee.
« * * *
It is whispered, but not officially confirmed,
that Mort’s Dock Co. will build that Austral champion
boat just as soon as manager Relph can get his workshops
properly going. Just at present that energetic young
man is experiencing all the difficulties that attach to the
building up of a new industry in a young country. The
construction of internal combustion motors is an art in
itself, and the management is hard at work organising a
staff to keep pace with the orders that are pouring in
under the new tariff. Over 100 men are already employed.
When this expanding business has found its limitations, it
is understood, although not officially announced, that the
big Dock will put its ears right back and seriously lay
itself out to produce the star boat of the southern seas.
Writer suggests that it should be built of phosphor bronze
and driven by a couple of motors of 200 h.p. each. A
boat like that would take some beating.
A STEWARDESS
Regards Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills as a
Splendid Remedy for Headaches and
Sciatica.
Mrs. Rankin,
who has some
time since retired
from the service
of the A.U.S.N.

whose steamers
she was so well
and favourably
known, says in
her letter that
she has found no
better remedy for
headaches and
sciatica than Dr.
Morse’s Indian
Root Pills, and
she has no hesita-
tion in recom-
mending them as
a splendid medi-
cine:—“ll4 Kent Street, Sydney.—Dear Sirs, —
As one who has used Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills for many years, I. can conscientiously say
that I have found no better remedy for headaches
and sciatica. For many years I was stewardess
in the employ of the A.U.S.N. Co., and I always
carried a bottle of your pills with me, and I in-
variably found them as satisfactory with other
people as with myself. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills are a splendid medicine, and I have no
hesitation in recommending them.” To those
who travel, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills should
be a constant companion, carried in the pocket or
handy in the portmanteau. Especially to those
journeying by sea are these pills invaiuaVle. A
day or two before sailing the bowels should be
well regulated, and on board ship a pill or two
taken each night will in nine cases out of ten
prevent seasickness, as it is invariably the
constipated condition of the bowels which precipi
tates that distressing complaint.*

By The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant: —
George Stevenson, who died, aged 76, at Malvern (Vie.),
on Sunday, was one of the pioneers of the softgoods trade
in Melbourne. Stevenson, sen., was in extensive business
in Manchester, and had trade connections with Van
Diemen’s Land. In 1846 he sent his eldest son, Leander Cox
Stevenson, to look after those interests. In 1851 George
left for Australia in the Roman'Emperor, arriving in 1852.
In 1853 a branch was established in Melbourne. In 1859,
George went to England, married, and returned to
Melbourne, where he decided upon settling. He became a
noted personage in the early tariff struggles of over 40
years ago. A discharged clerk, in 1876, charged George
with having forwarded to him from England two sets of
invoices, one indicating the true value of goods
imported, the other faked for presentation to the
Customs officer. Collector of Customs Guthrie attempted
a siege of the warehouse of Stevenson, backed up by a
strong army of assistants. The Stevensons resisted,
violence was resorted to on both sides, and the affair
occupied the law courts for some time. It was thought then
that the firm intended to sue the Government for im-
mense damages, but after much waste of good Costs the
matter dropped.
“Weka”: The Ward Government (Maori-
land) has struck trouble again, and it is a noisy
sort of worry. J. A. McCullough, a union leader
in Canterbury, made remarks about the Ministry
which might have galvanised a heap of moi
bones into action. Somebody remembered that
McCullough was a tinsmith at the Government
workshops when he wasn’t cursing the Ministry.
McCullough ceased to be a tinsmith at the
Government workshops, which was a mighty
foolish thing on the part of whoever was re-
sponsible ; the wise thing would have been to make
him two tinsmiths, or else a foreman. Then the
Trades and Labor councils, which had been spoiling
for a fight, called mass meetings and set the grass
on fire with their language. Nothing really ensued,
but a growing tendency on the part of a certain
section of erstwhile followers to bark at the
Government grows very noticeable.

Died in Adelaide, suddenly, Andrew Scott,
Prominent educationalist. He was one of
Hartley’s young men ” years ago, and had
latterly been head of the Training College for
fetate-sehool teachers. Scott was a great golfer,
amateur photographer, and church organist; but
™ s pleasantest recollection was that he bowled
w. (Jr . Grace with his second ball when W.G.G.
was in his prime in 1874.
An old identity of St. Arnaud (Vic.), Mrs.
Elizabeth Gormley, died recently. She was a
sister of the late Patrick Jennings, who was in
business at St. Arnaud in the early ’fifties, and
subsequently moved to N. S. Wales, entered poli-
tical life, and became a kind of Premier. He was
the founder of the town of St. Arnaud, and was
chairman of its first municipal council. Mrs.
Gormley was twice married, her first husband
being John Quigley, of Castleblaney, Ireland.
Following his advocacy of Protection for
Mort’s Dock (Balmain), Wilks, the alleged Free-
trade member, shamelessly and openly advocated
a duty of 15 per cent, on wire netting, Lysaght’s
wire-netting factory being in his electorate. So
Wilks is again in the position of the man who
says Protection is disastrous, yet demands
all of the disaster he can get for his own electorate
also the man who says that Protection is a
mighty good thing for Balmain, yet rages when it
is proposed for Bathurst or Ballarat. Wilks is
parochialism incarnate. He isn’t parish-minded
in the N.S.W. sense, nor in the Sydney sense.
Balmain is a big enough parish for Wilks’ mind.

“Reka,” from Maoriland :
“Experiences?” said the little dentist. He surveyed
the big dint in the gas-tank reminiscently. “ A man
from the backbloeks did that,” he said. “ Regular giant
he was. He’d had toothache for a week, so he tramped
eight miles through the bush, came 60 miles by train, and
walked in here one afternoon about four o’clock. I gave
him gas, and was just ready to begin, when suddenly up
shot his feet in the air, and the next second he was stand-
ing behind the chair, facing me. Then he flung one arm
through a window-pane, and rushed at me. Catch him ?
No ! I dodged him. Took the lamp, and got out of the
room, and waited for him to eome-to. He couldn’t do
himself much harm, and he could easily have killed me.
He smashed another pane, and kicked the tank into the
shape of an old jam-tin before he quieted down. When I
ventured in I found him sitting in the chair. “ Say,
boss,” he said, “ that darned thing’s stopped achin’ now.
I guess I won’t have it out to-day. Whatever yer been
doin’ to th’ room ?”
I had a queer experience once with a girl of about 12.
I put the cap over her mouth, she took one breath, and
then suddenly she slid with a curious, sinuous wriggle on
to the floor. I grabbed her and called for assistance, and
four of us held her. She fought like a young demon.
When she came round her sister said, “It’s no use.
She’ll just have to have it out without gas or have the
toothache. You’re the fifth dentist that’s tried to give
her gas to-day.” About five years after, a girl walked
in one day and asked to have a tooth out. I took charge
of her, and began to give the gas. She took one breath,
and began to slide down with an odd hut familiar motion.
Suddenly I recognised the wriggle, and snatched the cap
awaj-. We’d hard work to hold her then. I asked her
not to come a third time.
When I first began to practise I used to travel all over
the North. Once at Whangarei an old lady asked me to
go to_ her house and pull one of her teeth. We gave
laughing gas in those days, and it had a most exhilara-
ting effect on some people. My old lady arose and danced
a wild liaka, and then commenced throwing- the orna-
ments at me. I was hit on the nose by a china pug, and
dodged two gilt shepherdesses and the parlor clock. She
wasn’t a bad shot for a woman, and I’d quick work
dodging. Suddenly she dived into the bedroom and
seized a revolver she always kept loaded in case of bur-
glars. I left by the back door. From behind a lemon tree
I saw jugs, cups, plates, and sundries dashing through
the window-panes, and there was a hideous riot of
breaking crockery going on inside. Presently I crept in
and got the revolver, and she began to slow down. She
cried bitterly when she catne-to and saw the havoc
around, and said I must have knocked her about and ill-
treated her shamefully.
Maoriland has discovered that 25 per cent,
adval will not keep out the cheap paper foot-
gear. Boots are invoiced to the Dominion at
from 2s. (duty 6d.) to 4s. (duty Is.) a pair, and
sold for 7s. They pay the shopkeeper hand-
somelv, for at the first touch of water they fall to
pieces, ana the owners come back for more. The
cheap imported boot means large profits and
quick returns, and therefore is beloved by the
huckster. But it does not pay Maoriland, which
finds that since 1901 the number of people
employed in making boots has decreased by 490,
in spite of the increasing population. The wages
earned by the operative have also decreased. As
to the remedy—well, how about a duty of ss. per
pair?


Australasian
Interstate Mail
Steamship Lines
HE last of the Five Com-
panies is the Mcllwraith,
McEacharn and Co. Pro-
prietary Ltd., which took
its place in Australian
coastal mercantile marine
about 20 years ago, and has done its share in
the building up of our Commonwealth. Its
colliers carry coal from Newcastle all along the
Australian coast to far-away Westralian ports, and
its general cargo and passenger steamers run from
Sydney about once a fortnight over the same water.
Its fleet consists of nine steamers and two tugs, and
in addition to the services mentioned above the co.
regularly runs a steamer to Calcutta. The fleet con-
sists of the following steamers: Ashridge (2884),
Cooeyanna (3922), Coolgardie(2s42), Kalgoorlie (2981),
Komura (2112), Kooringa (3174), New Guinea (2674),
Noorkoowa (1643), Kooyong (2000). The tenders
Torrens and Zephyr make up the tail of this fine fleet.
Very few Australians have any idea of the con-
veniences and facilities they are provided with by the
Five Companies. No country in the world is better
served by its coastal marine than Australia, and
mighty few of them nearly as well. During the 87
days between July 3rd and September 28th, for
instance, the Five Companies despatched from
Sydney, Melbourne, and Adelaide for the Westralian
ports 26 steamers, which is, roughly, one first-class
boat every three-and-a-half days. From Albany they
despatched 13 steamers for the and the same
number for the east. Passengers from Fremantle
had the services of 19 steamers to take them east
during the same period. Then there are 17 steamers
in the Queensland service, which follow one another
at about two days intervals. Twice a week steamers
run to Maoriland, and once a week the eager tourist
can pack his gripsack and start for Tasmania. In
addition to all these, there are various Island
services which there is no space to mention
just now.
And the Australian public gets an added
advantage from the number of steamers
which is not enjoyed by
the dweller in other
. parts, where the
various proprie-
taries are in
cut throat op-
position.
With a
whole con-
tinent to
work up-
on, and a
trade that
is increas-
ing’ every
week and month, the Five Companies find that
they are able to better accommodate the travel-
ling public and still conserve their own indivi-
dual interests by making their tickets inter-
changeable, so that the man whose business or
pleasure make it necessary for him to break his journey
at some port, has not to waste days or weeks for a
steamer of the same company to come along before
he can continue his journey. No sooner is one
steamer hull down over the horizon than another
funnel, bearing the colors of one of the Five Com-
panies, rises above the opposite sky line, to
be followed by a third whpn that has dis-
appeared ; and on any one of them he will receive
the same attention, the same fine accommodation,
and the fact that he is travelling on another pro-
prietary’s ticket will not count unto him for un-
righteousness, because someone else is most assuredly
returning the compliment on another boat of the other
lines. And yet, in spite of this working agreement,
the internal competition is never relaxed. Every
time one of the Five Companies makes an improve-
ment, or gets outabigger and better steamer, the others
are forced to follow suit, or else the public will fasten
on to the line that has the most floating palaces, and
firmly but politely decline to “interchange,” no
matter how alluring the prospect may be.'
This page concludes the historical and statistical
account of the rise and growth of the Five Companies.
After this the space will be devoted to letting the
public know something of the interesting places to
which these fine steamers trade, and give some
account of the steamers themselves, each of which has
its interesting episodes. With a strict regard to
truth, of course, there may be occasional accounts of
the weird things that have been seen from the bridges
in the starry silences of the night, and more occasion-
ally descriptions of the awful things seen in the
smoking saloons, so that he who reads may run.
There is a whole fund of romance around the steamers
that make up the endless chain that holds the Six
States together, and, as far as space will allow, it will
be placed at the disposal of readers. The inner life
of the Australian coastal marine has yet to be written,
and when it is it will fill many interesting volumes.
Shipping statistics are hard to get, and never up-to-
date when they are obtained. From the last issue of
“A Statistical Accountof Australia and New Zealand,
1903-4,” we learn that the total value of Australian
shipping was, at the end of 1903, £6,359,000, and that
of Maoriland £1,944,000. And the bulk of that
6-2 millions is represented by the steamers belonging
to the Five Companies, and, further, it has grown
largely between the end of 1903 and October, 1907.
But the value of its coastal marine to Australia is
not gauged by the actual money value of the ships.
It is the work they make and the means they supply
of turning the produce of the earth into wealth. They
make railways to seaports profitable, they suppl}’’ a
market for the coal of a hundred mines, and give
work to an army of men who drive carts and
handle goods on wharves. When the Westralian
Goldfields broke out, the ships of the Five Com-
panies, without fuss or ostentation, transported a
bigger crowd of people as passengers who had
to be decently accommodated and properly provided
for than did the British Government when the
South African war called for what the world described
“a gigantic effort.” The soldier was bundled on
board auyhow, and had to be content with what he
got. The gold miner was a free man, and an un-
usually independent one, who liked his money’s
worth ; and no one ever heard him complain that he
did not get it on the steamers of the Five Companies.
But great as have been the accomplishments of
the Five Companies in the past, the signs are that
they will be called upon shortly to make an even
greater effort. Of the 7750 miles of Australian coast-
line, that huge gap from Thursday Island, on the
North-east to Geraldton on the west is a blank. The
Australian coastal marine does not plough up those
thousands of miles of water. But Australia is enter-
ing on a new chapter of history, and now the shout is
to fill up the empty North and erect a white barrier
against the possible overflow of Asia. The Northern
Territory is rich in minerals, has a magnificent
climate, and can grow everything that a white man
requires. All the navigable rivers the continent
possesses are there, and the place contains enormous
possibilities. There is talk of building a transcon-
tinental railway, and that it will be of pre-eminent ser-
vice from a national and strategic standpoint is indis-
putable. But the settlement of the empty North when
it begins in deadly earnest will fall on the shoulders
of the men who manage the Five Companies, as did
the rush to Coolgardie and Pilbarra. For the railway
will take years to build, and, when it has been con-
structed, it will be a more expensive method of
travelling than by the sea. The ocean waves cost
nothing to maintain. If the Australian Government
wants to set to work to make a new State in the
Northern Territory, there is no reason why it should
not commence immediately. The shipping companies
that have built up Queensland, Victoria, the Golden
West, and made South Australia part of the Com-
monwealth, are quite capable of making the Northern
Territory if the Government will devise some method
of inducing people to settle there.
There was a time when the traveller who loved to
revel in luxury used to travel round the Australian
coast in the English mail steamers, but it is a dead
day now that the Five Companies have commenced
to run great 7000-ton twin-screw liners like the
Kauowna and Kyarra, fitted with magnifi-
cent deck-cabins and commanded by
officers who have spent all their
lives navigating the coast of their
own land. The traveller to-
day, by sticking to the
ships of his own country,
has just as great
luxury on one of the
big liners belong-
ing to the Five
Companies, with
the added advant-
age of being able
to break his jour-
ney anywhere, and
continue hi s trip by
the first steamer
that comes along.
Under these cir-
cumstances, he
naturally prefers
the Australian ships.

European and American piano-makers have for generations sought after a simple and practical metal tuning-
system (vide Edgar Brinsmead, of John Brinsmead and Sons, and Julius Bluthner, Leipsic); but though various devices
have been patented almost every week, the Beale-Vader Patent Steel Tuning-System is the only metal tuning-system in
practical use. More than 9000 Beale Australian Pianos are now in use throughout Australasia, and are giving
universal satisfaction.
In addition to the above patent we have originated many other improvements in piano-construction—a system of
building the cabinet-work which assures absolute immunity from warping or splitting; a sounding-board which gives a
softness and sweetness of tone unapproached by any other piano (as vouched for by Herr Emil Greder, Miss Sara
Anderson, and Herr Karl Studemund, of Musgrove’s Royal Grand Opera Company); a hexagonal steel core for the copper*
wound strings in the bass, securing a firm hold for the copper on the steel; hitch-pins cast in the iron frame, instead of
laboriously drilling holes and inserting the pins—thus effecting a saving in cost, while improving the efficiency; and many
other details of minor importance, to instance one of which we mention a hinge for the lid of grand pianos which allows of
a moulding on all sides, whereas all foreign pianos have one side of the lid without moulding, due to the construction
of their hinges.
We do not neglect the lessons and experience of the old-world makers ; in fact, inasmuch as we hold the only
patents registered in Australia for pianos, we can and do use all the best points of foreign pianos, with the added improve-
ments above mentioned. The same energy and the same ability, inventive and directive, which have effected these
important improvements where older manufacturers have failed, are used throughout in the construction of the Beale
Australian Pianos, and the result is that the people of Australia can buy pianos made here by their own countrymen far
superior to any they can import. We invite you to compare our pianos with the best imported instruments, and you must
acknowledge that the Beale Australian Pianos are unequalled.

The Inky Way.
The new Wellington (Maoriland) daily, the Dominion,
explains its policy thus : “ Great questions are agitating-,
and will continue to agitate, the public mind. Our view of
those questions is decided, not by the strategic require-
ments of any established party, or vested interest, but by
the natural requirements of the whole public. Our
policy, to meet that view, will necessarily mean the
acceleration of this existing tendency and the retardation
of that—in brief, a broad policy, free from narrowness or
exclusiveness in any direction. It will look, not to the
effecting of temporary ends, but to the securing of valu-
able permanences.” The Dominion makes rather a dull
beginning with the announcement tliat it has “ a broad
policy free from narrowness.” And to all appearance it
is going to be run on Fat principles free from leanness,
and its ideas will either be high and free from lowness or
tall and without shortness.
* * » a
The modern serial is no class unless it lias the day
before yesterday’s biggest sensation as a central feature.
The Weekly Dispatch (London and Manchester) has pub-
lished numerous chapters of “ Evelyn : The Tragic Life
Story of a Beautiful Artists’ Model,” which lifts slabs of
character and incident from the Thaw murder case.
Leading persons in the story are “Herbert Yarde, an
eccentric millionaire ”; “ Evelyn, his wife, formerly
actress and artists’model ” ; and “ Stafford Nation, mil-
lionaire and pleasure-seeker, who is madly infatuated
with Evelyn.” The yarn is keeping just a few feet behind
the real case, and seems to be treading water (or gore), in
the hope that some more revelations will soon arrive
to be turned into “ literature.”
• * * *
“ St. Andrew’s University ” writes : Have been in and
around the Dominion for nearly five years trying to make
an honest living by writing, principally for English and
American journals. Occasionally, until I discovered
where I was, I offered MSS. to Maoriland newspapers,
and, with the sole exception of the Evening Post,
Wellington, they received those epoch-making contri-
butions of mine with considerable disrespect. In some
cases they were neither acknowledged, returned, nor
printed. But, strange to say, the same Maoriland papers
have all along displayed wonderful readiness to appro-
priate my work after it has been published—and paid
for—by the best class of London journals. I once des-
patched on the same day a short article.to the Pall Mall
Gazette, and another to the editor of an Auckland
weekly. That sent to Auckland I have never seen or
heard of since. Had it been printed, the uncongenial
duty of paying for it would have had to be faced. There-
fore it was not printed. But the other article had a
cordial welcome in the same office. Published in the
Pall Mall Gazette, it was abstracted by the said Auck-
land weekly, which inserted it as an original contribu-
tion —no indication was given that it had been
boldly scissored out of the big London daily. Another
illustration : An article of mine entitled, by the -way,
“ The Rim of the Antarctic ” appeared not long ago, also
in the Pall Mall Gazette. This was taken over holus
bolus by, at least, five Maoriland newspapers. Two
acknowledged that it had seen the light elsewhere ; three
pretended that it had been specially written for their
pages. One of the latter —an Auckland daily—two weeks
ago revealed its weak and immoral methods. A para-
graph appeared in its news columns setting forth that
the Auckland paper containing my article (stolen from
the Pall Mall) had been forwarded by a correspondent
to a newspaper in Glasgow. The Glasgow journal, which
didn’t know that the Auckland one stole the article, was
interested in a quotation I made from a Maoriland
book—a book, that is, written, printed and published in
Maoriland —and advanced various facts by way of show
ing that a date mentioned in the quotation was incorrect.
Observe the position. This parasitical Auckland news-
paper depends on London for the free filling-up of
its columns, and it has to go to Glasgow for information,
not only about Maoriland books, but also about Maori-
land history. And a good many more Maoriland papers
are in precisely the same category. Taken as a whole
they are a measly lot.
, * * * *
Some of the English newspapers have abandoned the
old habit of giving to foreign theatrical artists their
national titular distinctions, as though there were no
equivalent for them. Signor and signorita, or senor and
senorita, are now made to understand that they are
Mr. and Miss in English-speaking countries. Also the
new rule holds good as regards monsieur and mademoi-
selle. But there is a tendency to meet a “madame”
half-way, and give her the anglicised title of madam
rather than “ Mrs.” After all “ Mrs.” is only an abbre-
viation of “mistress,” which ceased to be synonymous
with “ wife ” when it came to Court. Giddy Charles the
Second seems to have divested the term mistress of
much of its original significance and respectability when
he associated his royal name with Nell Gwynne and other
easy-going spinsters, who were politely spoken of as “ the
King’s mistresses.”
■* • • »
“Ejayem”: Among the curses of suburban and pro-
vincial journalism are the patronising and impertinent
councillor and the local parson. When Arthur Rees
(before he joined the Age) was attached to a South
Melbourne bugle he had often to choke off the usual
clerical genius who “ used-to write for the Times ” (W. P. B.
doubtless). Present writer was with Rees at the South
Melbourne Town Hall on the occasion of the memorial
service to the late Queen Victoria. They had rigged up
a sort of Punch and Judy dais for the preachers in the
middle of the premises, and next to this funereal
platform the table for the ink-slingers was placed. As
the solemn procession of parsons wended its way to the
sombre-draped platform, one of them cast a malevolent,
blightingglance at Rees, and then shook his head and gazed
Heavenwards. “ What have you done to the sin-shifter ? ”
I whispered. Rees produced a big grin. “ He interviewed
me twice about uplifting the paper, and when he sent in
ten columns of stuff to uplift it with, I put the tripe in the
basket with a deadly thud." The tail end of the proces-
sion included a person with considerable whiskers.
Marching up to Rees in quite a commanding fashion, he
said: “ Kindly send me in a proof of my prayah ! ”
Presently it came to his turn to speak his “prayah !”
He fell on his hunkers and started to blow off his suppli-
cation. He blew devoutly for some time. Then slowly
his eyes opened and rested on the Archimedean lever.
The Argus man was leaning forward, with his chin rest-
ing in his hands. The Age man was leaning back with
his hands clasped behind his head. The local paper man
was looking at the roof, with his pencil between his
teeth. The preacher almost gnashed his teeth at the
Archimedean lever before he resumed, which he did with
extra vehemence, Rees leaned forward and hissed.
“ Did you see that ? He hasn’t been praying to God ; he’s
been praying to us—praying to the press. lie can go to
old Nick for his proof of ‘ my prayah.’ ’•
THE TEMPTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He came to me in friendly form,
He greeted me with oily guile,
He looked at me in manner warm,
And threw at me a ghastly smile.
The days were dull and work was scarce,
The Muses smiled not on my rhyme ;
And I was sick—so was my verse.
The duns would grant no further time.
I thought he was a proper friend,
The words he uttered touched my heart.
Said I, “ This man will surely lend
Me half-a-sov. to ease the smart
“ Of gloomy days and thirsty nights.”
And so I pushed at him my ear
What time I stood upon the heights
Of joy, and sniffed big mugs of beer.
And then he said, “ I know you write,
And you are stony. Are you not ? ”
And I said, “ What you say is quite
Correct, and things are rather rot- .
Ten.” Then close over me he bent
And said, “Just write for me in verse
\ A brief and nice advertisement
And I will give ” “ Biff 1 Whish ! Oh ! Curse ! ”
Don’t ask me what to him I did ;
And how I kicked him high and low.
Don’t ask me where the body’s hid ;
My soul hath sins enough I know !
TAS.

for a prolonged period, as is necessary
with some medicines, which even then
are mostly disappointing; you simply
take a dose of Frootoids when ill and
repeat the dose if necessary, but gene-
rally one dose is quite effective.
> Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so
far that they not only act as an ape-
rient, but do remove from the blood,
tissues, and internal organs all the
Waste poisonous matter that is clog-
ging them and choking the channels
that lead to and from them. The bene-
ficial effects of Frootoids are evident
at once by the disappearance of head-
ache, the head becoming clear, and a
bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly,
and by the food being properly di-
gested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison is present, or when
Congestion of the Brain or Apoplexy
is present or threatening. They have
been tested, and have been proved to
afford quick relief in such cases when
other aperients have not done any
good at all. It is of the utmost impor-
tance that this should be borne in
mind, for in such cases to take an or-
dinary aperient is to waste time and
permit of a serious illness becoming
fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
antibilious pills make worse. Many
people have been made sick and ill
by antibilious pills that could have
been cured at once by Frootoids.
People should not allow themselves to
be duped into contracting a medicine-
taking habit by being persuaded to
take daily doses with each meal of so-
called indigestion cures that do NOT
cure. Frootoids have been subjected
to extensive tests, and have in every
case proved successful in completely
curing the complaints named.
A constipated habit of body will be
completely cured if the patient will on
each occasion, when suffering, take a
dose ot Frootoids, instead of an or-
dinary aperient; by so doing, the pa-
tient will require doses only at longer
intervals, and will so become quite
independent of the necessity of taking
any aperient medicine.

STRANGE
PREDICTIONS
Planetary Professor Foretells
Events, and Quickly Reads the
Lives of People, though Thou-
sands of Miles Away,
Senas Letters to the Rich and Poor
Alike, in which he advises them about
BUSINESS, MARRIAGE, SPECULATION,
LOVE AFFAIRS, WEALTH, ETC.
Offers Readings to all who write and
send Date of Birth, Sex, Married or
Single, with //- to cover cost of
postage, etc.
IN his office in Melbourne, surrounded by
Charts and Dials of strange design, Prof.
Henri De Astro studies daily over the lives
of men and women who have written him for
advice on affairs of business, love, speculation,
travel, marriage, health, and the important
events of life. The following letter gives an
idea of
MR. DE ASTRO’S ABILITY.
My Advioe Brought Money.
Brisbane.
I am proud to tell you that I have profited greatly
by following the advice contained in my Horoscope.
I had about given up taking a journey, but when you
advised it so strongly, I reconsidered and took the
journey, passed my examinations, and am now in a
position to earn from £2O to £25 per month. I would
not take £lO for the advice you gave me.
I believe every young person starting in life should
by all means secure a Horoscope from you. Nothing
I ever received has given me such power to control
conditions and circumstances as your Horoscope.
Wishing you every success, I am, yours truly,
B. H. RANDALL.
THE accuracy of recent predictions made by
this eminent Astrologer has caused many
of his friends to believe that he possesses
a supernatural power, but he modestly asserts
that his predictions are due alone to a scien-
tific understanding of natural laws. The many
thankful letters Prof. De Astro has received
from people who have benefited by his advice
furnish ample proof that he is sincere in his
work and has a kindly feeling towards
humanity.
Readers of The Bulletin can obtain a
Reading by addressing a letter to Prof. De
Astro, 366 Bourke Street, Melbourne. Simply
say you wish a Reading of your Life, stating
your birth date, sex, and whether married
or single, enclosing Is. Stamps or Postal Note
to pay postage etc., and the Reading will
be promptly sent.

“The Grunyah’s ” little tale
The continual growling of “ Pro Bono Publico,” “Indig-
nant Taxpayer,” etc., had, until recently, given me the
impression that the Commonwealth Government Depart-
ments were the holders of the prize in the Red Tape and
Sealing Wax Section, but I now think that the following
should about annex the trophy.
Hear my lament !
After months of worry, and after struggling through
various Acts and Regulations of the United States Patent
Office, J thought my papers were, at last, correct for the
filing oi a patent, and consequently heaved a sigh of relief.
Sent papers along together with the $l5 filing fee, also
a working model of the invention for the flotation of a
company in Doilardom. By last mail received, in reply,
a letter headed like a writ for had debt, signed by a man
whose name looks like the Pilliga scrub and sealed with the
seal of the U.S. Congress. In this formidable document
the Office politely informed me. that my application for a
patent could not under any circumstances be recorded,
filed or considered, for the reason that on one sheet of the
drawings—there were 18 in all—one of my witnesses had
omitted to sign. The writ went on to say that the man
with the jungle of an autograph had paid my $l5 into
revenue pending further action, and that he would return
the culprit drawing for completion, on receipt of 15 cents
to cover postage.
Thus for the missing of a signature, Doilardom collared
my $l5, and I lost —on account of the publication of my
invention per medium of the working models forwarded—
all my patent rights.
If any of The Bulletin’s scribes can point out a worse
case of Red Tapeism in any Commonwealth Department,
then that Department should be “named’’ and handed
the prize, same being one tin of insecticide.
Extract from a review of a recent work,
“Baby Toilers,” showing the lot of the worker in
Freetrade and landlord-ruled England :
It is a striking contrast with which Mrs. Mackirdy:
(“ Olive Christian Malvery ”) opens her book. First she
pictures a Confirmation scene in a Roman Catholic
church —the deep-toned organ, the sun streaming
through richly-colored windows, the scent of the incense,
the bishop and priests in rich robes, the young girls in
white, crowned with dainty chaplets of white and silver
flowers. And then, the room in which those wreaths
were made by other girls :
“One child was seven, and the other ten... .Once I went
in when the small girl was ill. You could see her fore-
head throb ! She lav on an unmade bed, with a few rags
over her, and though the little mouth was hot and
parched, neither the mother nor the sister had the time
to leave their table of toil and fetch the mite a drink of
water. The water was in the yard, down some steps and
up again, and it might have taken, perhaps, seven or eight
minutes to fetch it into the house The motherland
sister had been at work since about four o’clock .that
morning—they were a hand short—and they worked with
scarcely a break until ten or eleven at night. They were
making Confirmation wreaths, and the price paid was
Is. Sd. per dozen for the finished wreaths. Each flower
is made separately, and every wreath requires something
like a gross of small white flowers.. . . Twopence an hour
is a wage which can be earned by expert workers....
Out of this money the worker has* to provide the gum
and paste, and, of course, the gas with which she warms
her instruments.”

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

EIGHT YEARS SUFFERING
A PERMANENT CURE.
THE CASE OF MRS. A. WEAVER.
BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.
No one can tell the self-denying work which
good men have done in the early days of this
country, neither can we exaggerate the nobleness
of spirit which animates present-day people in a
desire to ameliorate distress as much as lies in their
power. Therefore, the public should be informed
of some of these efforts, one of which was made by
Mrs. Alice Weaver, of No. 7 Louis Street, Redfern,
who replied to the reporter by Saying :
“ About this time last year I was living in
Telopea Street, and it was just before I left there
for this place that I was fortunate in casting aside
a complaint that had worried me for no less than
eight years. What a fearfulTife of suffering mine
had been all that time, to be sure, and to think that
nothing in the world could benefit me made my
spirits droop lower than they will ever be again,
I hope.”
“What made you think that nothing could
benefit you ? ” queried the writer.
“I arrived at that conclusion because nothing
had done me good so far, and I believe I tried
every medicine going. At the time my second
child was born I was under a doctor, who did
everything he could for me, but still my health re-
mained as bad as it could bes. Indeed, at that
period I was nearly dead with indigestion, al-
though I dieted myself and did all that could be
done to keep my torments in check. It was of no
avail, though, for when I ate anything I could
hardly breathe owing to a feeling which came like
a lump in my chest, from which place a sharp pain
went right through to the centre of my shoulders.
I then remained in the utmost agony until the food
was vomited up again, after which I felt so ex-
hausted that I could not think of doing a thing in
the house until I had had a good rest. It would
surpriseyouifyou only knew how poor my appetite
was. You would wonder how anyone could keep
alive with it, and as for my head, oh, my sufferings
with it were awful. The most piercing pains at
onetime, and the most depressing achesat another.
Really, I think it was a marvel how I kept my
senses with the agony I endured in the head ; and
it’s strange that I did not meet .with many a nasty
accident through the giddiness,which used to over-
take me. I was a misery to myself and everybody
around me, and to tell you the honest truth I never
thought I would live through it all. My eyes used
to feel heavy enough to drop out of their sockets,
and I was affected in the mouth by such a bitter
taste that it made me feel sick of a morning, after
putting in the usual restless nights that had done
much towards making a wreck of me. Even in the
daytime I was alwaysimagining something terrible
was going to happen, and these were just the very
things that Clements Tonic, which I commenced to
take, showed itself to be the master of ; for it sent
such a vigor through my frame, when I had been
taking it a couple of weeks, that I could not help
feeling joyful in the belief that at last my health
had taken a beneficial turn. I got more Clements
Tonic, and as each bottle was finished, so did my
prospects improve, for I commenced to take my
meals with a degree of heartiness whi h astonished
my people as much as it pleased them ; and to my
own satisfaction anddelight theformer discomforts
attending my meals were quickly out of existence.
Oh, dear, what a changed woman I am now to then.
Mv limbs do not ache from weakness like they
formerly did, and I can go about my work as
cheerfully and as actively as any housewife in the
country. Ido not spend any restless nights now;
never dream during my sleep, and the pains that
used to abound have long since been driven away
from every part of my body. Well, Clements
Tonic made me the healthy woman I am, and for
the sake of other sufferers I trust you’ll publish
this interview in any way you think fit-”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Alice Weaver, of Yo. 7 Louis Street, Redfern, in the
State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, and that it contains, and is
a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by
Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission to
publish in any way my statements, which I give voluntary,
without receiving- any payment, and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true,
and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Signed) ALICE WEAVER.
Subscribed and declared at Redfern, this sixth day of
September, one thousand nine hundred and four, before
me, L. ISREARLEY. J.P

Rheumatism.
The Dr. McLaughlin Co.
No pen or tongue can describe my sufferings
before I began to use one of Dr. McLaughlin’s
Belts. I could not get into bed, either dress or
undress myself, and often had to sit on a chair on

seat I had to draw myself up gradually; my feet
resembled two roly-polys, and I was in mortal
agony. Scores of people never expected to see me
able to walk again without assistance. lam just a
living miracle. A fortnight yesterday I went a
railway journey alone, and returned yesterday
alone, took a cab from the station home and got in
and out of the cab without assistance. I sometimes
think; can it be true? I’ve suffered so much —a
wonder to myself. I have not worn the Belt since
last June. I can now do all domestic duties,
washing and ironing included, make my own
clothing and thank God I rejoice to be able to say
I went out and did some shopping last night, and
though my daughter was with me I never once took
her arm for support. The first time for six long
weary years. Oh! how I wish I could tell to all the
world what Dr. McLaughlin’s Belt has done for me.
I would like to tell every little detail, but it would
require a short-hand writer to take it all I
I am so glad I can testify to sufferers the wonders
of the Belt. Mrs. S. G. CORY,

The Legion of the Lost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ON Wings of Hope uplifted
From youth, and many dreams—
By strange winds drawn and
drifted,
By divers roads and streams —
We come from all ways hither,
By tide and tempest tossed,
And journey, none knows whither—
The Legion of the Lost!
The highways whence we started
Are hidden in the past.
For long since have we parted
With ties of kin and caste.
We drift away, we others,
From holding, home and wife,
And leave our sober brothers
To till their plots of life.
We are the stones, that, rolling,
Must count the years a loss,
And take at last the doling
Of other people’s moss;
Yet we, who, in all weather,
Have known the bush so well—
We of the wild bird’s feather
May never captive dwell!
We cannot do but wander
From Autumn round to Spring,
For ever Further Yonder
Our thoughts are on the wing.
We build and garner never;
The deserts we have crossed
Still urge us forward ever—
The Legion of the Lost.
For each was born a rover,
Nor can he thwart his fate,
And when his days are over
He’ll enter through the gate
As naked, at his passing,
As to the world he came—
But those who worked amassing
Must even do the same !
Though, in some hidden pocket,
I’ve known a few that wear
A letter or a locket,
A little wisp of hair,
Their dear possessions only,
Held fast in sordid days
To cheer them, being lonely,
<on those forgotten ways.
We drift abroad and scatter;
Our graves are none knows where;
The men who do not matter—
The men who do not care ;
On shores forgotten landed,
By tide and tempest tossed,
Afloat, or sunk, or stranded,
The Legion of the Lost!
Vic.
C. G. A. COLLES.
In The Small Hours.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
small, red-faced cabman pulled up, and
1 opening the window of the four-wheeler,
shook his fist in Wilson’s face.
“Look ’ere,” he hissed, “don’ you tork
t' me ! ’'
Wilson— who is six-feet-three and athletic—
Winked inanely at him, and asked “ Why not,
'lie chap?”
Because,” said the enraged cabman, “ I won’
ffianky well start it ! ”
Wilson took out his watch and yawned.
“Cabbie,” he said, “I’d be’stremely’bliged t’
yer if you'd start th’ horse.”
“Well, I won't start th’ horse ! ”
“ W’y not! "
Because I ’aven’t bin paid ! ”
Wilson is always polite and calm and cool. In
the very early hours these vices are accentuated.
He started to review their transactions of the
previous three hours.
“ Look here, cabbie, I’ve told you’bout five
hundred times already that I’m short of money,
haven't I?”
“Yes, but”
An Ive also told you I haven’t remotest
chance gettin’ any more this time night, didn't I?”
“Yes, you did ! But ”
“ Well, what d’ye ’spect me t’ do?"
Get out ! ” yelled the cabman. ‘ ‘ Get out an’
walk ! ”
Wilson smiled at him. “My dear fren’,” he
said, ‘ ‘ I don’t think you’re an intell’gent cabman
at all! I don’ know how you ever came to be a
cabman ! How’d it happen ? ”
The diiver nearly burst. “ I’m an hones’
man, he shouted, “ an’ I pay my way ! "
“ Qui’ ri’ ! ” said Wilson approvingly. “ Tha’s
proper ideal f’r working classes. How many
children have y’ got ? ”
“ What’s that got to do with it ? ”
“ I beg your pardon ? ”
“ What’s that got t’ do with it ?”
“ Do with what, my man ? ”
“You ridin’ fr nothin’ in my cab ! ”
Wilson tapped him on the shoulder. “ Look
here,” he said, “ I suppose I’ve met, durin’ last
thirty years, at least two thousan’ cabmen, an’ not
one of ’em talked as much as you do ! I wouldn’t
nun’ it so much if you kept th’ horse going while
you converse; but you don't. Ev’ry now an’
then you stop th’ poor beast an’ let it shiver while
you talk rot. You’ll pardon me saying it, ole
man, but you’re a frightful gasbag—frightful! ”
Once again the cabman shook his fist in Wil-
son s face.
“ What do you think I am ? ” he yelled.
“ I think you’re ’stremely ’fensive.”
“ W’y don’t y’ pay ? ”
“ Because I can't!"
“ Well, get out an’ walk l ”
“ I won't / ”
The small cabman measured Wilson with his
gnashed his teeth, and hissed 1 ‘ I'll fix you
up, my friend ! I'll take you somewhere ! ”
“ I wish t’ Heaven you would, ole man ! ”
“ Yes,” snarled the driver, “ I will! ”
“ I’m ’stremely ’bliged t’ you ! ”
The cab jolted on, and Wilson fell asleep in
the corner.
When he awoke, he found the cab had stopped,
and the cabman, accompanied by a policeman,
standing at the door.
“ There he is ! ” the driver was saying.
“ Good-night, sir,” said the constable.
“ How d’ye do?” said Wilson, gravely extend-
ing his hand.
“ Cabman tells me you won’t pay him, sir.”
“ Wrong,” said Wilson, “ I didn’t tell him
anything of th’ kind ! I said I couldn't pay ! ”
“ That won’t dp, sir ! " ,
Wilson agreed with him. Wilson admitted it
was an unsatisfactory state of affairs. He grew
confidential, and related many similar instances—
curiously involved stories of long-gone mornings
and ancient cabs—mysterious detached fragments
of adventure rummaged out of the recesses of a
fogged memory, and given to the constable for
nothing in the cold dawn. It seemed as if he—■
Wilson—had spent the best part of his life adrift
in cabs manned by hostile cabmen.
And, all the time, the driver stood by repeat-
ing a sort of awful litany made up of six ejacula-
tions :
“ Make ’im pay ! ”
“Well, w’y d’yer ride in kebs?”
“ I ain’t goin’t’ stan’ ’ere all night."
“ Well, I’ll summons yer and charnce it! ”
“ Th’ likes o’ you oughter walk ! ”
“ Don’ you talk t’ me!"
Then again, “ Make ’im pay ! ” and so on.
The constable, at first, tried to stem the flow
of Wilson’s recollections. Then he made several
spasmodic attempts to interrupt him. But Wilson
was not to be interrupted. He waved the
matter of payment aside as a trivial thing of no
interest, and continued his sermon on cabs until
the driver climbed back to his seat and, thrusting
the butt-end of his whip through the window,
poked him gently in the ribs.
Wilson, with his arms outspread in an eloquent
gesture, stopped in the middle of a sentence, and,
turning slowly round, peered into the dark
interior of the cab.
“ Wha’s that ! ”he cried indignantly : “ wha’s
that ! ”
“Me?” murmured the cabman, in a weak
voice.
“ What are y’ ! ”
“ I’m th’ driver ! ”
“Th’ what?"
“ Th’ driver ! "
“ Where are y’ ? ”
“ I’m 'ere ! ”
Wilson groped in the gloom with both arms
and found nothing. Then he turned to the
choking constable and remarked angrily, “ There
isn’t anything! Th’ blessed bathing-box is
haunted ! ”
The constable perceived that the cabman had
drifted entirely out of Wilson’s recollection. He
gently, but firmly, introduced the subject, and
suggested that the man should be dismissed.
“ Cert’nly ! ” said Wilson, as he got out.
“ It’s all he deserves —d’smiss him ’mediately ! ”
The driver rushed round fuming, and caught
Wilson’s arm.
“ ’Ere,” he said, “ ’ow much longer am I coin’
t’ wait ? ”
Wilson wheeled round, and a gleam of recol-
lection stole into his eye. “ Hullo ! you’re th’
cabbie, aren’t y’ ? ”
“Yes, I'm the cabman ! ”
“ Yes,” said Wilson, serenely. “/remember
y’ ! You’re the cabbie that’s been gassin’ all
ni’ ! ”
“ Yes, an’ I’ll keep on gassin’ till I get my
money ! ”
The constable intervened. “ Pay him off,” he
advised. “ Pay him off and get rid of him.”
Wilson seemed struck with the idea. He
patted the policeman, and said “ That’s ri’ !
That’s jus’ what ’ll do ! I’ll pay him off! ”
“ That’s right!”
“ Yesh, I’ll pay him off an’ ” —with a sweep of
the arm—“ an’ I'll get rid of him ! ”
“ That's right! ”
“ Y’ know I want t’ get rid of him ! ”
“Yes.”
Wilson turned to the man and addressed him
sternly. “ Look here, my fren’, I’m goin’t’ pay
you off!"
“Yes, fr Gord’s sake pay me orf ! ”
“ An’—an’ I’m goin' f get rid of y' /"
“ Orright— orright! "
“ How much do I owe yer ?”
“ Call it ten shillin’s !”
Wilson fumbled in his vest pocket and drew
out a coin, which he closely inspected, and then
handed to the cabman.
The cabman looked at it in the light of the
lamp, and whined, “What’s th’ good o’ this?"
Wilson said angrily, “ It’s a sov’r’n, isn’t it ? ”
“ Yes, but I ain’t got no change ! ”
“ Well, who asked y’ f r charge ? I didn’t ask
y’ f r change ! ”
The driver mumbled something, and hurriedly
clambered up on the box seat, as if he feared
Wilson might alter his mind. Then he gathered
up the reins, and murmured apologetically:
“ Goo’ ni’, sir ! ”
Wilson, who was explaining something to the
constable, took no notice.
“ Goo’ nil, sir ! ”
Wilson manipulated an imaginary cue, and
showed the constable how he made the shot.
The driver leant out and stirred him gratefully
with the whip. “ Goo’ni’, sir—an’—an’ thank
y’, sir ! ”
Wilson begged the constable to excuse him,
and walked unsteadily to the cab. “ Look here,
my fren’,” he said, “ I’ll give you one more chance
—jus’ one more ! If y’ don’ shut up an’ clear out
now, I’ll pull y’r rotten ole shay t’ pieces, an’
throw th’ horse' at yer ! D’ye un’stan’ me ! ”
The cabman, without another word, swung
round on the seat and shook out the reins. • “Git
up ! Dammit ! git up ! ”
The horse looked round reproachfully and
started off.
Wilson stood on the kerb and told the con-
stable : “ Fearful gasbag, that man, I ’sure you !
Talked to me whole damn night ! ”
