Down Where the Lachlan
Flows.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I MET her first at a country dance.
Her eyes crossed mine in a backward glance
That fired my soul to a torrid love
Like the hot, hot night in the sky above.
I was fresh from town. I had scorned the sex,
And pitied the sickly fools who vex
Their hearts with a woman’s love and hate,
And flaunt their lives in the face of Fate.
But—to-night my heart in its dreaming goes
To Wunya, down where the Lachlan flows.
She was waltzing there with a moon-eyed clod
Whose face was turned to its natal sod
In the bestial stare of the under-born
And she left me there with a heart forlorn.
For the game was hers, and she played it fine ;
But I kissed her lips, and the game was mine.
I pressed her close in the throb.bing dance,
And our souls dissolved in a daring glance.
We wandered out where the night-mists rose
’Round Wunya, down where the Lachlan flows.
I bared my heart and I vowed my love;
And the passioned breath of the night above
Stooped low, and whispered it back to me
Thro’ the maze of a ghostly grey-box tree.
From along the creek came the mopoke’s cry,
Like the wail of a wand’ring soul gone by.
While, low in the west, ’mid a blood-red light,
M> stpx sank down to the depths of night.
Ani I shuddered there, where the death-weed
grows
At Wunya, down where the Lachlan flows.
Our life was a wondrous hymn of praise ;
Our loving the wine of drunken days.
I flung my soul to her soft white hands.
And wrapt my heart in a lover’s bands.
I dragged myself in the mire of earth,
And played the fool for her silly mirth.
For she was cruel as women are—
I called her my queen and my guiding star.
And I pray that her paltry conscience knows
Of the life she damned where the Lachlan flows.
She was fair as false, and as false as fair,
For her face was lit with the beauty rare
Of olden days when the world was young,
And saint-faced maidens their favors flung
To the gallant knights as they rode to fight
For the joy of war, and the 1 ve of right.
She held my soul in her hands, and said
That she’d play me true —and our hearts were wed.
But—lia-ho ! —lie-lie !—’tis my heart now goes
To the dancing shed where the Lachlan flows.
The fire is low, and the night is cold,
And the world is stale, and I’m growing old.
So I end my dreaming, and take this curl
That I stole from the head of that lovely girl;
And her scented notes, and her withered flowers
With mem’ries sweet of those wicked hours;
And thrust them deep in the dying fire,
And make of them all a fun’ral pyre.
For she married the clod and a life of prose,
In Wunya, down where the Lachlan flows.
N.S.W.
WILLIAM H. ELSUM.
Eucalyptus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TWO persons—a man and a girl—sat arguing
in low tones across a supper-table in a
restaurant. A weary waitress, dark and
pouchy under the eyes, stood waiting to pounce
on them with a ticket when they showed signs of
moving. She wasn’t in the least curious about
their affairs, though this was the fourth evening
in succession that they had talked earnestly over
the same table in precisely the same way. The
man —Arthur Linton—did not seem to the
waitress particularly interesting. The girl—
Dorothy Bell —was not one of those costly,
mysterious creatures whose jewellery roused the
waitress’s envy so much that she hated their
laughter. Dorothy had worn the same hat each
evening, and the hat was an old one. There was
nothing dramatic about the way in which the two
behaved. They held the same suppressed, urgent
conversation; ended it by shrugging their
shoulders ; and went away talking lightly and
casually.
In fact, the waitress was right in taking little
interest in them, for the theme of their talk was
in no way original. Linton, living more or less
comfortably on the third floor of a huge building,
which paid bigger insurance premiums because it
offered “residential chambers” as well as
“ business offices,' 1 wanted to persuade Dorothy
that this would be a happy sort of existence for her
too. Dorothy, who worked hard for her living all
day long, paid for her lodgings with an unpleasant
aunt who owned a large, aniemic family of boys
and girls. Probably Dorothy was in love with
Linton—at any rate, she was discontented enough to
be sure that she was. She resisted his sugges-
tions ; partly because, like all girls, she had been
brought up to believe that to retain possession of
herself was virtue, and partly from the instinct
which makes women hesitate at accepting any
proposal, for fear that they may somehow be
missing a better bargain. Yet if the waitress had
listened to their conversation, she would have
given Linton a prophecy of success. For Dorothy
came back every evening to be argued with.
There was a special reason for Dorothy’s dis-
content just now. For a year the unpleasant aunt
had allowed her a room to herself. In that
sanctuary she could stand before a long mirror
and appraise the value of all her beauty. But
now her aunt had begun to take other lodgers,
and she was forced to share a room with
Kathleen, her cousin. Kathleen, bony and ugly-
skinned, always slipped furtively from her clothing
to her night-gown, and looked with scared
eyes at Dorothy’s reckless way of getting ready
for bed. Moreover, Kathleen had a post-nasal
growth; she slept with her mouth open, and
snored. But this was not the worst.
One of the horrors of Dorothy’s childhood had
been eucalyptus oil. The unpleasant aunt had
anointed her with it, had poured it into her
stomach on every possible occasion. The mere
thought of its vapor sickened her. Kathleen, a
dutiful daughter, clung to the eucalyptus tradi-
tion ; and Dorothy, after crawling into bed with
her cousin, would lie sick and suffocating.
Arthur Linton, secretly dismissed at the street
corner for fear of the unpleasant aunt, had on this
fourth night gone home. Dorothy tramped
straight to her bedroom. On the chair before the
long mirror Kathleen was sitting, clutching her
nightgown round her thin breast even in the soli-
tary room, and with oily forefingers rubbing,
rubbing at her neck. Dorothy fell on the bed
and began to laugh.
“ What is the matter ? I wish I could under-
stand you,” Kathleen said, talking more through
her nose than ever.
The other girl laughed more and more. She
stopped, and stood up. “ This room stinks,” she
said; “I’m going out —to post a letter.” She
seized her cousin, who smelt like a thousand gum
leaves, and shook her.
“ Damn you, Kathleen,” she said viciously, and
ran out of the room. Kathleen, being foolish, sat
in front of the long mirror, and cried weakly.
She fell asleep waiting for Dorothy to come back.
* * « *
Nothing but the thought of a railway time-table
was worrying Linton as he sat in his shirt sleeves,
gently rubbing at a tan boot with a piece of
flannel. A soft knock came at his door.
“ You sweet little girl, what made you come
after all ?” he asked her. Dorothy laughed again.
In a month or so it would be time enough to tell
him about the eucalyptus.
“ Wash your hands,” she said. “ I don’t like
the smell of that boot-polish.”
• * * *
“ My wife has come to live with me,” Linton
shouted next day into the deaf ears of old Alt-
mayer, the caretaker. Altmayer nodded, and
shrugged his shoulders. No doubt Mrs. Alt-
mayer would hint other things in their confidential
talks, but both he and she knew exactly how far
they were responsible for the morals of the building.
Gentlemen visited by stray ladies, or ladies visited
by stray gentlemen —Altmayer could deal with
them without mercy. But if Mr. Linton chose
to be married, and to want an extra sitting-room,
what then ? He smiled benignantly, and offered
congratulations. It was not his place to tell the
landlords that the new wife came at midnight,
without a trousseau.
So Linton lived for a fortnight, almost always
in his rooms, and Dorothy decided that she had
not over-estimated her power of holding a man.
At the end of a fortnight Linton got drunk. He
got drunk with the fierce enthusiasm of a man
who hasn’t intended to do it, but having done it,
thinks he had better do it thoroughly. He was
still exalted by his triumph in love. So being
drunk, he was arrogant and aggressive. He
went home with many bruises, for you cannot
assume possession of a disreputable street from
midnight till 3 a.m. without finding violent
trouble. As he walked softly into his room he
realised that next day his bruises would be very
sore.
Dorothy, who had fallen asleep while still
uncertain whether to leel murderous or to cry,
was suddenly beset by horrible dreams. She was
again in bed with Kathleen, who was strangling
her with bony fingers. Her last convulsion was
just beginning. With a choked cry she sat up,
wide awake. In front of a long wardrobe mirror
Linton stood rubbing, rubbing, rubbing with
something that he poured on his fingers from a
hideous bottle.
For ten minutes Linton, dull and hazy-headed,
was in the grip of a cyclone. A fury in a white
nightgown attacked him with a soaking sponge.
He was mauled, splashed, scrubbed, rubbed and
dried. The white nightgown dragged a drawer
to the floor, and assaulted him with bottles of
sharp perfume. He was disinfected with eau-de-
Cologne, and pushed heavily into bed.
A window flew up ; something glittered in the
air, and smashed on the roadway. A cat put its
nose to the new object, and stepped back daintily,
disgusted by the fumes. Linton lay asleep, while
Dorothy leaned over him, with nausea and amuse-
ment fighting for mastery in her face. Finally
she slipped down to his side. His mouth was open,
and he snored.
“ Good-night, Kathleen,” she murmured,
putting her face against his.
PRIAM.
The Failure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I TAKE my mothers letter to the park every
day, and think, and think. She says how
much they miss me, how much they want
me back, and they talk of the green plains, and the
sweet old garden, and the larks along the creek.
Down at the end are some words about being
resigned, and the Hand of God in everything.
Resignation ! And I dreamed of Fame. Oh my
voice ! My lost voice ! And my people twelve
thousand miles away. I hear the cow-bells down
the creek, “ tinkle, tonkle,” and the lark in the
morning, and the curlews at night. I shut my
eyes and see the grey plains, the old homestead by
the muddy-watered creek, and under the olean-
ders Jim —and me.
That was before I dreamed of being famous;
but Jim is married now to a girl with money, and
Nell told me once, in awed and underlined sen-
tences, that she always wore silk stockings.
My shoes cost four-and-eleven, and my hat is a
Leghorn in November. Shall I go back, a
failure ?
The old home was too far back and times were
too hard for me to be decently educated. If I
stayed here I could be a housemaid or a shopgirl.
Perhaps in time I might marry a grocer or an
undertaker. . . .
And the future my voice promised —it is gone,
and nothing remains but going back.
Shall Igo ? Back to the old home and the old
people, back to Nell and the boys, back near Jim
• —and Jim’s wife.
My shoes cost four-and-eleven, and her stock-
ings—what a fool I am !
Oh, my Voice ! Oh, Jim !
I wonder !
